THE RED PAGE.
Of all the prophets that are smart,
There’s none like pretty Bowie ;
Be is a master of the art
Of proftts-I, I vow—he /
. The ancient prophet stuck to prayer ;
The modern is a fly’un.
He lets the heathen rage and swear
And sticks to deeds in Zion.
Of all the names in Holy Writ
I love best Adonijah,
For that’s a name that rhymes to fit
The name of sweet Elijah :
He has the grace, he has the face—
Of Scotland’s tribe the Lion,
And Zion is his dwelling-place :
Come, let us go to Zion !
When I get on the Public Works
Committee, I’ll have money ;
I'U hoard it up, with all my perks,
And give it to my honey ;
But when to Heaven he is called
His mantle I will try on,
And if you say l 'rn getting bald
Look out for bears in Zion !
IT was It. H. Stoddard, the American verse-
writer deceased last year, who wrote in a
haunting stanza that
There are gains for all our losses.
There are balms for all our pain ;
But when youth, the dream, departs.
It takes something from our hearts,
And it never comes again.
Last week found us gravely doubting whether
that was the matter with Moore. This week cer-
titude has come by the English mail. Much, so
much, has been taken from Moore’s heart. When
the heart was young he delighted, how he
delighted in Mallarme’s celebrated barrel-organ—
that decadent instrument which chanta languis-
samment et melancoliquement in the avenue of
poplars (so dejected even in spring since Maria
went away) [and if you call her—O horror !
“ Marai'a ” you will not merely adulterate “the
concrete essence, the osmazome of literature, the
essential oil of art,” but you will d n
everlastingly your pretensions to Taste in
the minds of the “ten superior persons scat-
tered through the uniyerse ”]. When the heart
was young, he translated, how he mistranslated
Mallarme’s “ miniature marvels of diction.” Yea,
he listened to the prosing of the organ (or versing,
rather, since “There is really no such thing as
prose,” said Mallarme gently, firmly: “every-
thing is verse”— “though,” remarks a wicked
commentator, “when you read the poetry of
Mallarme’s disciples, you feel inclined to believe
exactly the opposite”), and he asked exquisitely,
“ Why do its flourishes go to my soul, and make
me weep like a romantic ballad? I listen,
imbibing it slowly, and I do not throw a penny
out of the window for fear of moving from my
place and seeing that the instrument is not
singing itself.
But now the heart is old—ade, ade, the heart is
old, and
On 28th December two organ-grinders, hailing from the
clime from which such musicians usually come, were
brought before a Dublin magistrate, charged with
creating a nuisance (!) by playing in front of the house of
Mr. George Moore, the novelist (!!) and refusing to go
awav (!! !)
Mr. Moore narrated how the twain placed an organ in
front of his windows while he was engaged in his writing.
He had already warned one of them.
Asked by an interpreter why htf’did not go away, this
man said he “only wanted to finish the tune” [poor
artist-fellow with a feeling of his business].
The magistrate : You must stop this ; and in the mean-
time you must- find bail in 5 1. to he of good behaviour
and to desist from what seems to have been a deliberate
persecution.
Ai for the plainte d’automne and the orgue de
Barbarie ! at for Maria ! ai for Moore ! and ai, ai,
ai, ai for Mallarme ! But dream of that heroic
ghost attendant, 'searching vainly for the appro-
priate and faded word—him who has “ trop oru
aux mots ” ! But hear him at last falsifying his
art, verifying his emotion, with le mot trop cru...
de Cambronne !
J*
Several things are suggested—whether Moore’s
admiration of prose about a barrel-organ logically
implies admiration of a barrel-organ ; what is the
aesthetic use of “d n”; what should be
answered to distant Bill, turning over to ask
Jim “Who is this Mal-something that this
Bed Page bloke is gassing about?” Inviting
topics for a more spacious day !

The Athenaeum reviews Such is Life, by “ Tom
Collins,” in a liberal space and spirit. _ It is now
an open secret that “Tom Collins” is the pen-
name of Jos. Eurphy, an Australian of Irish
descent, born at Yering, on the Upper Yarra, in
1843. So that his work falls under The Athenaum’s
alternative 6f “ the book of a man past maturity,
with consequent regrets. His own account of
himself says that he “ has maintained a stirring
and unprofitable career in Central and Northern
Victoria, and in Baverina. Has had experi-
ence of many occupations—chiefly of an
arduous and thankless character —and has
followed each industry through all its
ramifications and details. Has nevertheless
realised nothing more tangible ” —he forgets a
wife, two sons, and a daughter i —“than a broaden-
ing interest in the general welfare, and a narrow-
ing solicitude for the personal concerns of Number
One. Is especially impressed with the conviction
that the first public duty of every born or adop-
tive Australian is to bear a hand in building up
an independent and common-sense civilisation;
while sis first private, duty is to pursue Australian
virtues and vices, in contradistinction to those of
the Northern Hemisphere, And the yearning of
pseudo-Australians toward European usages and
institutions too rotten to bear oversea transference
is more trouble to him than all his financial
investments.”
This is in the large, irreverent manner that The
Athenaeum, (Englishly) objects to, and omits
details of a youth and manhood spent in farming
and as machine-artificer and as bul-
lock-driver, until Furphy settled as general
mechanio in an agricultural-implement works at
Shepparton, Vic.—to be called “our modest
townsman” by the local papers. The Athenaeum
speaks of bis book well and critically (Jan. 9).
Such is Life
is as essentially Australian in its every line as the
wattle, the wara’tah, or the Sydney Bulletin. To the
literary mind there is a special interest attaching to
fiction or other matter written in and of Australia.
There are not many English-speaking lands left from
which we may look for new developments in English
literature. Australia is one of the few, and very interest-
ing it is to watch her progress in letters...
Such is Life is a book of another sort, long, extraordi-
narily' long, close packed with reflection, realistic in
most of its grim detail, clever, undisciplined, pseudo-
philosophical, and genuinely bitter ; a really remarkable
book, and well worth the rather serious effort which is
required to peruse il from end to end..
After two columns *>f a commentary that some-
times can be endorsed, and always is reasonable
from an Englishman’s point of view, the writer
sums up that, “ In any case, the book as it stands
is purely Australian and exceedingly interest-
ing ”...

and before he could retire dreamed he was assailed

Scotch professors, like Scotch fleas, are noto-
rious for esprit de corps.

H. I. Hancock’s book on Japanese Physical
Training (Lond., Putnam’s; ss. net) can be
warmly commended to athletic young Aus-
tralians. The muscular illustrations arp uncom-
monly good and interesting. Incidentally, the
author traverses our meat deduction, in affirming
that despite the Mikado the Japanese still live on
rice and fish, and thrive on it to the point of
being “the strongest, most enduring, and hap-
piest people on earth.” But perish a thousand
Japanese rather than one deduction.
Japanese physical training is called jiu-jitsu,
which means literally “muscle-breaking.” Ifc
means practically much more, and includes diet
and breathing as well as exercise in methods of
attack and defenoe—the whole system being
directed to the attainment of perfect health.
One of its specialties is the use of what may be
called centres of nerve- and muscle-paralysis. For
example,
Take a point about midway between the elbow and the
shoulder, of some one else’s arm. Employ the grip in
such a way that the fingers dig into the muscles behind
the middle of the bone. The thumb’s tip should press
into the muscles over the front of the bone. Without in
any way relaxing the grip, both fingers and thumbs
should be vigorously pressed over the parallel lines
of muscles and nerves. Any experimenter can readily
find on his own arm the exact .locations of these muscles
and nerves, and a little practice with a friend will teach
him rapidly how to seize an antagonist’s arm and to
render that arm momentarily helpless.
Of course, “it is a principle of fin jitsu that a
weaker man should be able to attack a stronger
opponent, and to defeat him by aid of the latter’s
own greater strength.”
When you haveacquired the arm-pinch, you start
hardening the edgeof your hand—which is useful to
hit an opponent across the throat with. Then you
harden the solar plexus so that it will resist any
ordinary blow. Meanwhile, you have been
breathing deeply until deep breathing has become
a habit; you have used water galore internally
and externally ; and, stomach, heart, and lungs
being at their best, 'the muscles gain strength
daily. “ There is no difference whatever between
the physical training of one sex or of the
other. A Japanese woman, well versed in
jiu-jitsu, is able to encounter a burglar in
a dark room, and to hold him powerless until
help comes.” And the author gives full details
of the grips, the holds, the finches and the blows
which may be used to bring about this ideal
power. He takes the wide view, too —“Health
is not created by mere combat. Physical training
does not consist alone of the creation of swelling
muscles. Nor does the possession of a few easily-
learned tricks of attack make an athlete.” Alto-
gether, one thinks the intelligent reader of his
book is bound to profit by it.
It will be useful to count some early traces of
Japanese ambition. Later Jap diplomacy has
been suave and careful, biding its time.
If England were to make Japan her enemy, it would
mean a sweeping of her influence from the East... In
•one sense, England is China in Europe. What she
depends upon is no more than the bequests of her
ancestors and of historical glory. Even that historical
glory was not obtained by fighting with equal powers. ..
Our'navy will be increased after the present war, with
a view to cope with combined squadrons of European
powers as our object. .. We are ready to sacrifice our
country,for its own dignity, honour, and right, and we
do not fear Europe.—The Japanese paper Kokimino-
Tome, translated June 21, 1895.
Should Japanese rule dominate China, it will result in
a process of civilisation and education of China’s millions
and the adaptation of their latent strength for occupa-
tion as a producing nation in open competition with the
other indqstrial countries of the world. The question
arises, pregnant with import to the labouring classes,
How will this competition affect the manufacturing and
producing countries of Europe, America, and Australia?
—W. Griffiths in The Empires of the East, reprinted
from The S.A. Register, 1895.
Mr. Coote relates that he was travelling on one of the
railways in Japan with a friend, who had been for thirty
years residing in the country, when his attention was
attracted by a loud conversation going on between several
Japanese military officers, who were seated in ttlbsame
carriage. “ Would you like to know what those fellows
are talking about?” asked Mr. Coote’s companion.
“ They are referring to your country, Australia, they
say that Australia has great pastoral lands, great forests,
and soil that will grow coffee, tea; rice, and every other
tropical product. The Australians, they think, are act-
ing in a dog-in-the-manger fashion in not allowing other
people to settle on the land, and, by doing so, develop it.
They consider England a capital ally in case of war with
any other nation, but should anything arise of an un-
friendly nature between themselves and the English, the
best thing for their Government to do would be to at
once send down some of their warships, and capture a
good slice of Northern Australia.”— Aodley- Coote, inter-
viewed by The Brisbane Courier, 19 September, 1896.
The Napoleonic craze has reached Japan. Tsabonchi,
a leading Japanese novelist, has made him the hero of
one of his romances, and prints of the great Corsican
adorn the walls of almost every Japanese cottage. The
historical essay, by the way, is a form of literature in
much favour among the Japanese, monographs on
Bismarck and Caesar being only second in demand to
those on Napoleon. —The Spectator, London, 1897.
Japan is not onlj' building more ships than any other
power except England, but she is building better shipsm
English shipyards than England herself is constructing
for her own navy... It must... be assumed that Japan s
purpose is the general one of predominant sea-power in
the Orient-.—C. H. Cramp in The American Magazine,
October, 1898.
Colonel J. F. Maurice, an expert strategist, writing on
the results of the peace conditions of the China-Japan
war, says that if Japan obtains the control of affairs in
China she is certain to demand the free admission of
Chinese into Australia.—London cable, 1898.
Asiatics as we are, we have made our Empire a banner
State, and have written the word- ’‘Civilization” in
glowing hues on our standard. China, Korea, Japan, the
Indian States, all look to Japan as the modern represen-
tative of Asiatic power and national vitality. With every
other Asiatic land looking to Japan for aid, we firmly
believe that it is our beloved country’s exalted duty to
lend a helping hand to one and all; to act as the friend
and saviour of those once powerful States now cowering
under European menaces ; to raise the Asiatic standard
of civilization, and to prove to the world at large that the
Orient is every whit the compeer of the Occident. —Prince
Koxoye, president of the Japanese House of Peers, in the
Japanese magazine Toyd, translated by The Daily Tele-
graph (Sydney) correspondent, June 15,1901.

A distinguished Irishman is dead in W. E. H.
Lecky ; a distinguished Scotchman in Alexander
Bain ; a distinguished Englishman in Herbert
Spencer; a distinguished German in Theodor
Mommsen. Many of the illustrious are approach-
ing their end. By 1910 we (?) shall have ceased
formal lamentation for Ibsen and Tolstoy,
Bjomson and Swinburne. The nineteenth century
will have delivered its last message and retired
down the back-entry of Time. The twentieth,
with the fire of youth in its eyes, will be fling-
ing a new challenge to Oblivion. Already “8.1 i.”
has tuned his virgin lyre. These are the tunes :—;
The proof is complete. Britain is Heaven-born ;
and Joe Chamberlain is the walking-delegate of
the Almighty.

Alfred Austin—the Poet Laureate who em-
bodies the last word of English satire—is reduced
to parodying Tennyson’s “ Vastness ” for a New
Year piece. (Yet somebody remembers that it
was A. A. who declared thirty years ago that “ In
Memoriam will assuredly be handed over to the
dust as soon as a generation arises which has come
to its senses.”) “Moving Onward ” tells the world
through The Times that twice two.are four, and a
door must be either open or shut, and though the
great Encyclopaedia opportunity has fled the
wonderful Dictionary invitation is open, and flesh
is grass, and grass is hay, and Russia is creeping
up, and mastodons have become marine animals,
and Russia is marching 1 on. But (with a flourish
of patriotic trumpets)—
.. .British sentinels stand erect at the fortress gates of the
Aridthe British Flag is on every sea, with its splendid
symbol unfurled ;
And the Lord of Right still sits on His throne, still melds
His sceptre and rod.,
And the winds, and the waves, and the years move on,
doing the Will of God.
(Witness to the Will )
ALYRED AUSTIN.

(says Maurice Allard in La Raison) represent the
securities for the religious Paradise. As long as you take

use reason, and open the safe, there’s nothing'there. The
imaginary Crawfords and the mythical will complete the
parallel. Clearly they stand for the blessed saints and
all the miracles.

In Eng. Church Times an Anglican clergyman dis-
cusses the ingratitude shown to the Kaffirs who assisted
the British in the African war. No mark of Britain s
gratitude has been given to these valiant men. Shall
their heroism go unrewarded ?...“ Let us found a
South African bishopric ! ” “ Gods, what humour .
Comments G. W. Foote.
“ How,” asks The Saturday Review, “can English be
well spoken when some of the greatest ladies speak the
idiom of Massachusetts through their noses, and a large
proportion of the men cough the idiom of Frankfort
from their throats ?”
The O’Ruddy, commenced by Stephen Crane and
finished by Robert Barr, starts serially in The Idler.
America already has the book, and describes it as holding
little of Crane’s characteristic value—that little wet-
blanketed by Barr.
Prof. Lounsbury, in Jan. Harper’s, points out that in
sounded we use a word-form precisely similar in character
and origin to drownded. Drownded “ was employed in
the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries by reputable
writers. Had during those centuries the form been
generally adopted by authors of the highest grade...
every educated man at the present day would be saying
drownd and drownded for drown and drowned, and
withal be ignorant that he was using what was in its
origin a corruption of the worst kind.” Let us be humble
Critics, therefore.
Lynching is by no means a forsaken pastime in the
U.S.A. According to a writer in Jan. N. A.. Review the
annual average for hen years to 1902 was 143 cases ; and
in eight and a-half months of 1903 there were 76 cases.
Thrice, in 1903, they lynched the wrong man—and apolo-
gised to the corpse ?
G. A. Sala (or Sarcey, or Max Beerbobm —it is a father-
less anecdote), failing on one occasion to receive his first-
night ticket, called at the theatre-box office, and was
handed a voucher for stall K 9. Ah,” (said Walkley, or
Lemaitre, or W. Winter), “ I always suspected that criti-
cism was going to the dogs.”
Some of London’s city missionaries are scandalised at
th* maiden tribute to Stead’s modern Daily Paper.
Five hundred young girls, at ss. per week, deliverthe
papers ; and the missionaries scent moral danger. “We
like them best at fourteen years of age,” says W. T. Stead,
jun., quite innocently. “ Mr. Stead loves employing
women, and would not have a single boy to work for him
if it were possible,” adds his lady representative, quite
too innocently. It is natural to chuckle, however base.
“ We shall always think of him as Bedstead,” wrote The
Bulletin when The Bulletin was a wicked paper, long time
ago.
Recollections and Impressions of James McNeill
Whistler, by A. J. Eddy, is published in America. The
author is said to declare that Whistler’s purity of colour
was a reflection of his puritanical soul : ergo, he was
American to the palette and spinal marrow ! Printed, by
the way, that Whistler had no baptismal right to
“ McNeill ” : the whim took him to add it, and he added,
in the airy fashion of his declaration that he was born in
Petersburg, though really in Lowell.
A. G. Bradlev, in Canada in the Twentieth Century,
tells N.S.W. Birth-rate Commission that the province of
Quebec has recently re-enacted an old law giving 100
acres of land to every man with twelve children.
Marinoni, the famous French printer and inventor,
died recently in his 81st year. He was chief proprietor
of Be Petit Journal, the Parisian halfpenny daily which
claims 1,000,000 circulation. “ Work is the whole of life,”
he said. “To live surrounded by one’s family, to employ
one’s money usefully, to do good, to be agreeable to one’s
friends-that is the whole of pleasure. So I count my-
self happy.” , "h
J. L. G6r6me, the celebrated French painter and
sculptor, died on 10th January. The N.S.W. Gallery
contains a fine example of his work in Promenade de
Harem. Visitors will find it hanging next to the
Whistler, in the same court as the Rodin statue.
RECEIVED
Buddhism , No. 2 (International Buddhist Society,
Rangoon; 3s.—annual subscription posted, 10s. 6d.)
From an exceptional article by Ananda M., upon “Trans-
” —with the intellectual stimulus of Poe’s
Eureka—it will be necessary to make lengthy quotation.
This article and other notes lift the maga. from the ranks
of doctrinaire publications, and subscription is justified.
The Literary Sense, by E. Nesbit (Bond., Methuen ; 2s.
6 d.) Singly, these sketches, of literary self-consciousness
held fa ! nt interest; collected, they are rather tedious.
The point and attitude were well taken, and deserved
more forcible treatment in a longer story.
Two Sides of the Face , by A T. Quiller-Couch (Bond.,
Macmillan ; &. 6 d.) Agreeable, magazine-work that
scarcely deserved reprinting. Vis has gone from the
anima of A.T.Q.O. : remains Noughts and Crosses, and
there you have the whole of him at the best of him.
Sea-Wrack, by Frank T. Bullet) (Lond., Macmillan ;
2s. Gd.) This fine, rhetorical, religious, veracious writer
is another tolerable magazine-man whose books now can
be missed without being missed. A recent piece of his
fiction told how he was whaling on Christmas
Dav, and fired a harpoon from a gun (not
a " long-bow), and hit a whale in the belly.
The whale smashed the boat, died, and floated. The ship
had vanished, and Bullen caught the line from the fortu-
nate harpoon and clambered on the whale’s back. There
he sat till rescued, drinking rain-water collected m a
hollow of the whale, and living on whale-beef dug out
with his sheath-knife. But he did not fear, for Cod was
with him. “Then came a blessed vision. Out of a
lowering squall the ship emerged haloed by the setting
sun; beautiful beyond all knowledge, a mighty angel of
deliverance, and we were saved.”_ It is a matter for
regret that The Spectator did not print this yarn.
The Growth of the British Empire, by M. B.
Synge (Bond., Blackwood ; 2.5.). Fifth and concluding
volume of a previously-noticed series of school-hooks,
designed to relate simply crucial episodes of world-history
up to 1900. The plan has merit; but the compilers
execution of the plan, though possibly as effective as
could bs expected from a single individual, is not entirely
satisfactory and his comments are frequently inept. In
Australia, we have reached a point at which no school-
books planned and executed beyond Australia can be
entirely satisfactory. Universal school-history must be
re-written with regard to the Australian outlook. One
or two of the illustrations in this little volume are well-
handled : but, as before, there are too few illustrations.
The picture of the death of Burke is meaningless or
ridiculous.
His Master Purpose, by Harold Bindloss (Lond.,
Bong ; 2s. 6 d.) Romance of love and engineering in
British Columbia. A good piece of realistic workman-
ship, with fresh scenery and some dramatic episodes.
Worth reading for relaxation.
The Bdion Sinister, by George Gilbert (Lond.,
Long ; 2s. 6 d.) Claims, in the guise of historical novel,
to give a more faithful picture of Eng. Crown-pretender
Monmouth ; but fails to amuse.
A.G.S.

Dear Sirs—There is nothing gives me greater pleasure than showing or
speaking of the great benefit I have received from wearing one of the Dr. McLaughlin Electric Belts.
For seventeen years I was a cripple, almost a burden to my people, and a perfect misery to myself, in
fact, I was what you might call a useless man. I tried a good many doctors, but got no benefit from
their treatment; lotions and drugs were the order of the day. Rub this into you three or four times
a day ; take one tablespoonful of the medicine every three hours, etc., were the principal pictures in
my house. I at last became so weak and sore that I often longed to die. I begged hard of one
doctor to apply Electricity to my body, but he would not, for he said I could not stand it, as I was too
weak, one shock, he said, would put me in eternity. I then told him that if he could not get inside
and rub it out, I should not have any more lotions rubbed in, neither did I. My boys subscribed £5
for me and got me to Sydney, where I soon got the Belt. That was on the 9th day of last June
twelve months, and from that day to this I have not taken one dose of medicine, neither have I had
one drop of lotion rubbed into me. I wore the Belt pretty regular for from five to seven hours each
day for about three months. My neighbors, like myself were more than surprised at the great good
I was receiving frcsa the use of the Belt. For near y 17 years I could not stoop to lace my boots
neither could I put my socks on. I then weighed only seven stone, to-day I weigh about ten stone!
I have not once had an occasion, during the last 12 months, to ask any person to lace my boots or put
my socks on. lam 54 years of age, and lama b( tter man to day than I was when only 40 years of
age. lam always ready for my meals, and I would think nothing of a walk of 30 miles any day I
thank God my footsteps were directed to the Dr. McLaughlin Electric Institute, for I am positively a
new man altogether. Before I got the Bek I was like a clock that had run down, but after I had worn
it a few times I was like a clock just wound up, and, thank God, I have been so ever since.

Mr. John Cameron, M.L. A-. of Kensington Downs, a Director of the Q.N. Bank
and of the Queensland Meat Export Co. reported on Colinton on 7th April, 1900:
“It is impossible to ride over the rich lands of Colinton without coming to the conclusion
that no Just or aopurate idea of their value can be arrived at by merely treating them as
grazing estates, as the large proportion of arable land embraced in them justifies the
belief that In the future a very muoh enhanced value must attaoh to them for cutting up
jJUrposes.’*
CO&XNTON is composed of a variety of sloping well-grassed ridges, with a
splendid percentage of Lucerne Flats, and has long been known as one of the Warmest
Winter Runs, as well as one of the best fastening Runs in Southern Queensland.
OOXiXNTON la magnificently watered by XS miles double frontage
to the Brisbane River, as well as by upwards of AS miles of Per-
manent Creeks.
OOXiXNTON has been kept free from pests suoh as lantana, thistle, and
burrs, ana there is no nut grass and, above all, no ticks.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Vogue la Galere.
A woman gazed on the sea, with eyes
Hard and fierce with a deep despair,
The moon looked down with a cold surmise—
Vogue la Galbre !
The sunset-light from the sky was gone;
Spectral stars with an ioy glare,
Like drowned men’s eyes in the water shone—
Vogue la QaUrt /
Good folk to their homes were walking fast—
Never one with a thought to spare
For that dark form and face aghast—
Vogue la Galbre !
She whispered low: “In that golden Past
Life was good, and I had my share,
But I have done with Life at last—
Vogue la Galbre !
Ah, where are all my Lovers brave—
Lovers who kissed my lips and hair ?
The dearest lies in a bloody grave—
Vogue la Galbre!
My hair was golden, my lips were red ;
Life was gay, and I had no care.
Now I shall soon be cold and dead—
Vogue la Galbre !
I know the story of Magdalen,
She who found forgiveness fair;
But Christ on Earth was living then—
Vogue la Galbre !
They found her body on Sunday morn,
When church-bells rang the folk to prayer.
And whether they rang in grief or scorn—
Vogue la Galbre!
N.S.W.
V. J. DALEY.
Winter.
Winds of Winter ! Wildly wailing
As they pass unheeding by.
Clouds of Winter! Sailing, sailing—
Reckless rovers of the sky—
Of the bleak, untempting snowlands,
And the rolling fog-draped lowlands
Where my kindred love and marry, where they
dream and drudge and die.
Winds of Winter ! Shouting, shrieking}
Brutal, biting, bleak, and blind.
Clouds of Winter ! Sailing, seeking
For the haven none can find.
Clouds of sorrow, lurking, lifting,
Ever drenching, dreaming, drifting,
With the Barren Lands before them, and the
snow-capped peaks behind.
Winds of Winter ! Bore ye seaward
Woeful whispers from the West;
And ye bore a message me-ward
From the lands I love the best,
In your sobbing, sighing, droning;
In your mirth, or maddened moaning ;
From the holy homes of comfort to the scenes of
my unrest.
Cloud and tempest! Ah, they saddened
And they shrouded Life in gray ;
But the golden sun has gladdened
Me, dear distant one, to-day.
And the clouds are lightly lifting—
See them drifting, drifting, drifting
Out beyond the ranges golden—outward, down*
ward, and away.
N.s.w.
GILROONEY.
Dies Irae.
The last great day, it may be near,
Or Man may pass ere it comes here.
There may be nothing but weeds and flowers
Over the Earth in her dying hours ;
Men, beasts, and birds may all be gone
Ere the world’s disaster shall come on ;
Or there may be neither grass nor trees,
But stony wastes round the ashen seas—
No life to take when the days aie dead,
And God is doing the thing He said ;
Nothing but Desolation’s wing
Like a sunless mist o’er everything !
And all the millions long, long gone
To ashes turned in Oblivion ;
And the last great day shall but consume
The bones of a world in its fiery tomb,
As God puts by for ever and aye
The thought of the sorrow that’s passed away!
N.S.W.
R. CRAWFORD.
Forgotten.
Over the mountain white with snow
All day, all day the great clouds go,
The sun above and the shades below.
And down in a nook by a garden gate
While shadows lengthen and day grows late,
A woman waits —how women wait!
’Tis a treasured place—a trysting spot
Of the blissful days of a brighter lot—
She waits for one, but he coineth not.
Behind the mountains white with snow
The great red setting sun sinks low,
A woman sighs and he*
N.S.W.
JOHN PHILIP COOK.
Glasgow Evening News :
It is not generally known that Captain Kossitch, who
commanded the Servian palace guard on the memorable
night of the murders, had his debts paid three days pre-
vmmdv by Queen Draga out of her private means in
order that he might marry the girl of his choice. His
gratitude was to open the gates to his comrades, and
join in the actual murder.

A LIVERS VAGARIES.
Nervous Weakness and Exhaustion.
A Great Change for the Better.
The Case of
Mrs (E. SHEWANj
(BS O UR RKPORTER.)
Mrs. Elizabeth Shewan. of No. 8 Little
Ed ward-street, Pyrmont, was recent!} in-
terviewed by a press representative, and
gave her views on various matters of in-
terest.
“I am given to "understand that you
created a great surprise amongst your
neighbours a little while back,” said the
newspaper man. “ Will you kindly tell me
what it was all about ? ”
“ With the utmost pleasure,” was the
hearty repl\s- It was by shaking off a
long-standing 'complaint that I surprised
everybody, myself included, for I had been
suffering for many years from dyspepsia.
The reason why my recovery was unex-
pected was because X had been under the
care of several doctors, taking their medi-
cines till I positively loathed the sight of it,
besides being treated as an out-door patient
at a hospital for live months, and no good
came from all the physics which had been
administered to me. What else could I
think than that I was incurable ? And to
be in such a hopeless predicament was really
awful. Earth and heaven were both blotted
out of my existence, as the ineffieacy of the
medical treatment I had received left me
alone in the world with nothing but physi-
cal suffering to endure. For years there
was scarcely a day passed bub that 1 felt
intensely tired and so weary and weak that
1 had to let my work go undone because I
was unable to do it, and often I could hardly
get about for the terrible pains in my chest
and between my shoulders. It seemed as if
something heavy was preventing me from
getting my breath, and if the weather was
at all warm the smothering sensations were
frightfully distressing The whole of my
system was out of order, the consequence
being that I was hardly ever without a de-
pressing headache, and a feeling as if my
eyes were swollen also troubled me. When-
ever I made an effort to do a little work the
exertion made me feel lightheaded, and at
times I was afraid to go outside the door on
account of giddiness, which used to seize me
and make me powerless to stand on my feet.
Another reason why I deemed it prmle.it to
keep as still as possible was that any exer-
tion gave me palpitation of the heart. It
might be through getting up from a chair
rather too quickly, or, perhaps, through a
little agitation caused by sounds in the
street that the palpitations came on : but in
every instance I felt greatly distressed, to
say nothing of the fears about what might
be wrong with me which entered my mind
bo many people in this country die from
various affections of the heart that anything
wrong with that organ is naturally alarm-
g, ut I now know that it was my liver
which causes those serious symptoms to
appear.”
evidence 6 ?' ,herC ° ther Uver s y m P toms in
.1 ''Y I f ll, gall-like taste in my mouth
and the furred condition of my tongue
pointed to the fact that my internal organs
required renovating, while to digest my food
was an utter impossibility. Try what I
would in the form of light diet, it was all
of „A e ’ a /. ter eatln g being violent spasms
of wind in the stomach which almost drove
me frantic, and often I felt inclined to roll
the floor in the excess of agony, which
was making my life insupportable The
ood I had eaten was not taken from choice,
because I had no appetite for it, and it was
continuous loss of appetite which
grieved me as I knew that I could never get
strong so long as I was unable to take
nourishment to give me strength. And as
y VJ a ity wasted away, so did my loins
and limbs ache. I felt as sore as if I had
been beaten from head to foot, and no won-
der that my bones were racked with aches,
i my jody and brain seldom got any rest.
fhYn' rY H hts I P assed in profusion, al-
though if I slept at all I dreamed in such a
terrifying manner that wakefulness would
have been far preferable. What a nervous
wreck I became, to be sure ! My nerves
were so weak and highly strung that if any-
body knocked at the door I nearly jumped
out of my chair, and then it took them a
long time to get steady again. A dread of
something happening tormented my mind
both day and night, but did I not have good
reason to be like that, considering that my
disease stood defiantly against my medi-
cines ? ”
“As you are so well now, Mrs. Shewan,
that statement is scarcely correct.”
“I mean up to the time of taking
Clements Tonic ; then everything changed
for the better. X made a great mistake in
not heeding the advice of many of my friends
who had repeatedly urged me to take
Clements Tonic ; but I thought if doctors
could not cure me, nothing could. When at
last I became persuaded, Clements Tonic
soon showed what a mistaken notion this
was, for after taking two or three bottles I
felt quite different, my improved appearance
being the subject of general comment
amongst my neighbours. The appetite I
gained through the medium of Clements
Tonic was, to say the least of it, remark-
able. I was always ready for my meals,
and how could I enjoy them ? That dull
and heavy feeling after eating passed away,
and when my food ceased turning to wind
and creating pain, I could soon go about in
comfort and do a little work, for I always
woke up feeling fresh and vigorous after the
sound sleep which came through using
Clements Tonic. In time my chest and
head pains disappeared, and nothing gave
me greater satisfaction than the b uiishment
of my heart troubles. I could walk about
quite briskly without there being any pal-
pitation or dizziness, the only incoin enience
which I experienced after three weeks’ use
of Clements Tonic being from nervousness,
for I still trembled a little if I had been ex-
erting myself very much ; but in a few days
later .that trouble was entirely remedied,
and it has never come back again. My
digestive system was thoroughly restored to
order, and from that day to this I must say
that the weary aches in my limbs have been
strangers. The acute pains in my head and
shoulders were also carried away by
Clements Tonic, which regulated my in-
ternal organs to a nicety. Such a catalogue
of human suffering as mine was could not
have been more severe, yet Clements Tonic
relieved me of every pain, besides pouring
into my system the invigorating and disease-
curing qualifier it possessed, so that I was
left with the full vigour and health of my
younger days. The return of that alacrity
and cheerfulness of spirits that I was wont
to have made my recovery complete, and I
am not likely to forget that it is to Clements
Tonic that my gratitude is owing.”
“ Will you make that admission through
the press ? ”
“ Willingly, for I recognise the fact that
many people are suffering in despair like I
was, and for their benefit you may publish
my testimony in any way you choose.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Elizabeth Shrwan, of 8 Little Edward-street,
Pyrmonc, Sydney, in the State of New South
Wales, Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly
and sincerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of four folios,
and consecutively numbered from one to four, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 25, 1904.
The Commonwealth and the State Debts.
The recent conference of Treasurers, which met
in Melbourne to discuss the transfer of the State
debts to the Federal authority, dissolved without
really doing anything. It didn’t even say any-
thing very definite. Its best feature was that it
didn’t disagree quite so violently towards the end
as at the beginning, and that it decided to consider
matters again in a future conference, at which it
seems fairly probable the parties will be sufficiently
united to avert personal violence. The objects for
which the late gathering held its disturbance were
twofold —to decide upon some method whereby
the State debts could be taken over by the
Commonwealth on fair terms, and to consider
whether it is possible to regulate and reduce
future borrowing. Nothing was really decided, but
Federal Treasurer Turneii, after thus insinuating
that State borrowing required regulation, got away
alive, which may be held as showing a spirit of
compromise on the part of somebody. And if he
brings his axe with him at next conference he may
manage to go further into the matter and still get
away alive.
• • • •
The position, as it exists now, is somewhat as
follows :
(1) It is advisable, for many reasons, that the Common-
wealth should take over, renew and consolidate the State
debts, and provide a sinking fund for their repayment.
(2) It is advisable that this process should apply to all
the debts—not merely those existing at the beginning of
the Commonwealth. To take over the later liabilities in
the ordinary way would require an amendment of the
Constitution, but the debts that existed at the beginning
of the Commonwealth have been so tangled up with later
loans that it is difficult now to sort them out.
(3) It is necessary that the Commonwealth should see
its way to meet the interest on the debts when they are
taken over. Its present revenues are not sufficient, so it
must either levy direct Federal taxation to make up the
difference (and there seems to be a curious reluctance to
do this) or it must get some reliable assurance from the
States that they will make up their respective deficits
out of their State revenues. The State Governments
seem very reluctant to give anything more than an off-
hand promise that the thing will be all right, and it is
questionable if that is good enough.
(4) If the Commonwealth takes over the State debts it
will have, even if it keeps reducing the debts by means of
a sinking fund, to float a very large renewal loan every
year for the next 48 years, and it wants some guarantee
that the States won’t mop up all the money available
with new State loans, and burst up the credit of Aus-
tralia generally with new extravagances, and thus make
it impossible to float Federal renewal loans, except at
ruinous interest.
(5) If the Australian Government sets to work to reduce
the country’s total indebtedness, or at least to prevent it
iucreasing'at an extravagant rate, by establishing a sink-
ing fund, it wants some assurance that the States won’t
nullify its efforts by borrowing on a still larger scale, and
that the fire won’t spread at one end faster than it can be
extinguished at the other.
The Australian Treasurer, in short, looks on the
transfer of the State debts to the Commonwealth
as a means of reducing the aggregate debt of
Australia. On the other hand, some of the State
Treasurers apparently regard it as a means
whereby the State Governments, having got rid
of their old debts, may haye a better show to pile
up new ones. These two views are so incompatible
that the necessity for Treasurer Turner to bring
an axe with him to the next conference is very
apparent. Before anything definite can be done
it may be necessary to explain to the State
Treasurers that they are an unreliable lot of
profligates, and the man who does that had
better be armed.
Meanwhile itis necessary to face the possibility—
if not the probability—of the present negotiations
falling through. In that case the Australian
Parliament can either let things slide, or start new
negotiations on the same old unpromising lines,
or make an effort on its own account. The last
idea is at least worth considering. The Australian
Parliament couldn’t make any very large imme-
diate effort, but that is no reason why it shouldn’t
do something. To save 10s. at once, and keep on
saving other half-sovereigns regularly, may be a
small thing, but it is better than to do nothing
and merely sit down in a hazy dream about saving
£lOOO by Highly improbable means in the highly
indefinite future.
• • * •
If the State Governments won’t come to any
terms that are agreeable to the Australian Govern-
ment, and if the State Parliaments won’t restrict
their borrowing, it is still in the power of the Aus-
tralian Parliament to achieve by other means
everything that Turner or any other honest
financier can desire. It would require a very strong
Parliament to do it, but it is impossible to say
for certain that the present Australian Parliament
isn’t that kind of Parliament until the proposition
is laid before it. It would take much time, but
time and drought are both plentiful. The central
fact is that the Australian Parliament can take
over the State debts whether the States agree or
not, and it can prevent the States increasing the
aggregate debt of Australia whether the States
agree or not. The process might be slow and
circuitous, but still the thing is possible.
• • • *
The most obvious method is for the Australian
Parliament to decide that it will constitute itself
the States’ creditor. It has unlimited powers of
direct taxation, and as a first step it must get rid
of the absurd theory that these powers were only
given it to be looked at—not to be used. It
should decide to raise, by the least oppressive and
most useful form of direct taxation it can devise—
say a graduated land-tax and an absentee-tax —a
sum of, say, £500,000 a year, to be set aside for
the purpose of buying up in the English market
whatever State securities happen to be the
cheapest at the moment. The interest paid by
the States on the securities thus hpld by the Aus-
tralian Government should also go into the fund
and be similarly applied. Assuming that, when
State loans fell due, the Australian Govern-
ment renewed the portion held by it at
per cent., at the end of 30 years nearly
£26,000,000 of State debt would have been bought
up by the Federal authority by this means alone;
the amount available for the re-purchase of State
securities would have risen to £1,260,000 a year;
and £26,000,000 of debt would be virtually ex-
tinguished, since it would be merely a sum which
the people in their State capacity owed to them-
selves in their Federal capacity.
* * * *
Furthermore, it would be a wise thing for
the Federal Parliament to borrow such sums
as it can raise on fair terms by the sale of
3 per cent, securities repayable only at the
option of the Government, and apply the
proceeds to the re-purchase in the English market
of depreciated State securities, always provided
it could do the transaction at a profit, and what-
ever profit was made should be added to the sink-
ing fund. Also the Australian Government, as it
now controls the Post Office, should establish its
own Post Office Savings Bank, and further con-
stitute itself the States’ chief creditor by applying
a portion of the deposits to the repurchase, in the
English market, of the States’ depreciated
securities. Again, it should seriously consider the
advisability of adopting the Canadian banking
system, whereby the banks are required to hold at
least 40 per cent, of their cash reserves in Federal
paper money. This method, which has been
successfully employed for many years in one of the
most conservative of British States, would give the
Commonwealth a perpetual loan without interest,
of some £8,000,000 or £10,000,000. Assuming
that a portion of this was set aside for the
construction of a capital city, it would still
be possible to devote, perhaps, £4,000,000 to the
repurchase of State securities. The interest on
these would bring another £140,000 a year or so
to the sinking fund. Further, whatever net
income in the shape of ground rents could be
derived from the Federal city and territory
(acquired at practically no cost to the country by
the method already described) could go into the
same fund. By all these devices there is a very
fair probability that, in 30 years or so, the Com-
monwealth could be the chief creditor of the
State Governments, and even if, in all that time,
no agreement was arrived at, a great deal would
have been done in the way of taking over the State
debts. But by that time the State Governments
would almost certainly have a yearning to come
to terms with their principal creditor—an over-
whelming yearning.
* * * *
If, meanwhile, some definite agreement were
arrived at for taking over the State debts bodily,
the fact that a large Federal sinking fund already
existed would be so much to the good. Even if it
could be made reasonably sure that the debts will
be taken over next year, that is no earthly reason
why a sinking fund should not be started this
year. It is never too soon to begin reforming,
While waiting for a definite agreement, the Aus-
tralian Government would be under no obligation
to find the money for the renewal of the
State debts. The States would have to struggle
with the monster they have themselves created
until they came to reasonable terms with
regard to future borrowings, and yet the
reform would be going on all the while. And
if the States, by extravagant borrowing, still kept
the aggregate debt increasing at an unreasonable
rate it would be in the power of the Australian
Parliament to double or treble, by increased taxa-
tion, the amount paid annually into the sinking
fund, in order to give the fund a fair chance of,
at some not too distant date, yielding enough to
counterbalance the increase of debt. In other
words, the Australian Parliament has in its power
to lay down this rule of conduct:
(1) That the State debts are as much its concern as the
concern of the States, because, whether the 4,000,000
Australians owe their debt in a State or a Federal
capacity, it is Australia’s debt all the time, and whether
they are impoverished in their State or Federal capacity
it is Australia’s poverty all the time, and whether they
go bankrupt in their State or Federal capacity it is Aus-
tralia’s bankruptcy all the time.
(2) That it has every right to do what it considers best
to prevent its taxpayers being ruined, either in their
State or Federal capacity, seeing that it depends on these
taxpayers for its revenue.
(3) That it represents Australia quite as much as, or
more than, the State Governments, and it has as much
right to buy Australian securities as the States have to
sell them.
(4) That it has even larger rights of taxation than the
States, and is quite as much entitled to impose taxes in
order to buy up Australian securities and reduce the
country’s foreign debt, as the States have to dodge taxes
by selling Australian securities and adding to the
country’s foreign debt.
(5) That, having these powers, it decides that it is its
duty to set to work to reduce the country’s liability, ana
it has as much right to that opinion as the State Parlia-
ments have to the contrary one.
An Australian Parliament which took up that
attitude would leave a footprint on the sands ot
history that all the wash of the seas of Time would
never quite obliterate. And the Australian
Government which fell through trying to assert
that principle would have greater honor in its fall
than any previous Australian Government would
have got by retaining office for a century.
Dowie.
It is partially regrettable that Sydney should so
publicly have thrown the defunct cat of scorn at
Preacher Dowie of the alleged city of Zion. Also
it is partially not regrettable. It is a pity for the
sake of Sydney that it didn’t simply express its
feelings about the blatant prophet by staying
away; the proceeding would have been more
dignified. At the same time, Sydney was never
very strong on its dignity, and moreover Dowie
is the kind of prophet who, unless the personal
and obvious dead cat of protest is thrown very
straight indeed, would probably go back to Zion
to boast that he had carried a wicked community
by storm, and that his progress through the Corn-
stalk capital had been a long and dazzling
triumph. That would have been a worse
shock to Sydney’s dignity than even the
record of last week’s proceedings. Dowie is
a brassy prophet, and the only certain way
to prevent him putting permanent disgrace on
the city by holding it up as a fool of a place,
which believed in his wings and his reincarnation,
was by Sydney publicly stating its opinion of him
at the top of its voice. To chase the weary pro-
phetic fragment round the city in a cab was a
strong measure and only justifiable under extreme
circumstances, but it at least demonstrated that
Sydney had an intellect which didn’t talm kindly
to wild cat religions and reincarnated Elijahs.
If it were a choice between being a larrikin and
throwing mud at the waistcoat of an elderly
apostle, or being suspected of a belief in the cult
of Zion city, The Bulletin isn’t at all certain
that it wouldn’t rather throw mud at a dozen
elderly apostles. The question is a difficult one,
but there may be times when it is justifiable to
clear on© ?^ character with a half-brick and dissi-
pate base aspersion with rock. It would take a
learned casuist to decide where Christian forbear-
ance should end and rock begin, but that is
another matter.
* • • •
Another view of the case is that when the Law
provides no remedy for a serious wrong the public
is under a great temptation to be a law unto
itself. Dowie and persons of his class have no
claim to the immunities and the consideration
which attach to the ordinary clergyman. The
man who simply preaches the doctrines of the
Bible, or the Koran, or any other alleged
sacred book, has a certain recognised right
to all the protection the Law can give him. He is,
in a general way, a person honestly expounding a
moral volume in which he honestly believes. If
there is anything wrong about the signs and won-
ders and miracles reported in the volume, he
isn’t responsible, and no one can fairly ask him at
this late hour to produce those signs and wonders.
He doesn’t profess to have personally fled round
in a partially nude state in Samaria or to have
“ sassed ” Baal on Mount Carmel. But the man
who professes to personally work by faith the
miracle of healing the sick may be justly asked to
produce his miracle, and if it isn’t forthcoming,
there is nothing in the Scripture against treating
him with the metallic laugh of derision. And the
man who passes a plate with the bald and unsup-
ported statement that he is Elijah, has come
out of the dim and holy hazes of the Scrip-
ture and climbed down into the business
arena, wherein souffle the “doped” horse,
and the confidence man with an uncle in Fiji,
and the wart cure, and other modern money-
making propositions. If he can’t show cause,
then he ranks with the person who floats a mine
on the baseless assurance that it is a reincarnation
of the Mount Morgan or the Garden Gully—the
difference being that the law provides some
remedy if the mine is bogus, but supplies none if
the Elijah is bogus. There is a statute to deal
with the man who passes a bad cheque, but none
to deal with the one who passes a bad pair of
wings or utters a counterfeit halo.
* * * *
To condense the situation, Dowie, as a preacher
of a recognised gospel, and an expounder of the
writings of men who, so far as we know, labored
earnestly with a view to making the world better,
has every right to toleration so long as he ex-
pounds decently and doesn’t bring discredit on his
office. The community has decided by a large
majority to lend at least an outward respect to
those writings. The Statute of Limitations is in
their favor, and the respectability of hoary an-
tiquity is gathered round them. The old prophets
and evangelists showed their honest belief by
living poor and often by dying miserably for their
faith, and the man who disinterestedly expounds
a disinterested belief calls at least for toleration.
But the man who steps right out of the Scriptural
picture, and collects money with stories of faith-
healing power which apparently doesn’t heal, and
passes the plate with yarns of a reincarnated
prophet of whom there is no evidence, is modern
circus—not ancient gospel. The tacit and general
agreement to speak kindly of a venerable tradition,
which is said to have been borne out in its own
day by good, solid evidence, doesn’t apply. When
the public is offered a new gospel it has a
right to ask for the evidence, and to decide,
when it isn’t forthcoming, that it has parted with
its hard-earned twopence under dubious pre-
tences. And if the law won’t give it satisfaction
it has some excuse for trying, in its own undig-
nified way, to get its own satisfaction. The
general agreement that modern religious ministers
must not be disturbed in their services doesn’t
say anything about disturbing ancient prophets
who died 3000 years ago. A reincarnated
Elijah is outside the bargain, and doesn’t come
within the four corners of any recognised gospel.
• Even if the public cast doubts on the solvency of
the Zion Bank, no Dowie has any right to
complain on religious grounds; when a creed is
partly religion and partly bank and building
society, it must expect to be treated like any
other bank or building society. If the public pays
for a motor that won’t mote, or a horse that proves
to be dead, or a faith-healing creed which
doesn’t heal, or a gold mine that contains
no gold, or an Elijah who, on inspection,
seems to be only Dowie, it can’t be severely
blamed, in the absence of legal redress, if it makes
a row like the crowd at a remnant sale howling
for the honest delivery of its remnant.
* * * *
Even the common law right of public meeting
doesn’t cover the case. A meeting at which
admission is by ticket isn’t a public meeting in
the ordinary sense of the term. There was no
legal or recognised right of public meeting, so far
as we are aware, in Elijah’s time, anyhow—at
least, Naboth was dispersed in his vineyard in a
fashion which seems to point that way. And
though it is assumed that at a public meeting the
audience must be cpiiet, and moderately respectful
to the speaker, it is equally essential that he
should not be abusive to the audience. The
short, violent, bad-tempered old prophet from
Zion is a whole riot in himself, and some of
his nasal lieutenants, who fly round before the
show commences, and call the audience “curs,”
“sluts,” “she-devils,” and other larrikin names,
and threaten to throw the public out on the
smallest provocation, are persons whom a really
lawless community would put under the pump.
Moreover, Sydney is strong on trades-unionism,
and it is partially justified in its wrath by Dowie’s
assault on that valued institution. The clerical
profession in general offers, on certain terms,
which are much the same in all churches, the
blessing of eternal happiness. No one can prove
that it doesn't keep its word, and the average
clergyman no doubt honestly believes that his
church is able to keep its word. Downs and his
kind bring the nefarious coupon system into the
church business by offering also faith-healing, a
bank, and a reincarnated prophet. This is equiva-
lent to sweating or working overtime; it tends to
make the churches so many hustling concerns all
trying to outbid the other; it leads to piecework
and under-selling. The world doesn’t want to see
a time when the churches will hang out a sign:
“ This sect gives more for 3d. than any other.”
The religion, so far as it is the same old religion,
may claim to pass muster on the ground that it
has already endured many centuries of inspection,
but the public has every right to scrutinise the
new coupon that goes with it. The unseemly
crowd which said things to Dowie last week
wasn’t satisfied with the coupon.

Cataleptic Hostilities.
Supposing Ruusia goes down before the Japs on
land as it has done on sea, there should be room
for a quaint, though not wholly unprecedented,
development in war. If Russia’s land defences
prove as unready as its sea defences it will
obviously want time for consideration and repair.
It is splendidly situated for both. It can go back
to the Ainoor if necessary, or even further back it
it is driven to it —and then it can sit down ana
wait and re-organise. It can spend five years
reorganising if it wants to. The Jap can hardly
tramp all that distance across Asia in search o
it; it would be an immense proceeding, and
one that would absorb almost all the money
Japan has got, to pursue the Bear into Ins
own strongholds, and the Monkey’s tail would
freeze hard in those wastes. There the Russian
could set himself to repairing, strengthening
and duplicating his Asiatic railway to make it the
military machine it ought to be. He could con-
tinue pouring settlers into southern Siberia, and
spreading cultivation, to make the country more
fit to support a large arpiy. Meanwhile he coul
build more ships in the Baltic and elsewhere, and
train new officers, and set himself to acquiring
those coaling stations he so much needs to support
his fleet on the long voyage to Asia. If the Japs
pressed for a definite settlement he could simpiv
say: “Go away, little fly ! ” If the Japs presse
very much he could say: ‘ 1 Come and look for me
in Moscow if you are in a hurry.” To all demands
for a settlement he could reply that he was coming
back some day, and that when he did every land
title granted by Japan in his temporarily-
abandoned territories would be cancelled and
every Japanese settler ejected. If China took
a hand in the war he might be able to smite
Western China, where the pigtailed one would be
hundreds of miles away fiom Japassistance. 1 j lO
Jap couldn’t force things to a conclusion unless be
sent his fleet round to the Baltic, and that would
be a very large and hopeless undertaking. jhe
Bear could do all this with very little more than
his ordinary expenditure; his army on the ordi-
nary peace footing would suffice for this kind or
warfare. And Japan, if it continued to occupy
On the Land.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
There’s a joyful prospect golden,
On the Land;
There’s a humpy sand-enfolden
On the Land;
There’s a halo in the hazes,
There’s a cure for all the crazes
In the brassy sky that blazes
O’er the Land.
There’s a drought that slays the dingo,
On the Land;
There's a frightful lust for stingo,
On the Land;
And the wrecks of half the cities,
With their million woes and pities,
Come and warble woeful ditties —
On the Land !
There are sheep in every boghole
On the Land ;
There are huts much like a dog-hole.
On the Land ;
There’s a parched grey existence,
’Neath a scorching sky’s insistence,
With no outlet in the distance
But the Sand!
Come, 0 pale and puny townies,
Seek the Land 1
Come and bake the festive brownies
On the Sand !
Come and make you men o’ muscle,
Bare your brea ts for many a tussle,
Crown your work with beery guzzle,
On the Land !
There’s a dearth of fine emotion,
On the Land ;
There’s a wealth o’ curious potion,
On the Land;
There’s a wild mirage before us,
There’s a cloudless sky bent o’er us,
And a dirge-like, cawful chorus
O’er the Land.
So, it’s fill the joyful flagon—
Here’s —“ The Land ! ”
And it’s load the festive waggon,
For the Land;
Leave the town, her daintinesses,
Brimming cups and wanton tresses,
For the life that waits to bless us
On the Land !

heavy extra expenses all the time. Over all bub a

In the interim there would be a state of frozen war,

Russia simply sat down on its snowball and
refused to speak to Napoleon I ; and also some-
thing akin to that in Egypt, when Britain sat
down for ten years preparing for the recapture of
Khartoum. If Russia were defeated on land, and
then simply went away to a safe distance and
camped and boiled the billy while it repaired
damages, and in answer to all demands for a
definite settlement professed that it had never
heard of such a place as Japan, and said that, till
it was ready, it declined to be aware of anything
it didn’t want to know, a very curious deadlock
would arise.

The Cry of Cohen.
Thk other day there returned to Sydney Mr. G.
J. Cohen of the firm of David Cohen and Co.,
and the S.M. Herald interviewed Mr. G. J.
Cohen in a serious spirit. Mr. G. J. Cohen took
the matter also in a serious spirit, and said various
things like this:
In England they feared that Australia had gone mad
with socialistic legislation. The fact of the matter was
that the country must be well governed first of all, and
then more people should be brought into it. It was
WRONG TO STOP IMMIGRATION.
The last remark is one with which The Bulletin
cordially agrees. To stop immigration is sinful
and foolish. And Mr. Cohen might also haveadded
that cannibalism is wrong, and that it is almost
impossible to speak too severely of infanticide,
usury and dog-stealing. But why G. J. Cohen
should throw this useless platitude at Australia
isn’t at all clear. If he simply meant to say, in a
general way, that it was wrong to stop immigra-
tion, then he ranked with the unwise old woman
who remarked sternly that there were milestones
on the Dover Road. If he meant that anybody in
Australia had stopped immigration, then he was
either a shamefully ignorant person or a slanderer
and a speaker of untruths. In the last nine years
before the Commonwealth started, Australia and
lasmania lost 1022 people by excess of departures
over arrivals. In the first two years of Federal
control, under the regime which produced the Six
Hatters incident and other things that the
Cohens of Australia have maundered about at
great length, it gained 6187 by excess of arrivals.
G. J. Cohen s State of N.S.W. shows an even
more remarkable result. In 1902 and 1903 it
gamed 11,446 people by immigration—this after
ten years of heavy loss by excess of departures.
It certainly would be wrong to stop this influx of
desirable immigration which has set in under the
democratic policy of the Australian Government,
and if any remote place, such as Bolivia or Thibet,
lias ixdopted the foolish policy of stopping immi-
gration, G. J. Cohen is right to speak severely to
that place. But he should make it clear what
place he is talking about. If he means to imply
that Australia has done the unwise thing he
alludes to, then he is saying what isn’t true, and
the S M. Hekald, in repeating the remark, is the
purposeful disseminator of a lie.

Playing 1 the Boers’ Game.
The Boer manifesto against the importation of
Chinese labor to the Rand is the first emphatic
indication of how blundering is the anti-imperial
policy of the Jingo-Imperialists in England, who
have allowed themselves to be bound to the
chariot wheels of Israel. It is complained in
English quarters with some bitterness, but little
sense, that the Boer leaders refrained from saying
anything at all about the Chinese labor scheme
until an irrevocable decision had been come to.
But it must be remembered that the Boers,
though beaten in war, are Boers still, and
almost certainly retain the hope of one
day regaining their independence. With that
idea in view, they look upon it as their
business not to prevent, but to profit by, the mis-
takes of the Imperial power. And in this case
the blind following of John Bull to the Johannes-
burg financiers’ lead has brought him into a most
awkward pit, for it has thrown white Africa into
the arms of the Boer party. There were three
parties in S’Africa before—the British whites, the
Boer whites, and the colored people. Now Jingo-
Imperialists re-cast parties into the Boer whites,
the colored men, and the mean whites. No reason
existed why the Boers should have sought to pre-
vent this, though there was every reason why
British Imperialists should. The only possible
“Empire” solution of the S’African position is
the granting of representative Government to the
newly-acquired States under conditions which
will ensure that the British vote will outnumber
the Boer vote. That solution is only to be
reached by an influx of British labor to the mines ;
and “ the Empire ” proposes to drive out British
labor by importing Chinese slaves. Nothing
could be more pleasing to the Boer “ Bitter-
ender.” He sees the Empire sapping its own ram-
parts.

Those 90 Members.
The N.S.W. Commission appointed for the pur-
pose is groping into the tough task of re-dividing
the State. into 90 electorates, and the quantity of
advice it is receiving is large enough to stock half-
a-dozen commissions. If the State had to be cut
up again into 125 districts, each with an equal
number of electors, the figures which have been dug.
out of the last rolls show that the metropolitan
and central area, with 62 members hitherto, would
have 72 ; the eastern district, losing 6, would
have 38; and the west, losing 4, would have 15.
If each of the three districts were given the
same proportion of the 90 as they have had of
the 125, the metropolitan would have 45, the east
32, and the west 13; but if the electorates had all
the same number of electors, the metropolitan
part would gain G, of which the east would lose 4
(leaving it 28), and the west 2 (leaving it 11).
Yet, curiously enough, if the country were cut up
into divisions according to the number of adult
male bread-winners, the present general distribu-
tion would be almost exactly correct; the only
difference would be that the east would lose one,
which the metropolitan area would gain. The
explanation, of course, lies in the fact that
the farther away from the metropolis you get, the
larger the proportion of adult bread-winner you
find, because the outback Billjim either isn’t
married or keeps his wife and encumbrances near
the seaboard. The chances are that the Com-
missioners will use the limit which they are allowed
to come-and-go on with the object of making the
divisions tally as nearly as possible with what
they would be if they were made on the adult
male bread-winner basis; and in that case, as the
metropolitan districts, which grow the fastest,
will be nearest the maximum of electors to start
with, they will be pretty certain to soon get to
twice the size of the outermost district, which
doesn’t grow at all, but is rather inclined to
dwindle away ; and so the present problem of lop-
sided representation will present itself again
within a very few years. The only way out of
that difficulty would be to give the outermost dis-
tricts the biggest number of electors to start with
(in other words, to give them for a start less mem-
ber per head than the metropolis); for that
inequality would soon right itself. The trouble
of the larger representation of the country would
crop up eventually, but it would take longer before
it did crop up. Certainly it might be a rather
rough way to handle Billjim of the Western
horizon, whose complaint even now is that his
own member, having to live alongside Parliament
House, becomes in course of time as much a
representative of the suburb wherein he lives as
he is of Billjim’s electorate which elects him ;
wherefore the suburb gets a member and a-half,
and Billjim, at the best of times, gets only the odd
half. Altogether, it’s a tough problem, which
can best be solved by making Billjim less
dependent on the State Parliament through
dividing up its powers and its duties between the
Federal House and the local governing body.

Depopulation.
“ Filius Crcois ” writes under this title, which
is rather inappropriate, seeing that no country
that we know of, except perhaps Ireland and
Polynesia, is being depopulated :
In a recent Bulletin its leading article commits itself
definitely, in at least two places, to the authoritative
statement that religion has been tried, and tried in vain,
to cure this evil. . If you apply that assertion to Aus-
tralia only, there is even then only a partial truth in it,
the immense majority of Australians being Protestant or
Infidel; the minority of Catholics is to some, of course
regrettable, extent influenced disastrously in the Malthc*
way by their surrounding neighbors. Still, as a
rule, they have large families, thanks to their Arch-
bishop’s fulminations against abortion, to Confession, and
to the teaching of their Church. lam a two-years-old
convert to R.C.-ism, but before that was a Protestant of
the Protestants, even an Orangeman was I, and I quite
literallj' never failed attending church twice a day, yet
never once did I hear my Anglican preacher utter a word
against abortion. Since ni}’change I have very often,
though, listened to vehement denunciations of it. As
the average bushman treats Bulletin statements as
being only a little lower than (statements of) the angels,
he will confidently adduce you as being on his side when-
ever he contends that religion should be kept out of
schools, or that it is powerless to conduce to a nation’s
prosperity. This is such a serious responsibility for Tub
Bulletin to incur that I hope you will insert this protest
of- mine. Looking at other countries we find Protestant
ones with dwindling birth-rates and small families,
Catholic and Greek Church ones rejoicing in high birth-
rates and overflowing quivers. My figures are from Mul-
hall, and the only exceptions to this, France and Ire-
land, can be accounted for thus : the former, as to its
cities, is largely Infidel, the latter sends the flower of its
population just at its best procreating period to America.
Catholic and Greek Church, Birth-rate per thou-
sand, Census 1896 ' Russia 47 "1, Hungary 41 "1, Austria
39-0, Italy 351, Portugal 31'2, Ireland 23’7, France 22'7.
_Protestant Birth-rate per thousand, Census 1896,
Germany Qof which is Oath.) 37‘3, Holland 32 0, Scot-
land 30‘8, Denmark 30.4, Norway 30 0, England 29 2,

Scotland, 40; Denmark, 38; England, 36.
Catholic total, 444; Protestant total, US9-90. Now,
quite apart from mynatural personal wishes that you
would print this, is the desire that you should thus
evince your thorough impartiality ; and, apologising for
seeming to lecture you, do you not think that consigning
my plaint to the w.p.b. lays you terribly open to the
charge of being ready to burke anything that does not
lend itself to previously-made allegations of yours?
“Filius Crucis,” in addition to being gratui-
tously offensive in his concluding remarks, and in
addition to figuring out that there are more
births per marriage in Protestant than in Catholic
countries (he only shows the contrary by including
non-Catholic Russia among the Catholics), some-
what misses the point. Any country can have a
high birth-rate if it is dirty enough and backward
enough to have a high death-rate. If its people
die off, as a rule, about the age of 50, and there is
a very small population that is past the reproduc-
tive age, the birth-rate may look high ; if the
country is so healthy that a large proportion of
the people live far past the reproductive age, a
larger birth-rate may look low. Where there are
1000 people, of whom 333 are past the reproduc-
tive age, the birth-rate may be 20 per 1000. If
these 333 old people were killed off by a mode of
living which ensured early death, the same
birth-rate would be 30 per 1000. Again, in
a country where most of the young children
die through dirt, poverty, ignorance or neglect,
parents cheerfully go on having more children.
In a country where children mostly live they are
reluctant to do so, because there is a limit to the
number any average parent can support. The
virtue of a large birth-rate is often merely the vir-
tue of filth, ignorance, poverty, and wholesale
child-murder. There is room for this year’s child
because last year’s child has dropped into the
grave. As throwing some light on the case,
Coghlan gives recent figures which follow
(“Protestant” and “Catholic” simply signify
that the States are mostly Protestant or mostly
Catholio): —

countries—and any country can have a high birth-

The verdict is that Filius Crucis hasn’t proved
his statements. Case dismissed with costs.

Justice Stephen, in the N.S.W. Full Court the
other day, took the occasion of an application in
the Stelling case to speak of a section of the
Aliens Immigration Restriction Act as outrageous
and tyrannical. He “could not possibly con-
ceive” of anything more wrong than that the
Commonwealth should exclude a man by the
subterfuge of objecting to his want of
knowledge on some subject, instead of say-
ing directly that his criminal habits or his
race made him an “undesirable.” Justice
Stephen, when he is in this vein, should
exhibit that directness and honesty of speech
and method, the lack of which he so much
deplores in the Commonwealth legislation,
and say that the British Government and the
blessed British Crown are low and despicable
characters, seeing that it was they who forced on
Australia the indirect way of excluding objection-
able persons by an education test, when Australia
would fain have taken more direct methods.
Then, even if the British Crown was so angry
that it never gave Mr. Steehen a title as long as
he lived, he would have the satisfaction of know-
ing that he had honestly put the alleged saddle on
the right horse. Meanwhile it is about time Mr.
Stephen was reminded that he is paid to
administer the law only, and that if the country
had thought him qualified to make laws it
would probably have offered him a very different
kind of billet. The opinions of the Government
tinsmith at the railway workshops about the
methods of the Government botanist may be
interesting, but the Government tinsmith isn’t
paid to express them in working hours. And
neither the Judge nor the gaol turnkey nor any
other part of the hired machine for administering
the law is paid to discuss the work of the hired
makers of the law either, in his working hours,
when he is supposed to be doing the work the
Government pays him for, and while he is
wearing out Government property by sitting on
it. Probably if the court policeman, who has
just the same right as his Honor to criticise, in an
official capacity, the country’s legislation, had
stood up and cordially endorsed his Honor’s
views, the impropriety of the situation might
have dawned on his Honor. It would assuredly
have dawned on the policeman. His Honor
would have attended to that.

A wages case of come interest cropped up at Port
Adelaide last week. A sailor sued the master of
an American ship for wages. Counsel for the
defence protested, under orders from the U.S.
Consul, who urged that the matter was beyond the
court’s jurisdiction, and should be left to the
decision of the consul. The magistrates agreed
that they had no jurisdiction. That may be all
right as far as it goes, but, supposing the master
and consul agree not to pay, rightly or wrongly
what, remedy has the seaman got? And if this
decision holds good all round—well, there are
some very small, cheap, unpaid consuls in
Australia, representing little jumped-up States,
wmch lend their flags to cover all manner of
cottin ships, and when the battered old vessel
which, for some reason or other, flies the banner
ot the Empire of Wild Cat coir.es in with a
healthy commotion on board, and the only court
of appeal is to some person with few visible means
of support who represents Wild Cat on the cheap
solely for the sake of the uniform, and if the cap-
tain offers a reasonable consideration, what mav
happen ?

The foolish scattering of energies in the matter of
establishing a decent democratic daily in Mel-
bourne proceeds merrily. The People’s Daily
has already appeared, with daily appeals for
capital. The National Independent is still in
the prospectus stage, and is busily canvassing the
State for more capital ; and the Trades Hall
paper, Progress, still in embryo, is placarding
the town and country with its debenture proposals
for still more capital. And the appeal is to prac-
tically the same people in each case—-to the for-
ward democrats disgusted with Age misrule.
Why can’t forward democracy get some level-
headed business man to frame a compulsory
amalgamation scheme of the three ventures, so
that in a year or so said democracy shall have at
least one press organ, instead of, as prospects are,
none at all, and only the recollection of three
failures ?
