The Bulletin doesn’t pay any great attention to
Book Arcadian Cole and his pamphlet, “What
Forty Eminent Japs say of the White Australia
Act,” but it hereby invites those nigger-loving
Australian papers that already barrack for the
brown man against the white to ponder what
Cole’s 40 eminent men openly aver. “ Japan’s
population is increasing at the rate of 500,000 a
year, and this increase must go and settle some-
where. ... It is absolutely necessary for the
Japanese to go and settle in Australia. . . . The
Japanese must go down to Australia by-and-bye.
... It is quite natural to go from one country
which contains many people into a country that
contains very few; so we have a right to go to
Australia .” Seeing that these are the opinions
of such as Japan’s Minister for Finance, the
President of the Nippon Yushen Kaisha, the
Governor of the Northern Island and others
equally highly-placed, it is evident that when
Japan has time and opportunity it will go in for
real business. The barracking of the aforesaid
papers will do neither harm nor good, but that
doesn’t make their treachery to Australia, in
whooping for the people who are preparing to
swoop down on this continent, any the less.
A short time ago the Melbourne rabbit export-
agents formed a ring to “ regulate ” the price to
be paid trappers for the rabbits. Buyers had —so
it was alleged—forced up prices to a point that
left the agent only the crust of a bare existence,
and the oppressed agent took steps to save him-
self. As soon as this price regulation was dis-
covered, the rabbit trappers’ association took
retaliatory action. Arrangements were made to
receive the rabbits of members at a central Mel-
bourne depot, and a representative was sent to
England to arrange for their sale. Since this
rather unlooked-for development, the Melbourne
papers have printed pathetic wails from the
agents in which it was “ conclusively proved ”
by the agents—that their profits were miserable
in the extreme. One desperate middleman,
indeed, over-did the business by quoting figures
which showed that the agents were losing money
badly, and were in the business from motives of
pure philanthropy. The stony-hearted rabbiters,
however, are determined to try the experiment of
selling their own rabbits. Possibly they are con-
sumed _ with grief over the losses they have—
according to the agents’ figures—inflicted upon an
•honorable body of men, and can’t bring themselves
to run the risk of repeating the shameful deed.
No honest rabbiter could go on employing an
agent when he knew the latter was being steadily
ruined as a consequence. A rabbiter who hoped
to ultimately get to heaven would shudder at such
a deed.

Unscrupulous lie from the S.M. Herald leading
article (18/2/’04):
There can be no doubt that those displaced by the
minimum wage may be counted sometimes bv the thou-
sand. They go to swell the unfed army of unskilled
labor.
The minimum wage, so far as the private em-
ployer is concerned, applies only to manufactures,
and in them it only commenced about 1897.
These are the results as regards the Common-
wealth :
Employees. Increase per year.
1885 .. 105,265 ..
1897 .. 151,775 .. 3876
1902 .. 200,017 .. 9648
Not only was the increase of employment far
larger under the minimum-wage law than under
the system the Herald admires and advocates,
but the increase of employment was largest in
Victoria, the State which first adopted the mini-
mum-wage principle. During the five years 1897-
1902 the number of employees in the manufacturing
industries of the Commonwealth (the only ones
subject to the minimum-wage law) increased by
48,242, and of that increase 20,413 was in the
little State of Victoria, which (under Premier
Turner) invented the minimum-wage idea. It is
on these grounds that this paper calls the S.M.
Herald a liar—and a very unconvincing liar to
boot.

There is actually on view at Sydney
Criterion a modern comedy in which the comedy
doesn’t consist either of people saying “Damn!”
or of Smith being mistaken for another Smith. It
isn’t all fresh paint, of course. Made in England,
and in London Punch office, there had of necessity
to be the mother-in-law joke in “The Lady of
Ostend ” —the large, overpowering, beetle-browed,
pince-nez’d mother-in-law, who wouldn't interfere
between man and wife for the world, yet tells her
poor, dear daughter what she would have
done if ever she had found herself in such a
position. As to that position, it’s just this.
Gregan M'Mahon, who is Dick Wortles, who is
the beetle-browed lady’s son in-law, has been in
the habit of going to Berlin on business—several
sorts of business ; part of it has left him very
bald. At Ostend he had a small affair on
the beach ; a really harmless affair, though
as far as it went it looked highly promising.
Unhappily, for just as far as it did go, it was
followed by a cinematograph ; but M‘Mahon
didn’t know that. A bit later, having been out
till an absurdly early hour in the morning—reach-
ing home, in fact, almost at the moment when the
awful inother-in-law arrives by early train —
M‘Mahon gets into trouble, and tries to smoothe
matters by taking the two ladies for a day in the
city. Dying for a wink of sleep, he inveigles
them to a cinematograph show, where in the dark-
ness he thinks he will get it. And the infernal
machine re-enacts, as picture No. 9, all that
promising little affair at Ostend. It splutters out
just when things had become promising ; and not
all M‘Mahon s explanations will satisfy the
dragon-in-law that the machine had not stopped
just there from motives of modesty.
* * * *
So far, W. F. Hawtrey isn’t in the
“plot.” He comes into the cinematograph busi-
ness when M‘Mahon buys himself up, driven to
the purchase not only by visions of divorce, but
by the just-acquired knowledge that the lady
of Ostend is the comparatively new wife of the
Tipton Slugger. Hawtrey, disguised as a French
baron, speaking with a North-of-England accent,
has been to Ostend, too, as a chum of M‘Mahon’s ;
and he also has had a small and promising affair
with the lady ; and now that film replaces the
other. When (the Baron having his eye on the
dragon’s niece) that one is also bought out, still
another is shown—the dragon-in-law’s own hus-
band this time, also fooling with the same female.
The explanation is that, before she married, the
“ lady of Ostend ” was engaged by the cinemato-
graph people to work up a series of “comics”;
and when at 10.30 or so, she explains this in a
bright-red frock and awful grammar, and says
that everything stopped just when the machine
stopped, everybody accepts the explanation, and
nobody gets either the black eye or the rule nisi
that was threatening.
* •* * *
Mostly the dialogue is smart, and the
humor more or less real. Certainly here and
there an old-fashioned Punch pun bumps up
against you. “I’m a perfect Jonah,” M‘Mahon
whines when some new sample of hard luck comes
his way. “ That’s why you’re always wailing, I
suppose,” says someone. But it is not all like
that. Gregan M‘Mahon’s part is really the big
one of the piece ; Hawtrey (Oft. some inches high)
couldn’t very well compress himself into the sort
of small, nervous son-in-law that a stage
mother-in-law requires. Miss Mabel Lane is the
wife, and Miss Brenton the dragon ; and in the
dragon’s husband, old and gay and shy, Oily
Deering has a fat part that suits him admirably.
In fact, there are no misfits in the cast; no
doubt the co.’s long season in Maoriland accounts
for the smoothness of the first Australian per-
formance.
** * *
Some years ago, before the Easter card, let
alone the Xmas card, had lost its vogue, there was
one beautiful frosted and ornate bit of cardboard
bearing a peal of bells, some sheep-faced angels,
and a verse that ran: “A voice grows with the
growing years. Earth hushing down her bitter
cry Looks upward from the graves and hears
‘The Resurrection and the Life am I. ’ ” This
specimen was the favorite of the season, and all
young and old ladies sent or received a duplicate
and found the lines as soothing as a noonday sleep
in a hammock. There is a flavor of that old
Easter card sentiment—a sort of holy sediment of
the ancient hymn—in the Tolstoy play, “Resur-
rection,” now dragging along its moral length at
Her Majesty’s (Sydney). In addition to rousing
a good old Easter remark from its grave, the play
incidentally draws attention to the Russian penal
system. There are whip-cracks and shrieks, and
the warders haven’t the manners of aides-de-
camp ; but remembering the tales of our
own system we find that the Tolstoyan
harrow is minus teeth. The dramatisers of
“Resurrection” were concerned only about the
fashioning of a play that would hold a many-
sided audience, and they succeeded. Roys and
lust and religion and humor and superstition are
the threads of its tapestry, and behind it sounds
the Easter hymn, and a lot of often lugubrious
but always Slavonic music. There are two on-the-
edge-of-the-moral-precipice scenes, but the audi-
ence looks on unabashed instead of through its
fingers. Eastercustoms, Eastereggs,andtheEaster
holidays of Prince Dimitry are in the foref ron t of the
play and one is rejoiced to see the samovar without
which no Russian story is complete. The humor-
ous seene is that of the jury-room, and the audi-
ence promptly christened two of the jurymen
Dowie and Larry Foley respectively, so true to
life was the possibly intentional make-up of the
actors. Julius Knight and Maud Jeffries and a
long caste of other seasoned artists, uphold the
drama’s considerable Weight with great apparent
ease. j

“ The Best Must Win,” produced at
Sydney Lyceum on Saturday night, hadn’t a
single horse in its composition, but made up for the
lack, in a way, with hearses. It contains, pro-
bably, more murders to the square yard than any
other Lyceum horror to date, and, as its most at-
tractive feature, offers a sort of combination of girl
and electrical machine. You are tempted to kiss
the girl, and an electrical current promptly slays
you; also the girl vanishes and a skeleton appears
and grins over your dead body. The grin is under-
stood to prevent the victim being resurrected
by Dowie or any other prophet in the faith-healing
or raising-from-the-dead line. There is a rich
profusion of villains in the piece. H. O. Willard
is a smooth villain, who remarks that he will
strangle a few people next morning in the same
tone as the average man Uses to say it’s a fine day;
Lawrence Dunbar is an inferior villain, who occa-
sionally gets slightly excited when the murders
are more than ordinarily atrocious ; and Stirling
Whyte is a third-class, cheap-doss-house sort of
villain. With this glittering array of monsters
the clown of the circus gets little show, and
Bert Bailey’s funniments prove rather wearisome;
he disturbs the happy progress of the battue.
The various womenfolk act as brightly as they are
allowed to, Miss Ada Guildford, Miss Ethel
Buckley and Miss Ivy Gorrick in particular. As
the hero Robert Inman rolls his tenebrous voice
around unctuously and confounds the murderers
in the last scene of the last act with satisfactory
completeness. For those who like their horrors
neat and raw with plenty of blood and groan
“The Best Must Win” is cordially recom-
mended.

“ Florodora,” with its charming lyrics,
has been attracting good houses to Sydney Royal
during the week, but will be withdrawn to make
room for “San Toy” on Saturday. The cast of
“ San Toy ” will be somewhat different from that
at the last presentation in Sydney, but Fred

** * *
Yulcana’s sun is setting in golden glory at
the Tivoli, and Clarke and Hamilton have arrived
to keep the Rickards empyrean in its proper state
of illumination. Clarke draws most of his fire
from the piano, and Miss Hamilton from her feet
and her throat. In plain Lower George-street
language, Clarke is a musician and his partner a
song-and-dance artist, only a. different kind of
song-and-dance artist. With the grotesque
Dumonds still on hand the bill is unusually strong
in music just now. Herein is seen the patriotic
statesmanship of the management, which, having
lately glorified handcuff-smashing, iron-breaking,
and the development of a mighty muscle, now
calms the savage breast and the uncultured back
(muscle-shows seem to be mostly back) with sweet
melody. Certainly it is many a day since the
Rickards house has had a more artistic turn than
that of the Dumonds, from whom the audiences
want more and more every night.

Miss Maud Dalrymple’s benefit at Sydney
Town Hall, on Saturday night, attracted a full
house, and the popular contralto ought to get a
cheque for about £2OO out of the proceeds. Miss
Dalrymple really looks as if she enjoys herself
when she sings ; and she ought, when she gets a
surer grip on her big voice, to hold her own fairly
easily in Fogtown. The rest of the programme
included Misses Usher, Mount, and M’Kenzie,
and Messrs. Gooud, Campbell, and Syl es.

At Sydney Town Hall, on Saturday,
Arthur Pratt’s Concert Co., supported by Ma-
dame Slapoffski and other singers of note, will
try to give a hand to the Queen Victoria Con-
sumptive Homes and the Fresh Air League. The
object in view is so commendable, and the chance
of breathing in the same atmosphere with a
number of eminent social lights is so bright, that
the public ought to roll up in multitudes and give
two deserving charities a lift such as they have
never had before.
** * *
Bland ljjolfc’s cyclodrama promises to run
another week at Melbourne Royal, what time
“ The Flood Tide ” is being made ready for pro-
duction. The bicycle race at the Exhibition Oval
is not the only feature of “Riding to Win ’’ that
excites a warm, neighborly regard, for Bland’s
amazing show. As a work of fiction, it is about
as unconvincing as ’Lijah Dowie from Zion, but
although the narrative is a target for scorn the
scenes of action are rich in local truth. Faint
odors of the Yarra come floating o’er the orchestra.
Slumbering memories of Richmond Reserve and
Studley Park are awakened in many a wrinkled
soul. The Botanical Gardens are brought so near to
the audience that an “ engaged ” girl in the back
row of the d.c. furtively pinches the arm of her in-
tended victim. This is to remind him of the fatal
Saturday afternoon when she lured him into the
fernery at the aforesaid Gardens and wrung a
definite proposal from him. “Riding to Win”
appeals to local feelings, and Brunton s stage
pictures of the surrounding district are accepted
as an ample apology for the sins of the dramatist,
whoever he may have been. The programme
conceals the dramatist’s identity.
* * #
The next Druty Lane drama on Bland
Holt’s list, and the one with which he will
conclude his present season in Melbourne, differs
essentially from “White Heather,” “Great
Ruby,” “Price of Peace,” “Great Millionaire,”
or any other of the Drury Lane plays produced of
late years. This forthcoming novelty “The
Flood Tide” —is described by author Cecil
Raleigh as a melo-farce, and its fattest part falls
to the low comediaii. Bland Holt will play the
foremost character in “ The Flood Tide,”
speaking only the lilies allotted to it, whereas
in some of the previous plays by Raleigh, and
Hamilton and Raleigh, the low comedian's
part has been altered or padded (in Australia) to
give Bland a promihbnce which marred the effect
of the work, though it probably pleased the
gallery. In “The Great Millionaire” this was
absurdly noticeable. The omission of the old-
fashioned “comic relief” from the modern Drury
Lane melodrama, and the introduction of a
grande dame coniedy character, written for
the inimitable Mrs. John Wood, has put Bland
Holt’s show at a double disadvantage. He played
his own part in the wrong key, with interpolated
variations as a rule, and thereby put the whole
performance rathet* out of tune. The parts
intended for Mrs. Wood in London have been
undertaken here by Mrs. Holt, who treats them
quietly and pleasantly, but is not able to give
them their proper value. She lacks the florid
dignity, the humorous mannerisms, and the trick
of pointing pungent lines in a pungent style. Mrs.
Holt imparts the necessary kindliness to those
serio-comic grande dame characters, but her suc-
cess otherwise is only negative. She is better
than many another might be, if called upon to
take her place. All the same, it is a pity that
such fine parts should be only moderately well
displayed while the most versatile and accom-
plished “old woman” actress in Australia —
meaning Mrs. G. B. Lewis —is mostly out of
engagement. Always a trifle overwhelming, i.e.,
a little too much of the old-school actress in
modern comedy, Mrs. G.B.L. would give all due
color and importance to a congenial character in
literary melodrama. One would like to see her as
Lady Janet in “ White Heather,” for instance.
** * *
Florence Young, with her valuable voice
in good going order, re-appeared as “A Country
Girl” last week. Also sundry new items were
introduced into that standard attraction on
Saturday night. One item was a common or
garden sort of comic song, from which the humor
had been carefully omitted, and another novelty
was “Hiawatha,” a song and dance for a double
sextette. The merits of this alleged “maddening
melody ” may reveal themselves later, but on
Saturday the united efforts of the twelve damsels
made no impression to speak of.
*« * *
At the Opera House, a new ventriloquist
is doing his best to differ from a long series of
previous ventriloquists. In some respects, the
hard-working stranger is successful. Prof. Dewar’s
educated dogs and cats, late of Sydney, made a
triumphant first appearance last Saturday. The
Empire Girls have changed two of their songs,
and the second edition of the Amateur Cake-
Walk is even stronger than the first edition in a
certain element of pathos. Two of the competing
couples are very young, and their air of anxiety
touches a thoughtful observer to the soul.
*# * *
“ Beauty and the Beast,” at the Adelaide
Royal, had the misfortune to strike a week in
which all Adelaide felt more like going out and
sitting with its feet in the sea, and with the husk
of a water melon on its head, than crowding into a
theatre. Therefore, the excellent promise of its
first night was hardly fulfilled. Shine has worked
hard as the amorous widow who, thoughtfully
padded with ice, can sit on a fire and remain un-
moved. And there are other bright spots. But
the general verdict seems to be that of Pinero’s
Frenchman: “Splendid ! Magnificent!! Pretty
good !!! ”

Howard Vernon, with Victor Prince as
funny man, finished a season at Launceston
Mechanics’ last week. They arrived from Hobart
in the nick of time, when Bain had to leave the
Academy of Music, and Spurr hadn’t arrived, and
Launceston was without a show, and they did
fair business with a company of four girls and
four men, who, in pieces like “Uncle Tom’s
Cabin” and “Maid Marian,” multiplied them-
selves by four and made a huge bill. On Sunday
night the Lord High Executioner was seen stand-
ing at a little table in the lobby, plate in hand,
taking up the collection as the crowd passed in to
a “sacred concert.” The night after Vernon
left, the Calvinistie Presbyterians took the hall to
feast the Moderator of All the Australias, who,
in cutaway coat, lace, frills, knee-breeches, silk
stockings, and a braw Scotch brogue, was a pic-
turesque figure between the wings, with a nice
bit of scenery in the background.
The new-chum’s story in brief : Chap. 1.,
swell; chap. 11., swill; chap. 111., swag.
“H.”: Sick man Shiels has apparently
decided not to stand at next Vic. election. Ewen
Cameron, who sat for Portland under the old
order of things, is out for GUenelg, which was to
be Shiels’s constituency. The Portland district
had been rolled into Glenelg, and it was under-
stood that Cameron was going to step aside for
Shiels, and give him a straight run against young
Burke, who is standing in the Labor interest.
There is now no doubt that Shiels is suffering
badly from heart trouble. The doctors are inject-
ing nitro-glycerine and administering same in
tabloids.

[A further £500,000 of Treasury bills have
been placed in London . . . Mr. Waddell, the Treasurer,
says that the State’s financial position has wonderfully
improved, and is now stronger than it has been for a long
while. Financing is now causing him no trouble.—
Daily Paper.]
The Advances to Settlers Board is the nearest
approach that N.S. Wales has to a State Bank ; it is, in
fact, supposed to be doing the same work as the Agricul-
tural Banks of Westralia and Queensland and the State
Banks of S. Australia, Victoria and Maoriland. But
while all these latter issue reports as ample and clear as
any private bank, and in some respects more ample, the
only information available regarding the N.S. W. Ad-
vances to Settlers institution is contained in the annual
report of the Bands Department, which devotes a few
casual paragraphs to the subject. There is nothing m
the way of either a profit and loss account or a balance
sheet; and even the figures that are quoted are conflict-
ing and apparently incorrect. Year by year the advances
are said to have been as follows :
1899 .. £121,834 I 1901 .. £35,865
1900 .. 81,109 | 1903 .. 98,847
This makes a total of £337,655. The last report says,
however, that £338,005 has been advanced. There is a
discrepancy of £350 here. In the report for 1901 it was
said that the total amount advanced was £239,218. Is
the past year (1902) it is said that the advances were
£98,847, which, with the amount previously advanced,
indicates, it is reported, a total advance of £338,005.
But it doesn’t do anything of the sort—the total of these
figures is £338,165. There is a discrepancy of £60 here.
Last year there was a discrepancy of £413. There is
always some discrepancy, and it does not even have. the
merit of being the same ; there is a new one every time.
As to receipts the position seems to be : —
Principal
Repaid. Interest.
Paid in 1901 and prior
thereto .. ,, £34,031 £9063
1902 .. ... .. 27,313 6394
If the advances have totalled £338,005, the amount out-
standing at the end of 1902 was £276,661. Apparently,
therefore, the work of collecting the interest was none
too successful. But exactly how much interest and in-
stalments of principal were overdue is not shown, nor is
anything said as to the cost of management. As to
whether the institution is a success or a howling failure
it is impossible to judge, though it is pretty hard
to believe that a concern run on these slipshod lines can
be anything but a failure. Surely a money-lending
concern which is mopping up £100,000 or so of public
money annually ought to be run upon business lines, and
should be compelled to furnish the public who are
supplying the funds with some reasonable information as
to its proceedings.

And he didn’t refuse me. Begone, dull Care 1

To talk of such nothings ! Begone, dull Care 1

And of praise for Prosperity’s dawning ;
And what is Prosperity for
If it isn’t to help us in pawning !

Let’s drink and be merry 1 Begone, dull Care !

It is recognised that much of the loss in con-
centration is due to the feeding at the same time of
many different sizes of ore particles, and while to a
certain extent the loss is obviated by the installation of
hyaraulic classifiers, the constant addition of volume of
water in the various stages of classification practically
always entails a heavy loss in fine mineral carried away
ln overflow,, thus, though a more or less regularly
graded product is obtained and the percentage of saving
over the concentrators, calculated on the value of the
material fed on to them, has been increased, yet the
aggregate loss has not been diminished. The McDermott
Sizer differs from other classifiers from the fact that the
separation into various sizes is effected by means of a sub-
merged screen. Careful tests have proved there is an abso-
lute freedom of movement of thfe particles under water,
and that the pulp can pass in succession through a series
of screens each finer than the preceeding, thus permitting
effective separation into sizes most admirably adapted for
close concentration work. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company of South Melbourne, are the Agents and Sole
Manufacturers of the “ McDermott Sizer.”*

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, INew Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

The figures for the half-year to June 30th, 1903, are not
given above as they appeared six months ago, because after
the balance-sheet and report were issued it was decided
to pay a bonus of Is. a share in addition to the ordinary
6 per cent, dividend. The erratic movements are due to
sales of property on time-payment. When a property
is sold the society puts most of the profit aside until it is
reasonably sure that the goods will not fall back into
its hands ; and when it is thus reasonably sure, it takes
the money out of its private hoard in a lump and puts it
into a “ contingent account ”in the balance sheet. But,
though the amount may then be pretty safely reckoned
as profit, things occasionally go wrong, as happened
during the past half-year, when this contingent account
had to be drawn upon. In the past six months £223 has
been written off, bringing down the net profit to £385.
Even that, though, is at the rate of 11J per cent, per
annum on the paid capital of £6843, or of 5£ per cent, on
capital and reserves combined.
• * * *
A Mr. Bottomley is roaming Queensland in the
interests of the British Cotton-growing Association ; but
he carries no authority with him in the way of offering
guarantees or prices. Minister of Agriculture Denham,
who is something in the produce line himself, reckons
that if a price (say lfd. per lb. in the seed) were fixed for
three years, it would be possible to do something; and
Bottomley has sent back asking whether the Association
will do business on those terms. Meantime, the Depart-
ment has sent to America for a quantity of the best seed
that can be got; experimental plots, planted with the
few grains that were available in Australia, are already
springing up all over the Central district; and “The
Cotton Industry ” has reached a permanent place among
the headlines in the daily papers.

le TL sugar mills which were erected
with State help in Queensland, mostly under Philp’s
business administration, have (under the same “ busi-
l!? s ? administration) drifted so far into arrears that the
state Solicitor is now carrying through the foreclosure of
one of them, and something will certainly have to be done
wuh a few of the others. Only three-the Isis, Mulgrave
and Mossman—have met all obligations ; several of the
others were quite surprised when the Morgan-Browne
Government seriously asked what they proposed
J.™ ln u th , e "ay of paying-up. The “business”
Mimstry had allowed them to drift into the
idea that the debt to the Government was a
myth, of value only for parading before Bull-Cohen as an
asset, and that demands for payment were things to be
merely winked at. In these mills £411,109 of borrowed
money has been sunk, of which £41,497 has been repaid
(to the Government—not to Cohen), leaving £369 612
arrears redemption money alone amount
to ±34,305. In numerous cases it is ascertained that,
though the law said that there should be no advance
unless there was sufficient land to keep the mill going
there was not anything like enough land for the size of
the mill. A null a fourth of the size of that at Proser-
pine, for example, would have answered all requirements.
as matters stand, the cane-farmer-; are being bled to
death in order to pay interest on this absurd capitalisa-
uon In every such case the Morgan-Browne Govern-
ment is about to insist upon its rights as an active co-
partner, and will put into the management of these mills
?.?“! tbe economy which it is putting into the manage-
ment of the public departments.

* * * *
Roma (Western Q.) has a bore which emits
both gas and water, and as the Government hydraulic
engineer has separated them, the town is to be supplied
with light and water from the one source—a novelt3' for
Australia and for most other places. Roma’s natural gas
has been tested, and reported to be 30 per cent, better
than the manufactured article. The local Council has
fought shy of syndicates that have wanted to get
control of the supply, and proposes to itself raise the
necessary capital through the State for the undertaking.

This is a belated report and a curious one. In the previous
report the directors announced that they had decided to
pass a dividend, the whole .year’s profit being required as
additional capital. Six months afterwards, nevertheless,
they not only paid a dividend, but a bonus as well, the
two together absorbing £905 ; and another at the rate of
10 per cent, followed. Together these mop up a great deal
more than the .year’s ordinary trading profits. But, in addi-
tion to that, the contingency account was drawn upon to
the extent of £376, apparently through losses which could
not otherwise be provided for. No allowance was made,
either, in the profit and loss account for wear and tear.
In these and other ways it is made to appear that the
balance brought forward from the previous year was
£1214, when, as a matter of fact, it was £2119 ; the divi-
dend and bonus paid in February, 1903, is made to appear
as though it were paid for the previous year(which ended
on June 30th, 1902); and a profit of £iolo is shown for
the past year instead of one of £544, which is the correct
figure after all proper deductions have been made. Alto-
gether an unsatisfactory state of affairs is disclosed.

Of the nominal reserves £47,750 consists of depreciation
fund. Formerly a specified part of the profit was transferred
to this fund, and whatever amount was written off was
taken from the fund. Latterly, however, the evil habit has
been contracted of bunching two items together like this,
dealing with appropriations of profit“ For de-
preciation, including provision for writing down
N.S.W. Creamery Butter Co.’s shares, £5750.’’ This item
appeared six months ago, and we have to wait until now
to find out that £2OOO was written off and £3750 added to
the fund. For the past half-year there is the same
bunching ; but, as the Creamery Butter shares stand at
only £5OO, presumably that is the amount which is to be
written off, leaving the balance to go to the nominal re-
serves. There ought not, however, to be any need of
these presumptions in regard to a balance sheet. Profits
show a still further recovery, and are now back to the
level at which they stood in the dividend-paying days of
the co. Moreover, the financial position shows very
marked improvement. The overdrafts have been reduced
handsomely during the year ; deducting cash and credit
balances, the totals have lately moved as follows:
June, 1899 .. .. £8,106 Dec., 1901 .. ..£15,054
Dec., 1899 .. .. 12,643 June, 1902 .. .. 20,839
June, 1900 .. .. 22,149 Dec., 1902 .. .. 22,057
Dec., 1900 .. .. 21,394 June, 1903 .. .. 22,213
June, 1901 .. .. 26,540 Dec., 1903 .. .. 11,260
During the previous 12 months heavy additions to plant,
totalling £5690, were made; an expenditure of only
£76 on this account during the last half-year, accom-
panied by an increase in profit, is mainly responsible for
the change of complexion in the overdrafts. A sub-
stantial reduction in amounts due to the co. and a slight
increase in bills owing, have also helped. On the
paid capital (£165,114) the half-year’s profits were at the
rate of just 8 per cent, per annum. The reserves in the
table at the top of this are referred to as nominal,
because a goodwill paper item is included in the assets.
Being bunched with other things—the co. has the
bunching habit rather badly—it is impossible to state
the amount or the real position.
* * * *
Broken Hill’s present talk is mostly of zinc-
production. Potter’s chemical process, which brought
the B.H. Prop, into the field with the Delprat process, is
now in the hands of a Melbourne co., with enough capital
to sell licenses on a royalty basis to mining eo.’s, but not
enough, it seems, to put up plant itself and handle stuff
for customers. The magnetic-separation people have, so
far, proved themselves a good deal more confident about
their process; they have themselves put up a large
plant, and come into the field of zinc “ mid-
dlings.” Ullrich, manager oU these works, is satisfied
that the magnetic system is the right thing, particularly
so far as the ore from the northern mines is concerned.
That ore suffers more from the presence of rhodonite
than the southern or B.H. Prop, ores do, and the magnets
get the rhodonite out without much trouble. The Sul-
phide Corporation is working with a magnetic plant,
which it is increasing ; but beyond an official statement
that results are profitable, nothing has been made known.
The B.H. Prop, has a plant under construction to work
the Delprat process, which depends upon chemicals ; and
a subsidiary plant to produce sulphuric acid (the chemical
required) is going up on the mine. This plant will be an
adaptation of the Carmichael-Bradford plant, which was
intended originally, not as a producer of acid, but as an
oxidising plant—to get rid of the sulphur, and convert
the sulphides into a smelting ore. It was found, how-
ever, that the gases could be easily and very cheaply
saved ; hence this new use for the plant. But the
trouble with all these zinc processes is that they don’t
produce metallic zinc ; they only produce a concentrate
of comparatively low commercial value, for shipment to
Europe, where the actual zinc-smelting is done. And it
is the zinc-smelting that Australia really wants, because
it is in that process that most of the coal, labor, etc.,
which make zinc worth £2l a ton are absorbed. A bonus
of £20,000 to be given on the first 20,000 tons of zinc
produced would probably put £1,000,000 into Australia’s
pouch within seven years.

The gross profit shown is the profit without deduction
for depreciation ; the amount thus deducted varies
considerably; last half-year it was £19,916. The price
realised for silver was 2s. 2 27-32 d. per oz. fine, or nearly
3 ? d. more than in the preceding half; lead averaged
£9 19s. 10d., or 11s. 4d. per ton less. This is the lowest
average for lead, with the exception of one half-year,
since 1894. For silver the price obtained is about the
average for the past 10 years. Basing the gross profit on
the lead output alone, it is equal to £2 19s. 9d. per ton of
that metal produced. That is to say, if silver were to
maintain this average, the co. could pay its way with
lead at £7 os. Id. per ton. Or silver could fall to Is. 9|d.
fine (equal to about Is. Bd. standard), and lead
could at the same time fall to £9 before the
co.’s operations would show a loss. These prices
are net to the co. The market quotation is, for lead,
10s. or 15s. higher; so that profit would disappear
when the market quotations reached, say, Is. BAd.
for silver and £9 15s. for lead. The co.’s liquid
assets, including reserve funds, show a surplus of
£426,706 over current liabilities, as against £401,304
six months previously. But of the reserve fund £40,000
is invested in the Iron Knob flux tramway, which
can’t be regarded as a liquid asset. Splendid progress
has been made in the way of eco lomies during the past
three years. For the corresponding half of 1900 silver
(fine) brought 2s. 6 27-32 d.„ and lead £l6 9s. Bd. ; the out-
put then was 2,780,937®z. silver and 21,855 tons lead ; the
gross profit £167,795. But if the past half-year’s prices
had ruled then, an actual loss of £14,002 would have
been shown, assuming that the same ore was handled.
Yet, in consequence of various economies, the co. can
work on present prices now, and show a gross profit of
£91,999. This means that the whole of the present pro-
fit, and a little more, springs from the economies which
have been introduced during the past three years,
notably in the re-treatment of “slimes/' the oxidising of
the sulphide ores, and in the local production of coke.
* * *
In the old style gold mine, equipped with the
primitive battery, in which little change was made in a
generation, it wasn’t so necessary to write large amounts
off the value of machinery as it is in connection with the
modern mine, whose complex appliances are scrapped
every year or two. Take the case of the Broken Hill
Prop. In 18 years its entire plant has been re-cast about
five times. Some huge and costly machinery—the amalga-
mating mill, for instance—had less than two years’life.
The old concentrating mill had less than that. The
smelting works have been re-modelled wholly, or almost
so, on the average every three years. From the first to
as far as it has gone, £1,290,093 appears to have been
spent on construction, of which £875,328 has been written
off. The present outlay is at the rate of about £40,000,
and the writing-offs average the same.
* * » *
South Blocks Co. (Broken Hill) has got its
credit balance down to £1650, and estimates that the ore
at grass is worth £1450. It is doubtful whether anyone
would give that many pence for it as it stands. If it
doesn’t pay the North and the Junction, with their own
mills and the railway right under their bins, to work, it
certainly would not pay to cart the crude South Blocks
ore away in drays to be milled. The mine’s value is
purely speculative ; it will only be worth anything when
something more is found out about sulphide treatment
or when the metal markets boom again.

* * . •
N.S.W. Hull Court has unstrung a millstone
from the necks of managers who run wild cats that don’t
pay their miners’ wages. The idea has always been that,
not only could the manager be sued, but he might also
be cast into prison in default. Tub Bulletin doesn’t
know a case in which a manager actually has served a
term on this account, though a western manager got as
far as the waiting-room before his friends put up the
money-. But when, a few weeks ago, the manager of the
Great Lucknow Joke, outside Orange, was ordered to pay
or do three months, J. Bull having failed in his remit-
tances, the matter was taken to the Full Court, which set
the order aside. It is certainly very severe on a manager
to gaol him for the yemissness of a co. which, in most
cases, leaves him in the lurch as well as the men (often
further in the lurch, in fact) ; but while gaol was possible
he generally took care to see that the funds were avail-
able. If they were not, he would not take the responsi-
bflffy of keeping the men at work. Probably the most
effective way out of the difficulty which the Full Court
has created w r ould be to assume that men who haven’t
been paid haven’t worked—in other words, that the labor
conditions have not been fulfilled, and that the leases
are consequently liable to forfeiture.

* * * *
Dowie’s officers have a table to themselves
at the hotels where they roost. They don’t drink
anything with their meals except the usual tee-
total stuff—tea, ginger-ale, lemonade, &c. But
the appetites of these people, whose eyes are fixed
upon the good things of the Hereafter and not
upon the flesh-pots of Egypt, are something tre-
mendous, and they keep the waiters on the run
the whole time. Much-travelled people say that
nothing in the world compares with the appetite
of a Yankee saint, except the appetite of the
advance agent of a Buffalo Bill show. Elijah
himself is credited with having a perfect ‘‘freak”
appetite, but he doesn’t make it public. His
meals are served privately in a suite of rooms
costing =£3o per week. That is £3O for the suite
and the meals—not £3O for the meals alone.
* * * •
Elijah Dowie uttered an awful threat at
Sydney Town Hall the other night:
If there ia any more trouble I shall call on Governor
Rawson ; and, if necessary, get the military placed around
this building.
There is a large, off-hand sound about that sort
of thing, but probably if Dowie’s pale, disconsolate
whiskers did wander up to Government House in
the moonlight to “call on Governor Rawson,”
his Ex. would simply send the assistant
flunkey to say he was out, and didn’t expect to be
back till next April. And as for lending Dowie
a cannon to point at his congregation, if a preacher
can’t scare his congregation with Sheol he should
go out of the business.
* * * •
“ Hermes ” on an urgent matter :
If Dowie is Elijah, then Elijah was Dowie, and this
being granted, the question arises why was Dowie not
excluded under the Undesirable Aliens Act? On his past
record Dowie is not a fit person to he at large in any
decent, law-abiding community. According to the
accepted account, Dowie’s first appearance in the pro-
phetic business was somewhere about the year 929 8.0.,
when he informed Ahab, sometime King of Israel, that he
had effected a corner in the natural rainfall of the coun-
try. He made no offer to supply wtt on fair terms,
but cut it off in a brutal, illegal manner
without any warning whatsoever. Having committed
this outrage on a community that had done him
no harm (his own community in fact), he retired
into hiding by the brook of Oherith, near Jordan,
and so prevented the Government making any terms
wifi him for the break-up of the drought. The result
wag one 0 f j be mos t horrible famines in history'. This
f ac t, alone proves that Dowie Is a dangerous man to be at
large in Australia. The w'hole of his career was a record
of crime and bloodshed. Even at that remote period
Dowie’s language was calculated to lead to a breach of
th a " nd had he had his deserts he would have been
fined heavily in the police court at Samaria. Also, he
was a s intolerant as a human being well could be. His
hatred of rival sects caused him to proceed to shocking
lengths. After a demonstration on Mt. Carmel, 450
clergymen of the then Established Church fell into his
hands, and by his orders were massacred by the
push. For this atrocity Mr. Dowie was never
placed on his trial, and although the crime was
committed outside of Australian jurisdiction, it
warrants Mr. Dowie’s immediate expulsion from the
Commonwealth. In or about the year 896 B.C. a warrant
was issued for Mr. Dowie’s arrest, and instead of using
his scientific powers for the temporary disablement of
the soldiers who were sent to execute the warrant, this
bloodthirsty person actually destroyed them by fire.
Two officers and one hundred men perished. By some
means Mr. Dowie, though eventually arrested, escaped
the just reward of such an outrage. Williams and Mont-
gomery were hanged for a much smaller edition of
the same action in N.S. Wales. The whole of the
legal machinery of the country seems to have been
paralysed by Dowie. Since his re - appearance, he
has continued to be a disturber of the peace, but, owing,
presumably, to the stronger establishment of the law,
Elijah has not been able to take the ministers of the
Presbyterian, Baptist, Anglican, Roman Catholic and
other rival denominations out to Muddy Creek and wipe
them out as he did at Carmel. But, as a matter of
high public policy, a man who is able to make a corner in
the national rainfall should certainly be expelled. As the
scene of his former lawless doings is now under the juris-
diction of the Sultan of Turkey, he might be handed over
to the police of Abdul the Damned for trial.

Certain sheep have been wandering in
and out of N.S.W. courts for the past two
years or so, and are still wandering ; in fact, they
don’t seem much nearer the final slaughter-yard
than they did at the start.
The trouble began at
Bombala, where they
wandered on to a
selection which was
not properly fenced.
The selector im-
pounded them, and
next morning they
weren’t there.
They were tracked,
so it was said, to
a farmer’s pad-
dock, where they
were identified as
the sheep that had
been in the pound.
Then a local police
sergeant arrested
the farmer ; and
next day the
sheep gob into
the local police-
court, where the
farmer was ac-
quitted. After
that the farmer
wanted compen-
sation, and the
sheep wandered
in and out of the
courts in Sydney
while prelimina-
ries were being
fixed. By March
the mob had
reached Justice Pring
and a jury; and, after
acres of argument as to the
powers of the police to make arrests, tho sergeant
got the verdict. After that the mob turned up
on an application for a new trial, and the same
points were argued all over again, and the new
trial was granted. By this time the sheep had
begun to die of drought and old age; but the
Acting-Chief Justice wrestled with what was left
of them towards the end of last year, and gave a
verdict for the farmer for £350. Last week the
hoary remnants of the flock got as far as the Full
Court, where it was argued by a large number of
lawyers and three Judges that some other Judge
ought to have allowed someone in the past to ask
a question concerning a lock, and the Full Court
said it was probably so, and decided that the
whole business might be gone over again. The
ease will, according to present appearances, be
over by about 1907, by which time what is left of
the sheep that have been causing all the row
will be almost old enough to be killed for prime
lamb.
* * * *
The latest marriage in the British Royal
Family promises to aggravate its consanguineous-
ness in a considerable measure. Bride and bride-
groom are respectively the granddaughter of
Queen Victoria and the son of her first cousin
(Duchess of Teck), and they are, of course, other-
wise related on more distant and more German
lines. Their children, when they arrive, will be
nephews and nieces on the paternal side to the
present Princess of Wales, and first cousins on the
maternal side to the Princess of Wales’ husband —
little York. The King will be their uncle on one
side and their third cousin on the other. Like-
wise His Majesty will be the father of their
father’s brother-in-law and the second cousin of
their aunt. And when these unborn offspring in
due course marry some more cousins, all their
uncles will be nephews to their aunts.

Ex-Gov. Brassey has been “up" in
England iur Uncuig at night without a light. His
son-in-law fined him Is., after formally asking
the policeman if he had been previously con-
victed and if anything was known against him. In
that respect Brassey had more luck than aMenzies
(W.A.) police magistrate did the other day.
Charged with biking on a footpath, the local J.P.
told him that he ought both to know the law and
to obey it, and fined him a crown. But perhaps
the fact that Brassey for once didn’t fall off was
counted in his favor. The day when Brassey
doesn’t fall off is like Christmas—a festival which
covers many sins.
As usual, the peaceful Asiatic has been
making things hum in Melbourne’s Chinatown.
A mangy building down there was occupied by
two secret societies —the Gee Hing up above, and
the Bo Leong downstairs. A squabble alioufc
ownership led to the Bo Leong throwing the Gee
Hing out on its ear and casting its vegetables and
its bamboo after it, but the Gee Hing promptly
returned in force, and threw out the Bo Leong and
its cauliflower and bamboo. There the matter
rests at present. The Gee Hing is in possession,
but it is afraid to go out and charge the usual
exorbitant price for the spling callot for fear the
Bo Leong should take possession in its absence.
And the Bo Leong is getting its customers, so it
is difficult to say if the Gee Hing has done much
good for itself after all.

AT THE BALE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Last night my wife, who dances well,
With me attendant, reigned the queen
Of all the ball—l cannot tell
How many men there may have been.
But one there was who once applied
(Before I gained her willing hand),
To make her heart with his allied,
And claim her bride, you understand ?
She waltzed with him. How often ? Well,
At first I did not seem to see—
I danced myself with Mrs. L ,
And thought she fascinated me.
But, ah, when Lucy floated by
Upon his arm with eyes a-shine,
I somehow thought, I don’t know why,
Her jest was miles ahead of mine.
’Tis true I’d danced with Mrs. L
A dozen times, and whispered low,
But she had ne'er proposed, and—well,
I just marched Lucy home, you know I
N.S.W.
WILL CARTER.
A THOUGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Day by day she said with a sigh
“ In the whole wide world there’s nothing new,”
And as the days went swiftly by
She lingered not with the ones that flew.
Till once, when Love was passing nigh
He threw o’er her his rosy hue ;
And she said no more with a little sigh
« in the whole wide world there’s nothing new.”
Nor ever thought as each day flew,
And each its flag of love unfurled,
That what to her was strange and new
Was the oldest thing in the whole wide world.
Xas.

Last act of the great Melbourne Wren
tote comedy was the discharge by the Col ling wood
bench of Brown, Ryan and Wren, charged with
evicting the police from the w<:od-yard, alleged
to be used for running a tote. For the defence,
it was urged
that the policemen were nothing else than trespassers
on the premises, and that they had no right to remain in
possession, as they did, so long after the warrant had
been executed on November 3. A constable might, it
was argued, as well stay at a public-house to see that no
trading was done after hours.
Which was obvious enough—except to a “cool,
calm, strong Equity lawyer.”

The See Government in N.S.W.—perhaps
urged thereto by the new police inspector-general
rashly enters once more upon a campaign
against the Sunday banana. Against that mild
and squashy fruit the arm of authority has been
lifted again and again, and has prevailed not.
1 here is something Asiatic in the methods of the
Sabbath banana when attacked. It bows its
speckled crest as the legions of the law thunder
by, and then rises again, over-ripe and unabashed,
and sells 12 of itself in a bag for threepence in the
same old way. Henry Parkes was pulled by the
beard and flouted at the hands of this pertinacious
fruit, and that holy cow-merchant, Brunker, bore
the dint of its scorn on his halo. All pr cedent
justifies the forecast that John See will also be
routed in the forthcoming struggle, and the pulpy
horror will continue to be retailed seven days a
week at the old price.
Boccacio’s story of the lady who married a
pious merchant, who religiously kept all the fast
days, and who, to her great satis-
faction, was carried off by a
wicked pirate destitute of all such
holy practices, was, in a dim sort
of way, recalled by a Melbourne
divorce case last week. The
lady, who appealed to a sympa-
thetic Court for a decree nisi,
showed that the first cause of
marital unhappiness was that
respondent, who was a lay
preacher, wanted to pray all
day and the best part of the night.
The prayers lasted for two to
four hours a day, and she ob-
jected. That was the first rift
in the lute, and as the prayerful
husband was a bad-tempered man,
he threatened to shoot her.
When they were parted tempor-
arily, his letters to her were
mostly made up of religious ex-
tracts and pious advice. Justice
Madden recognised that it was
not human nature to stand so
much devotion, and he referred
to respondent as a humbug and a
a scoundrel, and granted a rule.
The divorce court has already
grappled with the problem of
how much ill-treatn.ent eonsti
tutes legal cruelty ; it must now
face the question of how much
prayer constitutes neglect ?

When the Bush veldt Car-
bineers’ case was first discussed
in Australia, General Hutton in-
sisted on the dismissal from
N.S.W. Forces of Major Lene-
han, who had been commander
of the Carbineers. “It was im-
possible for Imperial officers to
serve here otherwise, ” said Hutton
loftily. “Then they can serve elsewhere,” said
Bill Lyne, then acting as Defence Minister. “I
am going to have all the papers in the case before
condemning the man.” So Lenehan was not dis-
missed, and his commission has been in a state of
“suspended animation” since. Now that he is
to be restored to his rank, the attitude taken up
by “ the Imperial officahs ” will be interesting to
note.

There is a
small prohibi-
tion area in Vic-
toria. At Ascot
Vale, a 40 or 50-
acre triangular
block bears the
thirsty name of
“ Temperance Township. ” It is held by the execu-
tors of a deceased temperance man with the wholly
inappropriatenameof Murphy. Murphystipulated
in his will that all pubs were to be kept off the
property, and that no grocer should sell or store
wines or liquor within the prescribed area. The
triangle is bounded by three roads. It contains
four grocery establishments, which get over the
difficulty by keeping their “hard tack ” in a joint
store across the road. Also, opposite each corner
of the triangle, is a large and flourishing pub.
* * * *
Sydney Evening News prints this pretty
picture of what long pursuit of its fiscal faith has
brought N. S. Wales to :
There are in Sydney probably no fewer than 50,000
people living below the waterline of human existence,
whose fare is the minimum that will keep body and soul
together ; who abstain from going to church because
they cannot afford even a humble coin for the plate ;
who cannot write to distant relatives because it costs
twopence for postage ; who, if they want to go to the
seaside, are obliged to walk, otherwise the expenditure
on tram fares would dislocate the affairs of the household
fcr a week, and who rarely have a change of attire ;
whose position, in fact, is worse than that of the meanest
serf of the mediaeval cities, and in whom the spirit of
hopelessness has so developed that never in their wildest
flights of imagination could they indulge the prospect
of becoming the possessor of a spare half-crown weekly.
Melbourne held its usual inquest on a
consumptive the other day. The evidence was to
the effect that a friend had sheltered the man for
about a year, till he became so alarmingly ill that
it was thought best to have him taken to hospital.
Accordingly a constable hustled round, and, as
usual, found not a vacant bed anywhere, so, in the
kindness of his heart, and with the consent of the
patient, he ran in the approaching corpse on a
charge of being ill in Bent’s State, so that he
might get medical treatment in the gaol infirmary.
There is no economy in providing aged wrecks
with hospital space in gaol instead of outside, but
when the wrecks are scruffed into gaol it makes
Bent’s Ministry feel that it is a strong Govern-
ment, and that the mighty arm of the law is
keeping order. The feeling is so strong that the
Bent Cabinet may yet make all the hospitals
gaol hospitals, and arrest the whole of the patients
in one act. It would at least make things uniform
and symmetrical.
The downfall of David Chenhall, the
Sydney solicitor, sentenced last week to two
years gaol, was almost the most complete and
sudden thing in the annals of Gentleman One.
Chenhall was a dreadfully smart young man with
something of a Japanese cast of countenance —at
least he somehow suggested that there had been a
chrysanthemum in the family a few generations
back. He built up a very large business in an
amazingly short time, though it was a scrambling
kind of business, and other Gentlemen One used
to speak disrespectfully about it, and profess that
they wouldn’t be Seen dead in the same law
court with it. Chenhall’s firm never kept
any proper cash-book or ledger, and neither
he nor his mild and apparently harmless
partner seemed to know that such things were
necessary. Accounts seem to have been kept
partly in the firm’s mind, partly on the back of a
disused envelope, and partly in a whirlwind. A
great deal of the money entrusted to the firm was
apparently kept in notes on the window-sill, and
blew away when the window was opened. When
the collapse came the firm simply said that it
“dunno.” No firm ever seemed to have less
definite information about its own business. So
David Chenhall was passed out. Then there was
more trouble, and David Chenhall was passed in.
* * * *
A smart question of infantile Faith was
discussed at Melb. Practice Court last week, and
is threatening to loom larger anon. Three years
ago Ellen Kay, aged two weeks, was committed
by a suburban bench to the care of the Neglected
Children’s Department in Melbourne. The Depart-
ment passed its ward over to the charge of a Pro-
testant foster-mother, who subsequently decided
to adopt the child in place of her own deceased
baby. Previous to this decision, infant Ellen had
been temporarily transferred to the custody of a
Catholic married woman, but she was unwilling
to support Ellen without fee or reward, and so the
State transferred its ward back to the Protestant
adoptress, from whom restitution is now claimed.
Ellen Kay, at three years of age, has no spiritual
convictions that she knows of. Her mother—as
Chief Justice Madden remarked —showed her
regard for religion by “ practically pitching the
child into the gutter, where the State picked it
up." The only evidence forthcoming re the
“ real ” religion of Ellen Kay is in the svvorn in-
formation of one Annie O’Connell, a midwife, who
states that the child was Catholic from birth. The
Neglected Children’s Act, whilst providing that all
wards of State shall be brought up in whatever re-
ligion suits their views and accords with their school
experiences and former home life, makes no pro-
vision for the sectarian classification of babies in
arms whose mothers have deserted them. More-
over, there is no evidence that Annie O’Connell
is an infallible authority as to the faith of the per-
fectly new child she washed in February, 1901.
She may be endeavoring to sneak a born heretic
into the bosom of her own church. The one thing
certain is that a Protestant woman acquired Ellen
Kay from the State gratis, while the rival Catholic
applicant wanted payment. All things con-
sidered, the State should be called upon to explain
why it has issued a writ of habeas corpus against
the estimable matron who took a neglected baby
off its hands. What particular influence has
been working the State in this matter? How did
the State become entitled to allot any particular
faith to an official orphan committed into its care
at the early age of 14 days ? The State will do
well to drop little Ellen Kay before she becomes
too hot to handle.

A Jap Jingo in Sydney D.T.—the Jap
in Australia nowadays puts on as much frill as a
worm in Westminster Abbey :
When the time comes Japan will guide the yellow
whirlwind and direct the yellow storm, and I am prone
to think that certain nations will find it a veritable
sirocco. In that day, not so far distant as may be
imagined by those who have not been through China of
late, Japan will feed fat on territory.
But the Bear is not finished with yet; in fact, he
has only had his front hair disarranged so far.
» » * *
The Victorian Railway Commissioners,
who intend running mortuary trains to Mel-
bourne’s new Necropolis, at Spring Vale, 15 miles
away, next month, have been running special Sun-
day excursions thither lately—with poor results.
Melbourne likes its cemetery full, and sticks to its
love for the old crowded bone-yard. When, about
the century end, the new planting ground begins
to get crowded it will be a veritable Necropolis ;
allowing five bodies per grave its 800 acres will
then contain some four millions of population.
So far about 100 acres have been cleared and
plotted off; paths are being made, and shrubs and
flowers planted, and about a score of departeds
have taken up a permanent residence. The trust
that controls the place seems to be as grasping as
most of its kind—Bft. by 4ft. is allowed for each
grave, but there are hardly any spare plots for
trees, though borders for shrubs are plentiful; and
a cemetery without trees is no more beautiful than
a stone-mason’s yard—and a lot less healthy. The
trustees have put up a dream of a fernery, with
seats whereon bereaved relatives may rest and
ponder; but, take it all round, the institution is
bare and moist and weepful, and when about a
million grave-stones accumulate on its surface it
will look like an immense insurrection cl false
teeth.

THE BARMAID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Oh, beauteous Barmaid with the Yellow Hair !
Your fingers white bear gifts from many lovers,
Or friends, as you would call them, and I dare
Say they were very friendly. One discovers
From these and other jewels, rich and rare,
It pays to be a Barmaid with such Hair.
In sooth you are both beautiful and fair ;
Although, no doubt, a trace of Art is seen,
Almost a Dazzling v ,sion ’neath the glare !
To me you’re fairer far than any queen.
You might have filled my heart with love and car#
Some years ago—you and your Yellow Hair.
It is indeed a very busy night ;
Somewhat retired, I watch the folk that come
To make their spirits and their pockets light,
With copious draughts of brandy, whisky, rum,
Olaret or port, all served them by the fair
And lofty Barmaid with the Yellow Hair.
They call her “ Lottie,” “Tottie,” “ Dolly,” “Dear,”
It really does not matter much, you know ;
She smiles on all impartially, to cheer
Them up, and help to make the money go.
She has her joke for every sinner there,
My Dream of Loveliness with Yellow Hair 1
The sharebroker, the dentist, journalist,
The auctioneer, the betting man, the clerk,
The soldier, and the peaceful pugilist,
At their most valiant task I do remark ;
All trades and all professions she serves there,
My beauteous Barmaid with the Yellow Hair.
Of all the many drinkers I behold,
The men who are most villainous and free
(I much regret to saj' it) are the old !
The young are bad ; but, O ! it sickens me
To see these men with grey, or with no hair
After my pleasant Barmaid dangling there.
But oft she gives to me a pleasant smile,
Thinking of me, 'mid all this thirsty crew.
Now, when she told me she would love me, while
She lived and after, it was surely true ;
Or so I think—it’s doubtful, you may swear,
When it’s the Barmaid with the Yellow Hair.
S.A.
WM. M'DONALD.

A GARDEN OF FLOWERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
At Sunset, when shadows come creeping and falling
On the green of the lawn half-way, and then over,
When the dying sun glints through the treetops, and
tints
Rosy red at our feet the sweet carpet of clover,
Oh, dearest and sweetest but rarest of hours
When I walk by her side in a garden of flowers.
Oh, 'tis well to be you, oh, lily so stately,
Madonna-like, pure in your regal repose,
Just to live for a while in the warmth of her smile,
Then to die —giving place to your rival, the rose.
For you’ve dwelt for a space where each sweet thing
embowers
Her presence itself—in a garden of flowers.
Q.
K. SMYTH.

FACILE PRINCEPS
SEMPER IDEM
BUCHANAN WHISKY.
Japan, it seems, like Russia, has a strong
Socialistic party. Writes a local observer :
A city like Osaka, with millions of spindles, is a plague
centre of public unrest. I believe that war would tje
welcome merely to offset the desire for further changes
in the body politic. Karl Marx and Ferdinand Lassalje
are the idols of the Japanese factory worker, and, when
you couple their teachings with our notorious racial
impulse to experiment with the new and untried, a ope-
eyed man can see the possibility of barricades. In I
country the agrarian element is still soundly Conserva’-
tive, though Socialism is spreading. At present the
working classes have no protection under the law from
the injustice of employers. There is no labor legis-
lation in Japan. The employer is well looked after
legally, but the employee has no statutes to appeal to in
the matters of hours of labor, child labor, the employ-
ment of women, light, ventilation, lavatories, employers’
liability, and the like, which are now commonplaces to
the British workman and workwoman. Mere babes toil
terribly, twelve hours a day, in the big factories, for a
sum that would not buy a single good cigarette in
London. Night and day the work goes on in twelve-
hour shifts, and the degeneration of the unfortunate
factory hand is pitifully fast. The lower classes are
really in a sorry plight in Japan, for the rich are rapidly
growing richer and the poor poorer. The public has
become greedy for scores of articles unknown a genera-
tion ago, and happy, satisfied existence has become
fatally costly, inasmuch as the wages and earning
capacity have increased very little, while desires, if not
actual necessities due to superior education, have
increased tenfold.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 23,1904.
My Dear Arini,—
Mrs. Venables Kirk, Lady Rawson’s sister,
with her daughter and Miss Rawson were in
town for a little while last week. Then they
went on to the Blue Mountains—that region
whioh Mark Twain declared, in the most labored
joke of his life, to be really a heap of rabbits.
Governor Rawson’s cocked hat still hangs on a
tree in Sutton Forest,‘"whilst himself strolls about
in carpet slippers.
A scheme is said to be “in the air ” to turn the
unsightly wool store now disfiguring Kirribilli
Point, North Sydney, into a large and dignified
residential hotel. Kirribilli was once the prettiest
point in Port Jackson, but its symmetry was
ruined by the vast, square, pale pile aforesaid,
which (to the joy of the people on the boats pass-
ing by) has never been much of a commercial
success.
Surely the proudest moment in Japanese Consul
Iwasaki’s life occurred last Saturday night at the
first presentation of the Russian play “Resurrec-
tion.” When he entered the manager’s box at Her
Majesty’s a section of the audience applauded
him. He didn’t “catch on” to the significance
of the glad hands until some better-informed of
his countrymen prompted him, when he arose and
bobbed like a cork on a calm sea. The applause
was started by seven feet of red pressman stand-
ing at the back of the about a
fourth of the d.c. audience joined in. If only the
opposite box had been reserved for a Chinaman,
and he had risen with his bamboo on his shoulder
ahd his vegetable baskets dangling therefrom, to
beam on the audience and typify the good feeling
that prevails between the yellowish empire and
the brownish one, the picture would have been
complete. By Monday last, no doubt, the Fuji-
yama Herald and the Evening Chrysanthemum
and the Daily Geisha had cables, telling how the
great audience at the principal theatre in Sydney
rose when the representative of the Mikado
entered—rose during the progress of a Russian
play, and sang the Japanese National Anthem,
and waved its kimono and its straw sandals till it
was hoarse.
The box with the four Japanese gentlemen was
an interesting objective for the audience. His
Consulship has little English, and the points of
the play must have gone astray on him ; but his
companions, who were evidently flushed with
news of battle, were intensely appreciative,
especially over the jury-room scene, with its
comments about women. When Maud Jeffries,
as Katusha, came on, looking mighty desirable in
her peasant frock and pretty cap with the scarlet
bows below her ears, they must have thought
long brown thoughts about gallant Jap officers
and Muscovite maidens.
“Resurrection”—its name really ought to be
“ Regeneration”—is not such a Doomsday play as
I had imagined from reviews of the book. The
dramatisers have left out much of Tolstoy’s tears,
which are composed of holy water and nitric
acid. If one is out after Fate and Duty,
one will get a fill of both by following
the play from those aspects, and come to the
quite unoriginal conclusion that crime and
punishment often go together. But the majo-
rity of us don’t worry about stodgy, ethical
values at the play. One of the vivid memories I
brought away was of scenery the prisoners’
halting place in Siberia. A silent, soundless
waste of snowy landscapes glimmers green and
ghostly under the moon. A few pine trees, look-
ing as if their very sap was congealed, grow at
lonely distances from one another. They make
you think of Heine’s lines about the solitary pine
(rooted in ice-bound earth) which was in love with,
and always dreaming of, a palm tree that grew in
a far country on a strip of burning sand.
The “submerging” of Miss Jeffries’ appearance
in rags and squalid environment, leaves Miss
Pendennis the beauty of the play. She acts the
fiancee of Prince Dimitri after the Jeffries’ fashion.
But did anyone ever see such extensive hips with
an ingenue ? Is physical culture played out ’
Audience showed symptoms that Persons are
drifting back to town. In the box opposite the
Japanned one—the box where the vegetable John
should have been—were Mrs. Philip Morton and
Miss Irene Marks, with considerable young man
escort. Mrs. John Hay is back from Coolangatta,
and Mr. B. R. Wise has closed his holiday in the
Kosciusko district. All the time of his absence
he was giving opinions, and untying legal knots,
so he must holiday with the other end of a tele-
graph line at his ear. According to Mr. Wise the
scenery of the Alpine range, which he crossed on
horseback, is as fine as that of the Southern Alps
in M.L.—the “land of the jaundiced tussock”
as a recently-returned Australian calls it.
Sydney will curse the Japanese alliance yet—if
it meets with many adventures like one of last
week. Between 1 and 2 a.m. an ancient
person with a voice like the crack of doom rent
the atmosphere of the opulent waterside suburbs
with cries of “Extry speshuU tremenjous
Japanese victory.” I forget whether the Chrysan-
themums had taken Moscow or St. Petersburg, or
both. Anyhow the Points poured out in their
pyjamas and bought the papers at 3d; each, and
there was nothing at all—not even the news that
Admiral Togo’s dog had barked at Admiral
Alexieff’s dog in Corea. If the old man had been
overtaken one of the great battles of the war
would have been fought on the cable-tram line,
but he got away before the pursuing forces were
dressed.
Five thousand pounds was given to Sydney
Children’s Hospital last week by an anonymous
donor. Some persons make good use of their
wealth. If this good giver has still a few pounds
left, he might bestow them on the Women’s
Hospital, which has to scratch hard for a living.
Curious thing how often prayerful people-
people in whom the love of praying almost
amounts to a mania—are very mean in money
matters. That recent divorce case in Melbourne,
in Which a wife brought the action, is a case
in point. Respondent had prayers four
times a day, and when in absence he
wrote to his wife, his letters' were mostly
extracts from sermons. But he didn’t send
her any money. I know a “ religious ” man
who left his wife and daughter without means
whilst he went on an eigfiV inonths tour to
“ furrin ” parts. But he wrote, from every port,
letters inculcating the love of God and fear of
Sheol, and eloquently descriptive of the various
churches in which he had prayed—for them. In
the seventh month of his absence he sent his wife
£2 ; and on his return, after he had offered up a
long prayer of thanks for his restoration to the
bosom of his family, he asked her what money
she had in hand. Also, I have heard of another
well-to-do man who, whenever his wife asked him
for money, used to back her against the wall and
ask her in dreadful tones of reproach what she
had done with the 9d. he gave her last Tuesday
fortnight—but he wasn’t religious, so this is a
digression.
Mr. Hugh Dixson, jun., who was associated
with his father in their big tobacco concern, died
at a private hospital last week. His widow, who
was one of Sydney’s prettiest married women, is
in England. The home of Dixson, sen., “ Aber-
geldie,” at Summer Hill, has some of the finest
gardens and conservatories to be seen around
Sydney.
Mr. Arthur Pratt’s concert, in aid of the Q.V.
Consumptive Homes and Fresh-Air League,
occurs next Saturday, at Sydney Town Hall.
The Test match will be on during the afternoon,
and the Test audience couldn’t do better than
adjourn afterwards to the charity concert.
Madame Slapoffski will make her last Australian
appearance (for a long time) at the entertainment.
A correspondent draws my attention to the
fact that in naming the principal wars of the last
hundred years the loser has always had the doubt-
ful honor of being mentioned first. Franco-
Prussian, Spanish-American, Chino-Japanese,
the Greco-Turkish, and even when but one
name is mentioned the coincidence holds good,
as Crimean, Indian, Soudan. Now, there’s the
Russo-Jap war, which arrives in time to be the
exception and so prove the rule. There was also
the Russo-Turkish war which helps to prove that
the rule doesn’t apply to the Bear.
By the Sonoma, last Monday, Edward Farley,
the operatic basso, left for America and work.
His steamer-ticket and a purse of 65 sovs. were
among his presents. An engineer son of Mr.
Justice Inglis Clark, of Tasmania, also went
Statue-of-Liberty-wards to learn somewhat of
Carnegieland. Minister of Lands P. Crick, whose
large silk, tie was of a most beautiful blue—the
tint to suit a baby girl—went on board looking
already sea-sick. He goes as far as M.L.
. Average age for matrimony as proved by statis-
tics is rising so steadily that widowed grand and
great-grand mothers and fathers of the future will
not be ineligibles for matrimony. P’raps—p’raps
not. Between the years ’76 and ’BO the number
of men in Britain who married whilst under age
was 77"8 out of every 1000 marriages, and of girl-
wives under 20 there were 217 per 1000. But since
then the number has been dropping like a plum-
met. A girl who goes to numbers of weddings,
tells me that. she is in an almost chronic state of
astonishment at the—well, the very mature
appearance of the brides. She thinks that this
“appearance” is increased by the uncompromising
bridal robes and blossoms, and the veil which does
not veil the face at all. After 30, complexion is a
rare grace. Amongst the very poor and the very
wealthy, under-age marriages are still numerous;
it is in the middle classes that marriage is being
postponed until the limited income is on the way
to surplusage. The new order of things has its
drawbacks, of course. Something of the “ne’er-
will-come-again-the-hour-of-glory-on-the-leaf-and-
rapture-on-the-flower ” feeling of regret must
attend the sedate lovers.
Private letters from London bring news of the
marriage of an Australian woman who once pro-
mised to be a singer of note, or, through marriage,
a society somebodj r . I never saw her, but I’m
told she was very beautiful and virtuous, and
assumed more frills than a river hat. Her voice
went early, and her beauty waned, and fora living
she took to rearing some small breed of ponies in
an out-of-the-way Irish town. She is very abusive,
in her letters, of Fate for not fetching along
her husband when she wasyoungerand more gifted
and pretty. He is just the sort for which she was
then on the look-out. Forty (she has three years
more), rich, clever, handsome, with ever so many
quarterings on his shield. Now she writes: “I
had learned to do without romance, and he doesn’t
like small ponies. Providence is inscrutable.”
Gregan MacMahon, who plays leading young
man in the Hawtrey Co., is a Sydney Uni.
graduate. When well into his studies for the
bar, lie scared his friends by renouncing law
for stage. He thereby took a big risk, seeing
that he had very little means, a child of about
the same dimensions, and a nice-sized wife. He
has panned out a capital comedy actor, Mabel
Lane, who re-entered on Saturday at the Criterion
with the Haw. Co., exhibits some orchid-fire frocks.
One is a pale primrose pink with stole ends
and lace and plenty of soft swirl effects. The
stage hour at which she wears it is 7 a.m., and as
the frock is far too fine as a dressing-gown for
anyone who doesn’t own the Kremlin, one is re-
lieved to find that it was put on at 7 p.m., and that
Mabel, who play 3 the part of wife of a gay
Lothario, has been up all night in her sunbeam
tea-gown waiting for her night-owl hubby.
The Haw. Co. women are all very well dressed,
but if you want to see a gown that looks as if it
spelt Intrigue, that fairly prints the word Danger
{to the male person), you will see it in the red
robing of Miss Mary Bancroft, who plays the
Lady of Ostend. There is Device in every
acoordeon-pleated flounce, and Flirt in the big red
hat, and Mercenary Character in the red velvet
florins of the transparent yoke. Leonard Davis,
who was treasurer in the man-of-war Macdonald
tour, pilots the co. and keeps the curl in his hair.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The Linlithgow (formerly Hopetoun) writes
to Australia, privately, that he has now re-
nounced public work, and ‘means to find his
happiness in his own home with his wife and
family.’ Mamma and he are delighted at their
new baby (a daughter), and Hopetoun also men-
tions gratefully that he himself is now ‘ able to
enjoy his meals and a glass of wine.’ Local
sentimentahsts, commenting on this epistle, lavish
pity upon the writer, baffled in his ambitions
through weak health. It is disappointing,
undoubtedly. Still, I prefer to cast my compas-
sion upon that other sickly man (lots of him,
hereabouts), who, ill or well, is debarred from
‘renouncing’ his graft, because to do so would
entailj renouncing ’ at the same time home, wife,
family; new baby and meals. The ordinary
toiler, laid aside from work and pay, sees no
‘ happiness ’ ahead of him —unless he finds it in
the grave.. <
“ There is to be a. formal beginning of the much
talked of ‘ Henley on the Yarra,’early in March.
The Northcote Excellencies and suite and a
swagger afternoon-tea are promised for the
occasion. Somehow the theatre ground of so
much sordid suicide and floating doggie strikes
one as rather an incongruous course for a fashion-
able boat race, but a determined set of English-
minded Johnnies have frozen on to the notion of
a Henley fete and they mean to have it.
“ Ex-Premier Irvine’s friends smile, sub rosa, at
the accounts of his ill-health. One woman gives
a genuine smile above board, and that is Mrs.
Irvine, for she gathers the sick (?) man up and
takes him on a voyage of some length. No poli-
tician—not even Bigjohn Forrest —has a more de-
votedly interested wife than Irvine. She stood by
him all the time of the railway strike, and had to
be carried out in a dead faint by Treasurer Shiels
(himself on the sick list) at the crisis of the move-
ment. Mrs. Irvine always had a gallery seat
when hubby spoke, cheering him up and bearing
him off for a good meal and a pick-me-up after.
William alwayshas been apampered and spoilt per-
son. Probably what he most suffers from is a sorry
neglect of the spanking department when he was
infantile. A neglect of that sort can only be re-
paired, later in life, by a wife of the sub-acid
order; and Irvine struck a particularly sweet and
petting sort of mate.
“News comes that Clara Butt—the Juno with
the mighty organ-notes—proposes to try her
cavernous rumble on Australian audiences. A
baritone, Andrew Black, scouts ahead first.
Admirers say he has great art, a fruity warble,
and the bump of reverence so largely developed
that his singing in oratorio always provokes
tears, and sends hearers groping for a thripenny
as if they were in church.
