squired by their smart chums, do the Nudoscopes
of the city—those penny in the slot affairs which
show girls mostly undressed or undressing. It is
called ‘ doing the peepers ’ !
“Premier Bent, as he waddles down Collins-
street, brolly in hand, and the corner of a grin
breaking out of his stubby whiskers, reminds one
of the smug philanthropist in Bleak House—the
flabby gentleman with a moist surface. Just now
Tommy looks extra moist with gratification. His
moistness seems to gleam and twinkle and say—
‘ What do you say to Tommy now ? ’

mixed couples. There is a movement on foot
to ‘reciprocate’ with the Empire of London,
so that civilities may be the more equally
exchanged, and touring members be accommo-
dated at one or t’other.
“About the most fetching of ‘Country Girl’
chorus girls is a sister of barrister Billy Williams.
She dresses up her brunette looks with rose-red
ribbons and bright dressy etceteras, and is so
inexperienced that she blushes the same rose hue
when the facetious Barry calls her out by the
name of Eugenie Duggan and presses a warm
motherly kiss on her blooming cheek. The chorus
battler who can blush atfa stage peck deserves to
come under the chaste notice of Elijah, junior—•
the unkissed freak of the century.
“ Rev. George Robinson has secured a fine
residence in one of the most salubrious suburbs of
Sydney for a first prize in a big art-union which
he is engineering. With the house and grounds
goes a first-class return trip to Europe. The
enterprising priest hopes to clear over £lO,OOO by
the bazaar and art-union.
“A jolly actress who agreed to an economical
trip to England with her jolly spouse on a cheap
liner, writes in a bitter vein of the nerve-racking
time aboard. She sends word that she let loose
on her unfortunate partner all the annoyances,
regrets, disappointments, and trials of her whole
matrimonial career, and said all the unpleasant
things she had ever been aggravated into think-
ing, but had suppressed under more cheerful cir-
cumstances. Cheap travelling was never meant
for the luxurious. It is all right as a Lenten
exercise for the sackcloth devotees, or the neces-
sitous. If a man wants to know his wife’s honest
opinion of him, let him take her on a sea voyage
in a low-priced steamer.
“A Melbourne erstwhile cabman named Nimmo
is wanted to pick up £lOB,OOO, left by his father
in England. ‘Cabby’ is known to have driven a
vehicle between Sandridge (Port Melbourne) and
the city. Long years ago, one John Nimmo re-
presented Emerald Hill in the Victorian Legisla-
tive Assembly. He was Minister for Works about
1886, and a dredge was named after him. This
Nimmo went back to Scotland some years ago,
after the ‘ boom ’ break, and is said to have
opened a small shop. Possibly he is a relative of
the lost cabman.
“ Elijah Dowie brings his ‘ healing ’ fakes to an
overstocked market here. There are already enough
faith-healers, clairvoyants, and self-styled ‘pro-
phets ’ on hand to cater for the class of invalid
who likes to be doctored through its imagination.
Last ‘ seance ’ I attended was conducted by a
cheeky little Yank, who rummaged out alleged
diseases from the anatomical fastnesses of his
hearers and then passed on the patients to his
‘doctor’ colleague. Fat Mrs. Fool, in the
audience, would place on his table for ‘ test ’ a
kid glove, several sizes too small for her. Yank
watched her place the glove there, so he knew to
whom it belonged, When its turn came to be
identified, he picked it up and said : ‘ This belongs
to a female with small hands and feet.’ (Murmur
of surprised assent from owner.) Then the
schemer pretended to forget that he had already
said the owner was a female, and went on, in a
dreamy way : ‘ This glove—paw-bag, we call it in
my country (laughter)—belongs to someone fuller
here (patting his own chest) than I am, and
dressed different somehaow—l can’t see clear
yet, but someone seems to be whispering to me
that there’s a yaller waist and a black skirt
in the environment of this article. Seems to
me _ this person hollers in her sleep—am
I right ? Think again. Don’t split hairs
with me and I waont with you ! Don’t holler in
your sleep? Something seems to tell me of
giddy-head. Ami right? I thought so.’ Best
of it was, the plausible mannikin posed as a saint,
boasted that he could make fortunes if he would
only give racing-tips and information re wills.
‘ But if he couldn’t make clean money, he’d ruther
make none. ’!
“ A moneyed widow who, for a conspicuous
period, wore the garb of a golden sorrow, has now
launched out into a dozen charitable schemes,
opened house in a swagger quarter, and bounced
into all the gay movements afloat. She lets the
world know plainly that she is taking notice
again.
“ One of the few flower-paintings in the Fenton
collection is a dark Egyptian bloom, the work of
Mrs. Ellis Rowan, who, by the way, has been
telling some interesting incidents in her career.
Once she spent some months on those pin-prick
islands shown on the map between Papua
and Australia. The diet had plenty of local
color, for it consisted solely of sea-cow, yam, and
turtle. Another time she led an expedition to
the Chillagoe caves. She has pierced the very
heart of Dominica via a wild bridle-path, to see
the last of the Caribs, and plied her brush on the
sides of Mt. Pelee just before it burst. She has
seen the midnight sun, and weird sights beyond
the Himalayas; has painted colossal flowers
whose centres held the bodies of rats and
millions of insects. In short, she has seem an
interviewer.

ing signs of wear. It flashed on a full house last
Saturday and was recalled three times.

healthful domestic industry to Toorak ?

NO APPETITE.
Every person who starts taking Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills finds that their first effect is to sharpen the
appetite. The secret of this is simple. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People actually make new blood—
and this fills and tickles the tiny veins in the stomach-walls. This produces that uneasy inner craving which
people call Appetite. And so, no matter for what blood disease you may take Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, their
first result will be to make you long for meal-time and to eat heartily. But, of course, Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills do more than that. With new pure rich red blood, they stimulate the flow of gastric juices, give the
stomach strength for digestion, steady the heart, brace up the liver, tone the nerves, stiffen the spine, and
put the whole system in the pink of condition. This is exactly what they did for Mr. John Kemp,
Lily-street, Hurstville, Sydney.
“ Until four years ago, I never knew what it was to
have a day’s sickness,” says Mr. Kemp, “but about this
time I began to be troubled with nast.v sick headaches.
Nearly every morning I woke with a dull throbbing ache
in my temples, and a horrible bitter taste in my mouth. I
lost all appetite—the very thought of food made me sick.
Often I felt too weak and languid to drag through the day.
It was nothing new for me to have to knock off work for
days. And my back was very bad, too. Sometimes I
could hardly get out of bed owing to the awful stabbing
pain that caught me right above the hips, as soon as I tried
to get up. I got dreadfully thin—my coat hung on me like
a sack. For two years I felt the most wretched man alive.
No medicine did me any good till I bought some Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People from Pattinson’s,
chemists, George-stteet. One box did me so much good
that I kept right-on with the pills until I had finished 10
boxes. Then I was as right as ever. The headaches, back-
aches, and nasty sick feeling left me, and they have never
returned since. It’s two years now since my cure, so I
know my health has been built, up for good—thanks alone
to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.”
Medical Advice given Free.
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydneyy-N.fi. W. Get the Genuine, 2s. 9d. a box.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Bent, whom Victoria has for its sins as a
Premier, has a brother, John Bent, who is Mayor
of Hay (N.S.W.).
The German Kaiser, long time the most-
talked-of person in Europe, is now the subject of
an ominous silence. Cancer ?
Of the descendants of late Justice Win-
deyer there are practising the law in N.S.W.
three as solicitors and one as a barrister.
Devon and Exeter Gazette (Eng.), Dec.
18, remarks that Lord Northeote is well fitted by
his knowledge and tact “to deal with the Labor
Party ! ”
The late Dean Cow per was a Sydney
native, yet, from force of habit, the Cathedral
chapter sent to England for a tablet to be erected
in St. Andrew’s to bis memory.
The Owe’Sullivau lias publicly denied that
he aspires to the presidency of the Water and
Sewerage Board. He will, he says, stick to politics
as long as politics stick to him.
Several of G. H. Reid’s followers describe
his influence over them as like that of an elderly
female relative. In the old days Dibbs dubbed
the monocled fat man “Charley’s Aunt.”
Names of those who subscribe to the
Irvine testimonial fund are to be kept secret. Is
it that Mr. Irvine is ashamed of his benefactors,
or are subscribers ashamed of their benefactions ?
Latest about our brown friend, Johore of
the glaring teeth, is that he will settle down in
England : it seems to be certain that his
“dominions” are to be formally annexed by
Britain.
Poor old Graham Berry left but £460 for
his widow. It is not too late for the Victorian
Parliament to make some provision for her now,
but with Thomas Bent as Premier such a thing is
not likely to be even thought of.
Archbishop Smith (Sydney) has evidently
been studying Mr. John Snorting’s style. His
Grease advertises a Special Series of Lenten dis-
courses on “Sin, its Reality, its Remedy; its
Resistance and its Relinquishment.”
J. E. Mackey, who gets a place in the Vic.
Bent Government as an hon. Minister, is a bar-
rister. He started life as a compositor, and is
reckoned the most saturated Herbert Spencerian
in Victoria. Also he began political life as a
Libera] and land-taxer, and threatens to become a
land-tax hating Tory.
A wandering Australian brings back word
that Ernest Govett, who drudged for years as
sub-editor of Adelaide Advertiser, next part-
owned and edited the defunct Silver Age at
Broken Hill, and afterwards went in for more
profitable mine-promoting and directing, has set
up now in one of the finest villas in Florence, Italy,
and as an art collector has developed a remarkable
nose for bargains,
THE FIRM MAN’S RETREAT.
“Mr. Irvine has arranged to leave for a trip to England
for the benefit of his health.”— Daily Paper.
IRVINE: “Strikes me this is a good place to get
out of.”
BENT: " D-! who’s afraid! ”
Eady, the tall Tasmanian cricketer, has
the strange distinction of being brother-in-law to
his own father-in-law. He married the daughter
of the handsome middle-aged Hobartian who took
his (Eady’s) sister for his third wife.
Recently deceased veteran Victorian jour-
nalist Reuben Quarril was, as a boy, a very popu-
lar public soprano singer in Great Britain. He
served on the Melbourne press in the middle
sixties, and succeeded the late Graham Berry as
editor of Geelong Advertiser.
John Gavan Duffy, of Melbourne, has not
had a birthday for eight years, but he will this
month. J.G.D. was born in Dublin on February
29, 1844, the year in which his father (with Daniel
O’Connell and seven others) stood his trial for
some alleged political offence—lack of applause
for the viceroy at the Castle or something of the
sort.
Lord Monk Bretton (whose real name is
Dodson) has come to Australia for a week or two
“ to ascertain the public feeling in the Common-
wealth with regard to preferential trade.” The
man who thinks he can gather the opinions of
4,000,000 people scattered over 4,000,000 square
miles, in a week or two, must imagine himself a
dredge or an octopus.
An American description of Elijah Dowie :
“A short, grandmotherly tub of a man, with the
shoulders of a piano-mover, the corporation of a
London alderman, the legs of a jockey, the head
of a philosopher, the accent of a scholar, the
authority of a general, and the emotional versa-
tility of an actor, with a facility of shedding tears
that Clara Morris might have envied.”
Died at Brisbane, Walter Hill, botanist
and ex-curator of the Botanic Gardens there.
Hill, many years back, introduced the dreaded
si do retusa into Queensland in the hope of found-
ing a gigantic hemp industry. The project failed,
although there seems to be no reason why it should
have done so—experiments in the direction of
starting the industry on a commercial basis were
made quite lately up Mackay way. The weed is
now one of the scourges of the man on the land
up North. Hill died in the firm belief that, given
the capital, there was a big fortune in his idea.
THAT CORDIAL RECEPTION.
“I would he an ingrate indeed if I had already forgotten the cordiality of my reception on reaching your
shores.”— Gov.-General Northeote in a recent little speech.
Well, it’s some comfort to know that His Excellency is easily pleased.
Iceberg Irvine gets out of the indecency
of accepting a monetary reward from his Tory
friends of Flinders-lane and Queen-street by
allowing his wife to take the money. A big effort
is being made to raise the sum to £2OOO. Irvine
declines to say whether or not he is going to con-
test a seat in the new Parliament. As he has
booked his passage to England and intends to be
absent for six months, the chances are that poor
Victoria will have to rub along somehow ©r other
without its great, strong, cool man.
Dowie was badly “stage-managed’* in
Sydney. He was not strong enough to appear as
a star on “ bare boards, ” and there should have
been some pageant or some stirring music to lift
the show along. The addresses or discourses
should have been written for the maudlin old
man, and he should have been taught a few tricks
in stage deportment. The worst blunder of all
was to allow John Alexander to “go on” in
evening dress. In his robes he passed as a pro-
phet of some sort, but in evening dress he looked
like a combination caricature of Dan O’Connor
and Paul Kruger.

Another case of the creaking door hanging
long. The valetudinarian ex-Judge Bundey is
now the sole survivor of Blyth’s “ Progressive ”
S.A. Ministry of 73-5. Blyth, Mann, Glyde,
Hawker, Everard, and Bray passed out years ago ;
Bright, the Works Minister, has just gone;
Bundey, the man who was always sick, remains—
with fewer enemies and more friends, perhaps,
than any other South Australian.
“A.M.”: The vehement temper of a
certain much-advertised preacher is not a recent
development. People who knew him in the long
ago tell a story of how he once invited a fellow
parson to dine with him, in order to discuss a new
paper which he intended to start. Their opinions
on the editing differed, and a heated argument
ensued, which ended in the reverend host hurling
a dinner plate at his visitor’s head.
A political career in Australia does not
seem to be a particularly healthy one. In
Victoria just now two members of the Ministry
have retired for more or less genuine health
reasons; in N.S.W. two members of the See
Ministry are said to be on the point of giving up
politics by medical orders ; and what a number of
M’s.P. one sees sadly sipping milk and repentance
water at lunch !
Tam See, a leading Chinese merchant of
Gerald ton (Q.), offered the other day to indenture
1000 laborers (Chinese) from the Northern Terri-
tory to take the place of Kanakas in the canefields
as the Polynesians disappear by deportation.
Tam See was one of those Northern Chows who
once sent in a petition to Premier Philp asserting
the Chinese were “the backbone of Cairns.”
For years the late William Hart, of the
Tasmanian House of Dodery, enjoyed the rare
distinction and profit of being chairman of
directors of the State’s three greatest mines, of
tin, silver, and gold—the Bischoff, Western, and
Tasmanian. But he missed the Lyell boom,
though there wasn’t much else going in the Speck’s
financial way that he didn’t have a finger in.
Jack Maslin, one time partner with Barr
Smith at Hummocks (S. A.), is said to have passed
practically all his cash over to his
sons, and kept only £50,000 for
himself. Even £50,000 is too much
for simple Jack Maslin, who talks
broad Cousin Jack, and walks
round with his hands in his pockets
like Dan Moran in “Robbery
Under Arms.” In cold weather
he wears three coats of varying
newness—worst out and best in-
side. Used to, feel flabbergasted
when he came to town to see Barr
Smith, whom he regarded as
“flash.” He knows everyone in
the districts where he made his
money, and everyone knows him,
and he is probably treated more
familiarly than any other man of
his wealth in existence.
Melbourne suburbs boast of
“councillors” named Coon, A.
Voice and Bluefitt. Councillor
A. Voice, by the way, is not the person who
makes so many interjections at public meetings.
Dr. R. M. Sly, of Sydney, a man of very
few inches, has just “taken silk,’’and ranks as
K.C. next after Mr. John Armstrong, commonly
known as “Jumbo” and sometimes as “The
Elephant.” The position has some elements of
safety about it. If the newest K.C. had ranked
immediately before “Jumbo” that personage
might have stepped on him unconsciously. Now
he is quite safe, unless “Jumbo” sits down sud-
denly.
Melb. Argus has suddenly found out that
business at the Law Courts is in a dreadfully
congested condition. The pressure is due, as a
matter of fact, to the unusual coincidence of
several long cases. The Argus, however, paints
the congestion as chronic, and alleges that there
is an obvious remedy. The old lady then points
a bony finger at Irvine, who, the Tories reckon,
deserves some reward for his reactionary efforts.
An English magazine points out that three
Premiers of New South Wales were poets—Henry
Parlies, William Forster, and W. C. Wentworth.
The date when Wentworth was Premier of N.S.W.
has not yet been fixed.
Canon Smith, whose widow died last week
at Petersham (N.S.W.), was incumbent at St.
Barnabas’, George-street, Sydney (the ancient-
looking building between two undertakers’ shops),
and built at least a portion of the structure by a
racing gamble. The Canon was an even more
inveterate beggar than the average for “ my poor
church.” One day, meeting Governor Hercules
Robinson, he pleaded for his partially built struc-
ture, alluding to the then rumor that the Governor
had pltmged on a coming event. “It is true,”
said the Governor, “ I’ve backed the double at
Raudwick, and if I win you'll have 5 per cent, of
the pot for your church, and God bless you!”
“ Amen ! ” cried the Canon. The Governor won,
and the ill-gotten money is invested in St. Bar-
nabas’.
The shockingly wasted figure of C. D.
O’Halloran passed out of Melbourne life last week
after a three years struggle with Bright’s disease.
In his jolly-and-fat days O’Halloran owned race-
horses, and was thought to pull a good many
strings at Caulfield and the minor Melbourne
meetings. He was a steward of the V.A.T.C.
when he sued H. H. Champion for libelling him
in the Champion newspaper. Plaintiff scored a
record in criminal proceedings for libel. He
prosecuted his suit until Champion’s allegations
of dishonesty had been widely advertised and in-
directly proved, and then he (O H.) backed out of
the mess, claiming that his honor was fully vindi-
cated. O’Halloran belonged to the hearty, happy,
popular class of “sports” who persuade them-
selves that roguery is “ tactics ” and straight
dealing impossible on the turf. Such men
as he are the deadly enemies of straight dealing.
With their jovial remarks and genial winks they
demoralise their more honest-minded associates.
About a recently dead R.C. bishop :
Xmas Day of ’B4, accompanied a friend at Arniidale to
11 o’clock Mass in the K.C. church. Just at a moment
when all heads were bowed a man, wearing a long over-
coat and holding a bunch of greenery in his hand, walked
up the aisle of the cathedral and opened the gates of the
sanctuary. The bishop was kneeling at the foot of the
altar, his back to the people, and knew nothing of the
man’s approach. A young girl in the front pew was the
first to detect the reason for the coat, so remarkable that
warm day. Through a rent in the side she perceived a
big cavalry sword, sharpened back and front, the hilt of
which the man held in his covered hand with the bunch
of leaves. A scream escaped her. The man walked
quickly to the sanctuary, and, flourishing the weapon
about his head, struck the bishop with the flat, and loudly
demanded the key of heaven. The congregation rose in
confusion with hoarse shoutings of the men, and
piercing shrieks of the women. Dozens of men leaped
the sanctuary rails and made for the assassin, pluckily
enough, for he confronted them with a revolver in his
left hand and was fiercely waving the sword with his
right. He then fired point blank at the bishop, the bullet
going through his vestments. Then he made a grand
defence with his sword, retreating to the sacristy before
he was pinioned. A good deal of blood was shed.
The comic element was not wanting from the scene
A big Irish woman, Julia by name, heaped a storm of
invective upon the pinioned man, belaboring him with
her gingham, and hitting with equal fervor at the police-
men who sought to prevent her. The bishop resumed
the service without a word.

More Smith literature :
Died recently, in a Sydney suburb, Mrs. M. S. Smith,
aged 75 years. The deceased was the widow of Canon
Smith, of All Saints’ Cathedral, Bathurst, a church that
somehow has always been quarrelling with its Bishop.
Canon Smith used to admit that his early life in London,
where it is said he kept an East-end pub., was of a
particularly “ rorty ” character. He came to Sydney in
1857, and was ordained in 1858 for “ Blackwattle Swamp ”
parish, a neighborhood that smelt strongly in every
sense. He built St. Barnabas’ church and schools. In
1873 he went, to Bathurst but failed, after many efforts,
to agree with Bishop Marsden. Then he retirad from
church work and lived at Manly for some years. Dr.
Marriott succeeded him, and his quarrels with Bishop
Camidge are public property. Apparently the microbe
of dissension and cantankerousness was buried in the
foundations of the old Bathurst church.

Aboriginalities.
During a bush fire in Bingara (N.S.W.)
district, the other day, Bangheet station hayshed
caught alight, and when men reached thescene it
was found impossible to get near enough to
release two sheep dogs that were tied up there.
It was decided to shoot them to prevent them
being burnt alive. One was shot dead, but the
bullet intended for the other just grazed its face,
severed the collar, and thus released the animal.
Many clever things are done by accident ; and
probably in his old age the man who fired that
shot will contribute to the world’s miseries by
telling the “boys ’’ how he intentionally saved his
pup’s life, and by lamenting that the good old
days, when a man who could shoot through his
dog s collar at 100 yards was quite an ordinary
thing, are gone for ever.

“Ku Klux”: Regarding artists. Have
seen one drawing in an English magazine where
a milking-shed was portrayed and every milker
sat on the wrong side of the cow. I have in my
possession a first copy of the Commonwealth
Annual. An illustration of one of Will Ogilvie’s
poems depicts a young girl resting against a three-
rail fence with the left elbow on one post and the
right hand on another. What length rails in that
fence ?
* * * *
V Ed.” : Three miles out of Moe (Vic.) is
a hill over which winds the road to Morwell. The
hill, a stringy-bark ridge, is called “haunted.”
No animals or birds are ever found on it; cattle
camped in the vicinity invariably break away and
horses are always uneasy in the vicinity. A dull
rumbling is often heard on calm nights. The
rumbling is presumably the ghosts. I never met
a ghost that didn’t rumble.
“Vinco ” : The kooka-
burra (laughing jackass) is
almost extinct in Central
Queensland. During
the drought thousands
were mercilessly killed
by mutton birds, which,
as soon as they sighted
a jackass, flew round
it in a swarm and
pecked it to death in
mid-air. I havenoticed
that even the distant
laugh of the bird at-
tracts the muttoners,
and they fly off
speedily to put it to
death. Directly the
kookaburra falls the
flock leave it and
hasten after its mate,
for the jackasses gener-
ally fly in pairs. I have
seen 500 mutton birds
shot in one day at M‘Gee’s
cornfield near Yeppoon, and
during the season mutton
birds may be seen hanging
dead for miles along the wire
fences of Perron’s orchard,
M‘Gee s selection, and Adelaide
Park station. When the starving sheep
were on the coast, one no sooner
stumbled than his eyes were picked
out with lightning swiftness by the
mutton birds. The mutton bird is
black and vvhite—more black than
white—and is well known to all bush-
nien. It is altogether different from
the magpie or the butcher bird, though T
have often heard people say they are identical.
“ Snell ” : Be the sheep-like nature of the
sheep, I am reminded of an exhibition of cussed-
ness made by a flock in
Sturt-street, Ballarat,
in the days of my early
youth. The mob was
being driven towards
the east, and at one
point spread upon the
path. There a pugi-
listic ewe suddenly be-
trayed a dangerous re-
sentment towards her
reflection in the win-
dow of a large pastry-
cookery, and, backing
off to get impetus, she
led with her left, and
went straight through
the plate glass and in
amongst the buns. The
second ewe had no in-
terest in the quarrel,
but having every con-
fidence in the other she
followed suit, ditto the
third. And now the
whole idiotic flock be-
came possessed of an
inexplicable craving to butt and flounder
through the ruins of the pastrycook’s win-
dow, mingle with his perishable stock, and
join the flock rapidly accumulating in the
shop. Sheep poured into the establishment, and
made a holy mess of fragile fittings, and trod
pounds’ worth of buns, tarts, seed cake, jellies and
etceteras into apfcfmrny mass on the floor. The
invasion only ceased when the place was jamb full
of sheep, and placid ewes stood two deep all over
the shop, and the drovers who went to rescue their
mutton found the proprietor, a German, tree’d on
his counter, tearing his scant hair.
* * * sjj
A mob of 14,000 wethers, from Manuka
(Q.), have just been delivered at Killar-
ney Station, Narrabri (N.S.W.), by drover
Harrison, without the loss of a single head. The
trip of 900 miles occupied six months, an average
of 6| miles a day (half-mile over the regulation).
Harrison had with him six shepherds, and though
many difficulties were met with in the early
stages, such as want of water, prevalence of burrs,
long grass, scrub, and grass seeds, the mob was
delivered in the “best order and condition.”
How is that for a record clean trip ?

At the Vic. Government’s experimental
tobacco-farm, Hedi, where, so far, little progress
has been noted, an attempt is being made to raise
a few sample-plants for cigar leaf. The plots
include Comstock Spanish, Sumatra, and Con-
necticut Seed Leaf, which are grown under cheese-
cloth, and are at a serious disadvantage in being
grown in heavy soil. The result of this experi-
ment (under the supervision of the locally-raised
expert, Temple Smith) is being watched for with
interest by “ weed ’’-growers throughout the State

“ Nymsie ” : Can any Bulletin reader say
when the first Chinaman landed in Australia, or
if that is too large an order, in N.S.W. or Vic. ?
An old Eureka Stockader tells me that in ’55, a
year after the riots, he was sinking a shaft’at
Ballarat when he broke into a drive where there
were six dead Chinamen, laid away in their clothes
between sheets of bark, and quite fresh by reason
of the current of cool air passing from one old
drive to another .*

“ S.B. ’ : In this part (Swamp Oak, Tam-
worth district), caterpillars are killing the forest
before them. There are millions of them, and I
know they are at least 12 miles N. by S. and six
miles E. by W., and goodness knows how much
farther either way. They have a decided prefer-
ence for stringybark ; then peppermint and apple
trees ; then gum. They seem to fight shy of iron-
bark, nor do they appear to relish too well white
box. Old bush hands have never seen them
before. A few years ago the large, long ladybirds
went along in millions and killed thousands of
acres of timber along Nowendoc. They were
named Laurie's ringbarkers, as they killed an
immense tract of timber ou Laurie’s Nowendoc
Station.

“Ne’er-do-Weel ” writes :
Re “ Alba’s ” picturesque fiction (B. 23/7/’O3). Bid not
know that Hindus can die to order, but I do know' that
West Indian blacks can. Have seen a strapping young
fellow, apparently in the best of health, suddenly de-
termine to give up the ghost and announce that he is
about to do go, because some infernal old scallywag of a
West African has stuck a broken ginger-beer bottle with
a few mangy feathers in it and some fragments of
crockeiy on his bed. He has “put fetish ”on the man
about to die, who will thereupon go to the hospital and
be refused admittance as being perfeetlv sound. The man
will, however, commence to fall away then and there, and
will return to the hospital daily until admitted, and
will actually die in an incredibly short space of time. A
post-mortem will reveal no diseased organs—nothing, in
fact, to cause death. I have asked such a one why
he did not consult an Obi man and have the fetish re-
moved or counteracted, but have always been told it was
impossible,—Obi man not strong enough for West African
fetish. We used to think the Obi men and the West
Coast operators worked together. Besides, most Obi
men were West Africans. The mortality in the West
India regiments in the old days was so great from this
cause that recruiting on the West Coast of Africa was
stopped years ago. “Starlight,” if he’s knocking about
Sydney, could tell all about it (if he wonld)-he was a
corporal one time in the Ist. West India Regiment.

“Pyalla”: This is from Sydney Tele-
graph of 16/2/’O4: —
Yesterday, at Dry Creek, near Merrendee, a woman was
cooking at an open fire, when a spark ignited her dress.
She rushed into the tent, which caught fire, and set the
grass alight. The flames spread with great rapidity, and
were not mastered till five square miles on the Burren-
dOng and adjoining holdings were burnt out.
In its grief about the burnt grass the paper forgets
to even mention whether the woman was burnt to
death or not. Cows eat grass, and they don’t, as
a rule, eat women, and the loss of valuable cow-
sustenance blots out everything else.
“ Periwinkle ” : The average mission-bred
aborigine—half-caste or full-blood—seems to know
just as little about the bunyip as his superstitious
forefathers. Was on the Murray last season
moving my sot-lines, as a freshet was coming
down stream, and had a black from the
local mission station giving me a hand. We
were just finishing when we heard some-
thing snorting and groaning up the
stream. Suddenly a black object
appeared in the current round the
bend. My pal didn’t wait to pull
ashore. With one yell—“the
Bunyip ! ” —he sprang overboard,
and breasted the flood to the oppo-
site (N.S.W.) side, and made a
beeline for the source of religious
consolation. Pulled to my side, a
bit nervous, and got back to my
tent to call my white mate, who
was cooking. Cautiously we recon-
noitred, and found the bunyip afore-
said was a prime fat sow. We lent
her a hand to get ashore, and had splen-
did smoke-cured bacon till a week ago. We
watch every flood now for another bunyip.

“ Vigilans ” writes :
The Orow-eaters are making a great noise just now over
an insignificant little place called Esperance, on the S.E.
coast of Westralia. “We will not sanction the transcon-
tinental railway,” they say, “ unless a line is run from
Esperance to the goldfieids.” A more dismal, God-for-
gotten place it would be difficult to find in a God-forsaken
land—a sand patch that wouldn’t raise a potato, situated
on a desolate coast without one redeeming feature. One
marvels how the inhabitants live. There are four
“ hotels ” there, three of which pay no rent. Four stores
are still in existence. A butcher tries to sell mutton at
Is. 4d. per lb., and a baker disposes of a 21b. loaf at sd.
Most of the buildings are untenanted, and the only people
who know the feel of coin are the Afghan camel-drivers
about the place. Perth land-sharks are endeavoring to
boom the patch, and ridiculous advertisements are to be
seen in the papers concerning the land. Why, the
nearest land suitable for any kind of cultivation is a mere
flat of a few acres owned by a sheep man 50 miles west,
and another strip on the Thomas River (called a river bv
courtesy) 80 miles east. “The railway must come,” a
sleepy citizen remarks whenever you meet one ; but they
have as much chance of getting one as our old friend
Buckley. The name of Esperance is misapplied. There
is no hope !

“Trotter”: The northern portion of
South Australia is mostly in a bad way. Draw a
line at the head of the Gulf across the continent,
and the country north is usually as dead
as Golgotha. Some of this country has been
given to cookies to try and raise wheat upon,
which for the past six years they have not been
able to do. The rainfall averages lOin. : the land
is covered with stones, and the cocky slings his
wheat about in a half-hearted fashion, and trusts
to Providence, and there is no Providence beyond
Quorn. The Government supplies seed at from
3s. to ss. a bushel to be thrown away in this
wilderness. lam going to write a book and call
it “Seed in Hell.”
* * .
The early extinction of the lyre-bird—the
most distinctive of Australia's feathered tribe
and its champion mimic—is threatened by the
devastations of foxes throughout the fern glades
of Gippsland. Any bird that doesn’t roost high
is liable to come, sooner or later, to Reynard’s
maw, and the lyre-bird, unfortunately, "doesn’t
roost at all. When a fox wants a meal, he has
only to go softly to a lyre-bird’s lair, and then the
prettiest tail feathers in the southern hemisphere
are mauled by a smellful red animal that is
neither useful nor ornamental.

Drivers of Western Queensland coaches
often execute peculiar commissions for residents
along their routes, and have extraordinary re
quests made to them. Recently a Jehu on one of
the lines that traverse the Never-Never was asked
by a rouseabout to delay until he had “tried on ”
a pair of pants (just received by parcels post).
The “trial”—made by the roadside —proved
satisfactory, and His Majesty’s mail resumed its
dignified progress.
A SPRING IN THE GRASS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The squatters have flitted and fled,
They sing of their woes by the sea,
The ’hoppers have sprouted and spr ad,
They clamor tumultuously ;
In billion-battalions of glee
The flies of Beelzebub pass
And impress the great fact upon me—
Behold—there’s a Spring in the Grass !
The land that was land of the dead
Now beats in a new energy ;
A ripple is heard in the bed
That was dry as a desert could be;
The crow is a-perch on the tree,
And wailing in sorrow—” Alas !
The Drought is my festivitee —
But—mow there’s a Spring in the Grass ! ”
The ’hoppers they woo and they wed,
And the hawks pick them up as they flee ;
The swaggie he scratcheth his head
And he dreams of a dead-and-gone spree ;
Eternal the life that we see
Spring up from the soil that was brass,.
To the tune of a mad melodie—
Behold 1 There’s a Spring in the Grass 1
Envoi.
The tender bull-ants riot free,
And, fearing the bard and his gas,
They charge like a wild Cherokee—
And, Jo ! there’s a Spring in the Grass !
P.L.

“Prooshan ” : Our most truculent ants —
the bulldog and the “jumper”—cannot sting if
the breath is held while handling them. I was
with a bush party recently, camped for dinner in
the Bargo forest, where “jumpers”are as thick as
flies. My mates, however, wouldn’t try the ex-
periment, even after I had patted a “jumper
hole and laid my hand flat thereon for the excited
ants to work their will upon. But one mustn’t
speak while demonstrating. I had a bulldog ant
on my thumb one day, and, after teasing him and
proving his inability to sting, I was proceeding
to say—“ There, you see, he can’t do anything ” ;
when he instantly did something. I have tried
this plan successfully also with bees. [Without
offering any opinion on the main question,
Bulletin would suggest that if the experimenter
got a number of ants on him, and it took some
time to locate them and get them off,, and he held
his breath all the while, he might burst.]

“ Werrimce " : “Pyalla” (B. 11,/2/’O4)
says: “ One of the possessions of swagmen that I
have never seen mentioned
is what they call the ‘ hipper.’
It is worn round the hips at
night, generally consists of a
marsupial fur or a stuffed
strip of bagging, and is a
great saver of the hip bone.”
I have never seen nor heard
of that kind of hipper, but
the bush is manifold and its
people various. A few men
are met with who carry their
hippers with them—a small
pad stuffed with horse-hair,
feathers, or wool. Some (bony
individuals) make a small
hollow in the ground for the
convenience of the hip bone;
but the majority of travellers
are content to gather a few
leaves or an armful of grass.
Examine camps along road-
side, and seven times out of
ten you will find grass or
leaves near the ash heap. At
shepherds’ huts, shearing
sheds and sheep-station
homesteads woolly sheepskins
are almost invariably used,
not only by callers, but by
the permanent hands, as mat-
tresses. I have seen men
going out to lamb-marking
yards, or to do a few days’
work somewhere on the run,
take half a cartload of sheep-
skins with them as part of
their bedding. Some men
object to them, saying that
the yolk absorbed from the
wool causes rheumatism.
Dunno! Used skins for
years; got no rheumatism yet.
Pommy J.P.” ; Lost my way once on a
WG j * n Bie Big Scrub between Twowharves
and Ballina, but about midnight I struck the hut
of a lonely cane-cockie. He gave me a good feed
—pumpkin-pie, roast corn-cobs, johnnie cakes,
®c., topped off with a swig of raw Richmond
River rum. “Now,” said my host, “ya gotter
s ! n g * song f’r ya blow-out and ya bunk.” “ Can’t
smg, I said. “Well, spin us a yarn.” “Don’t
knowany.” “Showus some card-tricks.” “Don’t
play cards.” “Whistle a tune, then.” “Can’t
whistle. ’ Then suddenly the cockie, a powerful
giant, fell on me, picked me up, threw me under
the table, dragged me round by my legs, bumped
me against the slabs, sat on me, thumped me all
° v « T r > al [d finally flung me far out into the night.
Now, he grunted, “ that’s a story ya can tell,
anyhow. Good night.”
When two men with turbans on meet
nowadays in far-back N.S. Wales, the proper
thing is to wait and see whether they will throw a
half-brick at one another or only a curse. About
half the times they will throw something; the
other half they will stop and arrange to throw the
brick at a third turban when they meet him. At
Broken Hill, which is the distributing centre for
the camel trains, and outside which they have
their homes, so far as they have any homes at all,
they are' divided into two main camps. One
camp, east of the town, is mostly the Abdul Wade
faction ; the other is the faction of Afghans who
have gone into the business more recently.
Things went all right while the drought was on
and they all had plenty of work to do at about
any price they liked to ask ; but now, when
drought is “off,” and the waggon teams
have a chance again, business isn’t brisk,
and prices have been cut, and the dark
shadow of the mortgage and the hill of sale has
spread itself over some of the camel trains.
As the bill of sale isn’t a bird with which
either the Afghan or the camel had any acquain-
tance in their native lair, there has been no end
of trouble. Few of the camel-trains are any
longer owned exclusively by the Afghan ; where
the big storekeepers dealing in station supplies
haven't had a cut-in, station men themselves have
got there, and the consequent mixture of European
and Asiatic methods of doing business has caused
an unholy commotion. Every time a bill of sale
or other disaster strikes a camp it blames the
other camp, just as Dill Maoky blames the Pope ;
and there have been more broken heads over the
business than have got into the papers.

THE NIGHT OWL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
It is the diners’ happy hour,
And round the glowin';; logs they sit ;
Each old rag tent, a fairy bower
Appears, by dancing firebeams lit.
The moon sails high, and sees, below,
In lazy mood, the diggers there.
With faces and with pipes aglow,
They’ve left their toil to shepherd Care.
While Russian Jack goes on to tell :
“ I catch the old dam horse, and so
I gallop it as fast as hell
From here right to the Overflow/'
And Posthole Jack, on mud-map, shows
A south-east track to Watty’s Hole.
Old Jimmy Lynn excited grows :
“ It’s due sou’-west—Odamitall! "
No man dared laugh at Scotty’s tale
When big fat Scotty swore ’twas true ;
But when he’d done, and silence fell,
They’d bless the owl that cried “ Coo-hoo ! ”
I camped last night beside the Are,
Where five long years ago they met
(What fate has fallen on Mac the Liar ?
And is the Riger living yetij,
A dead forgotten place it seemed :
Wild daisies in the ashes grew :
And as I lay, and, smoking, dreamed—
The night-owl sang his soft “ Coo-hoo ! "
D.B.G.

The essential difference is that we can’t prove the ordi-

By the way, your figures regarding a certain Sydney
“ death-trap” are exaggerated, and you seem to forget

wholesale, without question, can’t avoid having a high

CLARENCE WEBER, the young Victorian
strong man, who forms a bridge across two
chairs with an anvil resting on his chest,
whilst a blacksmith breaks a piece of cold
iron on the anvil.
Photo, by The Swiss Studios, Melbourne.
IF THE HIVES OF CHINA SWARM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
O ! ye nations of the White Man don’t be foolish any-
where
Now the Jap has got to death-holts with the large pale
Russian bear ;
'Tis the White against the Yellow, and the struggle will
be grim,
For the Bear is very powerful, and the Jap is very
“ slim.”
Keep your eye upon the combat, and your ammunition
dry,
And your cannon all in order, you may want them by-
and-bye
When the shells are flying wildly and the fight is pretty
warm,
And the issue complicated—if the hives of China swarm.
Let the pride of race determine where your sympathies
will go,
For you’ve nothing much in common with the Yellow
man, you know.
You may sell your Color now', but the reward that you
will get
Will fall in showers of curses on your hapless people yet.
And the little Jap is wily, and he isn’t good to trust
When he’s striking for more elbow-room to hustle for his
crust.
But he’ll hide his deepest purpose till the eager legion
form,
And he leads potential millions—if the hives of China
swarm.
They may rail}' to his banner and their ancient feuds
forget,
From the country of the Manchu to the steppes of old
Thibet;
For they loathe the foreign devils from the mushroom
nations white,
That have forced on them the reason and necessity to
fight;
That would desecrate their temples and would trample
on their dead,
And the customs that have flourished while ten thousand
years have fled.
Ay ! the Yellow men are brothers, true in blood as well
as form,
And they’ll take a lot of beating—if the hives of China
swarm.
And, inspired by all the glories and traditions of the
past,
They may leap to life and action now the die of W'ar is
cast,
By a common motive quickened, and by racial reasons
urged,
They may fight with old-time spirit till their Fatherland
is purged
Of the trash that roughly tramples on its idols and its
faith,
To replace them wdth a deity that’s but a misty w-raith.
O ! the world will stand in wonder at the bursting of the
storm,
And the crisis of its story—if the hives of China swarm.
VIC.
BEN SUN.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Its sanction of a Chinese-labor policy in
S’Africa seems to have about put the last nail into
the Balfour Government’s coffin in England. The
funeral may be any day now.
Ex-Premier Irvine gets £l5OO or so
because he is sick. The average Victorian gets
12 months in gaol for being sick, the hospitals
being always overcrowded.
Dear B.—Approaching visit of those Staf-
fordshire men to found works here is further
evidence, I presume, that, through Federal legis-
lation, Australia is going to pot!— B. Gosh.
Westralian Government, pushed on by the
Labor party, has in three years enacted Industrial
Arbitration, Electoral Reform, a Factories Act,
and a Workmen’s Compensation Act ; and Old
Age Pensions are in sight.
The Vie. Political Labor League nomi-
nates “Mr. Scott-Bennett, of Toorak,”as Labor
candidate for Ballarat West at the State election.
Isn’t there a Montmorency-Vavasour or a Tavis-
tock-Cholmondeley available ?
The success of Edward Terry for Ryde
(N.S.W.) State election caused much bitterness,
and it was proposed to celebrate the occasion.
The result is, three banquets are in prospect —one
to Terry, because he was successful; one to
Henley, because he might have been successful,
but wasn’t; and one to Thompson, who stood
down because the Deform Ass. didn’t select him.
Mr. H. Curry, Dubbo (N.S.W.) post-
master, received on November 2 last a Federal
memo, instructing him to exhibit the electoral
roll for 30 days and to return it on November 14.
Mr. Curry did his best, but is still troubled by
official explanations as to how he might have
done better. His, however, is only one of a
number of impossible orders, which should not be
repeated at future elections.
THE SOURCE OF OUR GREATNESS.
IMPORTER : “ Hi, where are you going?"
N.S.W.: “ I'm off to make some engines for my
railways.”
IMPORTER : “ Man, man, what are you thinking
of? Don’t you know you must borrow the
money and import the engines? ”
Of 57 members of N.S.W. Legislative
Council, 44 put in an appearance during last short
session. That represents the extreme effective
strength of the House. Within five years, if it
were allowed to dwindle naturally, the Council
could muster about 25 members. The difficulty
about letting the Upper House die out in this
fashion is that it would be a Chamber of toothless
imbecility in its closing years.
They've noticed in Maoriland that, whilst
the bulk of the people are misled into sympathi-
sing with Japan in the present war,
The policy of backing the white man has a few ad-
herents ; these are very staunch, and, curiously enough,
they are all of the stamp of the pro-Boer advocates of the
eariy part of the last war In fact, they are mostly the
same persons.
In short, the genuine Australian-thinking person
thinks Australia all the time.
Broken Hill, which, 12 months ago, went
back to some extent on Labor administration of
the municipality, and gave the anti-Labor party
just enough majority to elect as mayor one who
wasn’t a Labor alderman, has returned to its old
allegiance. The new mayor is an engine-driver
who, before getting into the council, drove the
local steam-roller. He has long been the sec. of
the local engine-drivers’ association.
Queensland Ministers are still digging up
reasons why that State couldn’t pay its way under
the guidance of Philp and his Ministry of “busi-
ness men. ’ As far back as 1895 a license fee of
£5 was imposed on Queensland winegrowers who
sold their own produce ; but it was never col-
lected. Curiously enough, one of Philp’s col-
leagues represented a wine-growing district. The
duty should, it is said, bring in ,£2500 a year.
Truly, times have changed. Premier
Seddon, of Maoriland, now denounces the British
Government for breach of faith with the Boer
remnant, “ those fine fighting men, who certainly
would not have surrendered as soon as they did
had they known that hordes of Chinese were to be
brought into the Transvaal under slavery con-
ditions.” And a loud voice in the audience pro-
posed that Maoriland should send an army to
rescue the Boers from British tyranny.
“ Polonius ” writes :
Re “ Wusslean ” (8., 11/2/’O4) and Bent’s locomotives on
the Deepdene (Vic.) line. This line is used only at one
end —the other end is a dead boom-relic, and rusts peace-
hilly in the vicinity of the lunatic asylum. As regards
the locomotives, “ Wusslean ” mentions one as an upright
donkey engine. If it is driven by one of Bent’s loyalists,
then the engine isn’t the worst part of the show ; it is
better to be an upright donkey than a crooked blackleg.
I believe the donkey engine alluded to was put on the
line to supply a job for a man of that class, who found the
ordinary loco, quite beyond him, and who had to drive
something as an excuse for his official existence. Another
of Thomas’s “loyalists,” I believe, gets (or got) engine-
driver’s wages for driving nails. He couldn’t even drive
an upright donkey engine, and he also had to drive
something.
There has not been a contested election
for the Tas. Leg. Council in Launceston for 18
years. A vacancy has just occurred by the death
of William Hart, and in May the immortal Adye
Douglas retires by effluxion of “time” —but what
has Adye to do with time? No by-election can
.be held within three months of an ordinary
election, so the two events will be held on the
same date. Then a candidate will have to say
which seat he will go for—Hart’s or Douglas’s;
but the law doesn’t say that he can’t go for both.
Douglas (he’s only 88. and no sign of old age
setting in yet) says he means to seek re-election.
Probably he and some other ancient will have a
walk over.

Melbourne Age on Premier Bent •
The failure of Mr. Irvine’s powers became visible long
before the close of last session, though he himself was
unaware of it, or perhaps sought to blind himself to the
unpleasant fact In Mr. Shiels the physical breakdown
was so complete that he remained at his post only in
defiance of his medical adviser. Of the other members of
the Cabinet holding seats in the Assembly, Mr. Bent was
the only one who had vigor enough to brush the flies
away from the departmental door-post.
In other words, the new Ministry consists of a
fly-whisk and some nonentities.
Another of Victoria’s “permanent and
reproductive ” works has gone more or less up
the spout. This is the Carrum swamp, whose
drainage and management is looked after by
an “ Irrigation ” Trust. Melbourne district has
had 10 inches of rain already this year, as a
consequence of which the Carrum swamp, mostly
settled by potato-growers, is now from a yard to
five feet under water, and the ruined market-
gardeners are appealing for help. Carrum land is
as rich as the Garden of Eden, but in a dry
summer the peaty soil itself is liable to catch fire,
while in a wet year it can only be reached in
boats.
THE DOG AND THE DUST CLOUD,
“Mr. G. H. Reid is spending an enjoyable holiday at Riama, on the south
coast of N.S.W. He makes excursions around the district in a motor car.
THE DRY DOG: “Great Jee-rusalem! This is the driest job I’ve
taken on yet.”
Just before the outbreak of the present
war Britain obliged Japan by buying, over the
head of Russia, from Chili the two warships Con-
stitucion and Libertad. Britain had declined at
first to buy them, but, when Russia came forward
with a cash offer, it outbid the Muscovite. There
is not much concealment about British help to
Japan, or French help to Russia, and it seems
more than probable that in the course of the war
some overt act on one side or another will change
more or less concealed sympathy into open
assistance.
“ Watcher ” writes :
“ Vic ” (8., 11/2/’O4) asks “ due credit for Tait, Hudson,
and Fitzpatrick at the Victorian railways.” By all
means ; but consider, also, their luck. Erratic weather
caused the most straggle some harvest on record ; and
when, despite that, a block did seem imminent, shipping
arrangements collapsed temporarily and gave the manage-
ment a respite. Moreover, the wheat, hasn’t all been
handled yet. At date of writing (15/2/’O4) the stripping
and winnowing isn’t finished, threshers aren’t available,
and acres of wheat sacks are stacked in station yards
here (N.-W. Vic.). Further, the wood traffic has been
entirely neglected, and complaints of late trains, slow
trains, and no trains are as rife as ever.
About the only practical thing that the
Treasurers’ Conference did was to come to an
agreement by which the various State Govern-
ments will be able to use one another’s London
balances in case of need, and thus save heavy
exchange. Under the new system a State, which
happens to be “in funds” in London, will allow
its balance to be used by another State needing
money in London just then, and the loan will be
squared in Australia. And the idea isn't worth
so much after all. It is a long time since any
State has had superfluous funds in London or any-
where else.
In its platform for the coming Victorian
elections the Kyabram crowd goes back on the
only plank that ever seemed to justify its exis-
tence. Formerly it was against borrowing;
now it advocates the “restriction of borrowing,
except for works of an undoubtedly reproductive
character, loan resumptions and necessary national
works.” In other words, there maybe unrestricted
borrowing for reproductive works, even if the
money costs 4 per cent, and the works are only
reproductive to the extent of 2 per cent., and for
“necessary national works ” which are not repro-
ductive. Then, it seems that, in addition, there
may be restricted borrowing for other works.
Good old comprehensive Kyabram !
Under Wentworth’s draft of a Federal
Constitution, 50 years ago, George Turner might
have had an easier job than under the present law
—and again he might not. The Federal income
in the Wentworth idea was to be derived, not
from imposts determined by the Federal Govern-
ment, but from a percentage on the revenues of
the States. The province of the Federation was
to be the making of laws, and the laws were to
cover tariffs, railways, lighthouses, and courts of
appeal; but the States were to collect the taxes
and pay over a percentage to the Federation —
provided the Federation could get it. Wentworth
was backed by Cowper, Martin, Macleay, Murray,
and others, but the scheme did not catch on, and
in a few years was forgotten.
It looks as if the Bear-and-Monkey war is
to be another disappointment to the naval expert
johnnie who is always planning the war of the
future. Ever since the battle-ship was in any-
thing like its present form he has been longing
for a real fair bluggy engagement between two
evenly-matched modern fleets. He does not
count the American-Spanish war because the
Spaniards were hopelessly overweighted. And
now there bas been a night rush, a surprise, a cat-
fight in the darkness, and one fleet is crippled
before there is time to strike a match to see if
there were any tactics at all. The nearest approach
to a lesson so far is that it is poor business to
build ironclads at £1,000,000 each only that they
may be obliterated in the dark by a little penny
torpedo boat.
S. M. Herald, in a doxology voice, refers
to Compulsory Arbitration :
Some protection against “ sweating ” there must be,
but amongst, ourselves that protection should be afforded
by the existence of a public opinion that makes for
justice. Legislation and labor artifices of all kinds far
oftener lag at the heels of such opinion than they act as
a lubricant to the wheels of progress.
“The existence of a public opinion that makes for
justice.” The Herald has been ostensibly
making public opinion since 1831, weekly, bi-
weekly, tri-weekly; and daily since 1840; but
protection against sweating did not appreciably
advance on that account And curiously enough,
when it was a question of giving the Herald 50
per cent, bonus by carrying it free through the
post at the taxpayer’s expense, the old paper
wanted concrete law sure enough. The idea of
trusting to that flabby, ineffectual thing called
public opinion where its own valued halfpenny
was at stake didn’t enter into its calculations.
A pugilist’s yarn-
told some months ago:
“Yes, I’m doing a quiet
bit of trainin’ for the elec-
tions. Out of form last time
when a candidate bloke
offered me a ‘ fiver ’ to keep
people from arsking ’im
nasty questions. If ’e didn’t
answer anybody straight
away, then I was to give
them the straight tip, with
just as much stoush as was
wanted. Got along all right,
flattened out four atdifferent
meetings, till the night be-
fore the election. Then a
bloke arsks ’im a nasty
question about a tart, and
I worked me way through
the crowd to be ready. The
boss kept on talkin’. I told
the bloke to pull off and
look up the girl’s address in
a directory, or something,
but 'e got nasty and roared
out ag’in. The boss looked
down to see what I was up
to, so I lashes out. I
missed ’im by about six
inches, and then ’e came at
me like a shower of tomb-
stones. I was fat, and ’e
buzzed round me like a mad ’ornet. At last ’e brought
me one on the jaw and flattened me. When I came
round ’e ’ad told that crowd such a tale about the boss
and that tart that the boss was pelted and ’owled at.
This time I’m going to be ready ; no more bloomin’
cyclone surprises for me.”
A memo, for Australia, which, with some
of the best iron ore and coal and plenty of idle
labor, still imports all its iron from the other side
of the globe :
In 1786 the Pennsylvania Legislature lent a certain Mr
Humphries the sum of £3OO to enable him to make steel
“as good as in England.” This was the beginning of the
steel and iron industry in the United States.
In 1902 the U.S. produced 17,821,000 tons of iron,
being 41 per cent, of all the world’s output, and
just over twice as much as Great Britain was-
credited with.
There is something of the thimble-rigger’s
taotics about the latest development in the Yic.
Bent Government. A sort of now-you-see-it-and-
now-you-don’t business. The Government had
no sooner been formed than it was broken up
again. Taverner was sent off to London, and
Sam Gillott was taken into the Ministry; he
takes the portfolio of Chief Secretary, Murray
being transplanted to the Lands. Gillott was
Attorney-General in the Peacock Cabinet. He
went into Opposition with Peacock when Irvine
came into power, but shamelessly deserted
his party at the next elections. Swinburne,
who takes up Shiels’s job at the Railways,
is a tall, thin Tory. Bent was Minister
for Railways a few days ago, but what Bent is
now people hardly know, except that he is still
Bent. This rushing round from portfolio to
portfolio and from job to job of Taverner, Gillott,.
Murray, Swinburne, and Bent is confusing. It
makes the Ministry look like a devil turning
somersaults in a dust-storm.

M.L.A., who regards borrowing by Treasury,
bills as “increasing the revenue.”

sent to the field again, and at last had to return to
Australia feeling very bad indeed.
I saw Mr. S. Wood, of Brunswick-street, Fitzroy, and
he said to me, “ Come to my shop and I will give you a
bottle of VITADATIO, and if it does not cure you, you

I felt a great relief of the pains in my side. I continued
taking the VITADATIO, and after the fourth bottle I
was one day taken very sick and vomited up a black jelly
substance full of Hydatids.
I am now in the best of health, and anyone who would
like to see me can learn what your medicine has done for
me.
I am
Yours faithfully,
K. FERGUSON.

Fred Gillett, Esq., Tutor, Walgett, N.S.W.,
writes “ For years I had been a sufferer from
chronic constipation, due to my profession of
Tutor being a very sedentary occupation. The
ordinary aperient medicines I was using only
made matters worse. I sent for a free sample of
Chamberlain’s Stomach and Liver Tablets, and
the results were so satisfactory that I have since
used half a dozen bottles. The Tablets have
certainly done me a great deal of good, for when
I first began to use them I had to take four
tablets twice a week to produce the desired effect.
Have been gradually decreasing the dose, until
now two tablets a month keeps the bowels in good
order. I recommend Chamberlain’s Stomach and
Liver Tablets to all who suffer as I did.”

“Argo”: What a foolish thing it is to
publish the Papal Coat of Arms—keys and all!
I have just been thinking that your corres-
pondent, “Burglar Bill,” will be making
duplicates of them. Trouble is likely to ensue
when, some morning, a few halos, harps, &c.,
are missing.
Died near Adelaide, H. E. Bright, one of
the quaintest old figures in former S. Australian
politics. He was a country butcher, and looked
and talked like it; but he cultivated polite
literature to some extent, and mingled
Shakspeare and sausages marvellously. “ Mr.
Speaker,” he would say, with his hands thrust
deep into his trousers pockets—“ Mr. Speaker,
I tell you what it is, the lily don’t want no
paint.” This would probably be apropos some
railway bill of which he had charge as Minister
for Works in the Blyth Cabinet—he was in that
office just when S. Australia had a bad fit of
railway building, now 30 years ago. For nearly
quarter of a century he was in politics, but
dropped out over 20 years ago. He knew the
Lower North better than most men, and in his
time did fine work for it and for Gawler, which
was his stronghold.
Rich Gas Co. director Swinburne and bar-
rister Mackey, selected by Bent to strengthen his
new Vie. Cabinet, have both been in politics
only about 18 months. They are the only new
politicians of any ability that the Kyabramapootra-
managed to hatch. The offer of salaried office to
Swinburne so shortly after he entered Parliament
almost, but not quite, beats the record—lawyer
Sam Gillott, the friend of Syme, crept into the
Turner Government as an honorary Minister 12
months after his first return to the Assembly, and
took Isaacs’ job as Attorney-General two or three
months later. If Swinburne manages to be a dead
failure in less time than Gillott did, he will be a
remarkable man.

“ Kodak ” gives a snapshot of an ocean
excursion party:
He was an undersized, red-faced man, and wore a
small boxer hat and a glazed eye. Obviously he had
been drinking. He roamed up and down the crowded
upper deck, towing a fat, stupid kid, and fell over
people’s feet. Then he went downstairs and fell over the
feet of the excursionists on the lower deck. Finally’ he
fell up against the temporary tea and coffee buffet. He
leant heavily on the counter, and leered at the girl
serving, and pointed to a plate of cake. “Got some
cake f’r a baby ?” he gurgled, confidentially. “ I suppose
so,” said the waitress in a crisp, business-like tone. She
seized a knife, slashed off a slice, put it on a plate and
passed it across. The fat man extended a grateful paw
and started to return thanks. The waitress snatched the
plate back. “It will cost you threepence,” she remarked,
briefly ; and put the cake out of his reach while she went
away to attend to two girls. The fat man seemed dazed
at her action. A look of sorrow crept into his eyes, and
he reached down and patted his corpulent infant’ sadly
on the head. “ Pore little kid,” he mumbled, huskily ;
“ they ain’t got any cake f’r a baby.” The kid, who up till
then had been contentedly licking a brass rod, gave one
upward glance at his father’s sorrowful face, and started to
snivel wretchedly under his hat. In another minute the
waitress darted back again, and the fat man stretched
across the counter and laid a fat hand on her arm. “ Ain’t
yergot some cake f’r ababy?” he asked again, imploringly.
“ Threepence apiece,” snapped the girl. “I told y’ou so
before.” The fat man started violently, as if someone
had thrown a loaf of bread at him, and gazed at her
distractedly. “ But it’s f’r a baby !” he protested. It’s
only f’r a baby." Then he seized the kid and lifted it
until its fat chin rested on the counter. “ There you
are ! ” he gasped, triumphantly, “ there’s th’ baby.” I
didn’t wait to see any more. The fun of the position
had reached its apex, and I feared an anti-climax.
There’s generally an anti-climax to a promising situation
like that.
A MODERN ANTHONY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Anthony Giles, from youth to manhood throve.
And found the life he lived as sweet as honey,
Although he never knew the pangs of love,
Nor yet the bitter sweets of matrimony.
’Tvvas not the lack of chances was to blame,
So do not either pity or deride him—
Indeed, he bore a most befitting name,
So very often had the ladies tried him.
And so Anthony stayed a celibate,
And gave in many eloquent discourses
The reasons why he shunned the married state,
And love and debt and babies and divorces.
But in the passing of the years, our friend
Took up the dice of Fate and threw the seven,
And waited with complacent mind the end
Which opened up the endless joys of Heaven.
He died—his shadow reached the pearly gate
Whose shining beauties promised something sweeter
Within. Just then he found he had to wait
And talk the matter over with St. Peter.
He looked around awhile, and then perceived
The keeper-saint on guard before the wicket,
And murmured out his thanks as he received,
And then prepared to read the proffered ticket.
“ Look here, good Saint!” cried Giles with rueful face
“This ticket’s for Gehenna that you’re giving.”
“That’s right, my friend,” said Peter, “that’s your
place ;
You had your share of Paradise whilst living.”

The Farmer Who Can’t Be Protected.
Sydnet Telegraph, booming again what it calls
the “foundational industries,” prints that in 1901
“Commonwealth manufactures were only worth
a little over £27,000,000, while the products of
other industries reached a value of £85,500,000.”
“Therefore,” saystheD.T., “ the public welfare is
conditioned not on the urban industries, on
whose behalf some politicians are always be-
seeching the community to tax itself, but on
the directly-producing industries which Pro-
tection cannot protect, but can only clog by
making the tools of production and the
food of the producers dearer.” Most of these
“foundational industries” are concerned in the
production of foods. The farmer’s and the
dairyman’s and the orchardist’s business is to sell
such goods; and the D.T. says that a system
which allows these people to get a better price is
a bad thing for them —that it “clogs” their
industry ! As a matter of fact, if there were not
a single duty on foodstuffs or fodder or the other
products of the “foundational industry” man,
Protection would still put pounds into his pooket.
Compare, for instance, the present prices of
wheat in Australia and Europe respectively.
The Australian farmer is lucky who gets 2s. Bd.
per bushel, delivered at the seaboard. _ If, as
more often happens, he has to sell at his local
railway siding, he probably gets 2s. sd. Yet the
very wheat which he has been selling at that price
has been sold for delivery in March or April in
Europe at 295. per 4801 b., or at the rate of 3s. 7|d.
per bushel Tne man who makes most of his
machinery, and his moleskins, and his Crimean
shirt, and his youngsters’ flannelette, and his pipe
and matches, and the concertina which he played
when lie was younger —the man who makes these
and most of the other things that he uses, lives in
Europe on wheat at 3s. per bushel. The
farmer thus has to buy on the three-and-seven-
pennv basis, while he has to sell on a tiwo-and-six-
pmny basis. If the man who made his machinery
and moleskins and Crimean shirt and other
t hings lived alongside him, in the same country,
the Is. Id. would be saved, not to mention the
cost of bringing the machinery and moleskins back
from the other end of the world. Even if he still
only got 2s. Gd. for his wheat he would get his
machinery, moleskin, &c., cheaper, because the
man who made them would be living on two-and-
sixpenny wheat- ,\Vhat happens to the farmer
happens to all the other “foundational industry”
men; through lack of local markets they have to
accept very low prices, otherwise, on account of
the heavy expense of shipping such great dis-
tances, they could not compete with the
producers nearer to the large manufacturing
centres. We can’t shift the whole of those manu-
facturing centres closer to us; but we can shift
them so far as they are manufacturing centres for
ourselves. When we have done that, by a policy
of Protection, we shall save two lots of freights—
the freight of the wheat and the wool and the
metal to Europe, and the freight of the manu-
factured stuff back —and the “foundational” pro-
ducer will put into his pockets the money that
now makes dividends for the foreign shipping co.,
and wages for its Dago crew, and about half-a-
score of commissions for half-a-dozen greedy
middlemen who now live fat, while the squeezed
farmer is as lean as he might naturally be expected
to be in those circumstances.

An interfering person, called the Earl of Meath,
has announced that he desires the children in all
the schools of the empire to sing, on the 24th of
May (the birthday of the late Queen Victoria),
a new composition called the “Flag of
Britain.” Meath himself is a representative of
the class which is the largest and vilest ink-blot on
the Flag of Britain, but he would probably be
very angry if he were told so. In Britain
12,090,009 people live on the verge of starvation
because Meath and the other great hereditary
1 unigrabbers own so much of the country’s wealth
that the scrap which they leave for the bulk of
the nation can’t by any possibility, or by any toil
or struggle, be made to go round. Thousands
of British infants are cruelly murdered by slow
starvation every year by people like Meath ;
thousands of women starve for the sake of
people like Meath ; thousands of persons com-
mit suicide, and, presumably (according to the
church), go to everlasting torment, and thousands
die miserably in workhouses, and millions have
hardly- a gleam of happiness in all their sordid
lives—all because of people like Meath. The
country is full cf ghosts pointing fleshless fingers
of accusation at people like Meath. Some day,
the people will, perhaps, take down the Flag of
Britain for awhile—they may want the flagpole to
hang people like Meath upon.

A so far unnoticed passage of the Knibbs
education report:
In view of the fact that at the present time the schools
of the State are not absolutely free, and that there is
always a possibility of the logical consequence of com-
pulsory, i. e. , free education, being realised, it may be
said that a change of the regime would be a fitting
occasion to consider the wisdom of establishing two
classes of schools, viz., free schools, and schools in which
a fee is charged.
The idea of establishing two classes of schools
must receive short shrift. Free schools, and
schools in which a fee is charged, are the Britisli
elementary schools of 50 years ago—the schools for
the children of paupers and for those not ranked as
such. Evidently Mr. Knibbs knows nothing of
the spirit that has inspired the establishment of
State elementary schools in Australia. It has
consistently aimed at the fusion of all classes and
all religions, not at the segregation of any.
Another foreign syndicate is just now dangling a
tempting bait before Victorian Premier Bent of
the loud cuss. A while back the Victoria Electric
Light, Heat and Power Distributing Co. obtained
concessions under the Victorian Electric Light
and Power Act to supply Melbourne suburbs
with electricity at 4d. per unit for light, and 2d
per unit for power. All the Bay suburbs, from
Brighton right round to Wyndham, granted these
concessions for nothing. The company was under-
stood to have certain large deposits of brown coal
at Werribee, and it was going to supply electricity
forthwith. That was about a year ago. Having
got its concessions, the company sent its manager
to England to negotiate with an English syndi-
cate, and this seems to have been formed—of five
electrical engineering firms. This syndicate
reckons it can put up furnaces and electricity-
generating plant at Werribee and can get its
brown coal thereto at 2s. per ton, and on
the strength of this calculation it has cabled
to Victoria offering to supply unlimited
power at Id. per unit for the electrification of
Melb. suburban railways and other purposes,
provided the Government agrees to take not less
than 10,000,000 units annually for at least 10
years. Also, the syndicate undertakes to spend
.8500,000 in “ developing the brown coal industry
and in laying down machinery.” Before Victoria
makes any promises, it might inquire how it is
that the Victoria Electric, etc., Co. hasn’t started
to supply light and power to at least one of the
seven suburbs which have granted it the right to
do so, and why the extensive deposits of brown
coal at Werribee—that can be brought to the pit’s
mouth for 2s. a ton —still remain undeveloped;
also, why the 00. had to send its manager to
England to form a syndicate, and whether, if this
syndicate gets its bigger concession, it proposes to
form a new and bigger syndicate to work that, or
float it, or dwell on it. At present there is only
large talk about millions of tons of coal at 2s. per
ton, and millions of units of electricity at Id. per
unit. Such talk generally ends in certain foreign
shareholders and bondholders blocking develop-
ment for a long time, and finally camping on the
State Treasury doormat wailing about the
“inhuman treatment they have received from
the blasted colonies.”

Employees of the Midland Railway Co. (W. A.),
who have much poorer wages and much worse
labor conditions than the State’s railway men,
have prepared a case for the Arbitration Court;
and the co. has met the move by dismissing the
officers of the union. When a tiger gets into the
last stage of mange and disreputable degeneracy
it takes to man-eating. Midland Railway Co.
hasn’t enough energy to run a decent service, or
to fulfil its obligations to the State, but it is still
strong enough to act the petty tyrant towards its
employees.
“W.S.T.”: The Bulletin rightly draws atten-
tion to the advent of the Staffordshire pottery
makers, attracted by the moderate protection of
the Commonwealth tariff. Commenting upon
this circumstance one of the Sydney daily fact-
twisters affirms that the Staffordshire men and
their backers are Freetraders and have a lordly
contempt for the assistance derived from the
import-tax on ceramic ware. The answer is that,
though the fine kaolin of N.S.W. has been known
since Captain Cook’s time, no attempt was ever
made to work it under the alleged Freetrade
regime. All the same, many Sydney manufac-
turers have talked against Protection for so many
years that they have actually come to believe in
their own futilities. One of this class, whose out-
put of manufactured goods is about £lOO a week,
recently told me that business was never before so
brisk in his factory, and that he would be very
sorry to have the Bakton-Deakin tariff reduced.
“And yet,” I commented, “I recently saw your
name on a Reidite Senator’s committee-list. Is
that your idea of consistency ? ” He was forced
to weakly reply that he had voted the Freetrade
ticket from force of habit !

“ J.E P.” sends some crow facts :
The man who told “Cornelius” (B. 17/12/’O3) about
crows dropping pebbles to break emu eggs need not have
been a liar. The crow’s sagacity equals its rapacity. It
knows more than even a good liar can invent. It has
been seen to make itself vomit poisoned pollard by stick-
ing a claw down its throat. I once saw a crow trying to
throw a weak lamb by hanging on to the latter’s tail
with its beak and swaying vigorously from side to side.
Again, I was one day lying still under a tree when two
crows lodged up above and eyed me furtively. One
dropped a lump of bark near me, and, as I didn’t stir, it
dropped a bigger bit on me. Then it hopped lower down
and cawed loudly, to try if the noise had any effect. It
next eyed me steadily for several minutes, and in the end
it flew down, and hopped gradually closer, until I was
able suddenly to grab L. That crow’s was the only neck
I ever wrung, and it was a wonderfully satisfying opera-
tion.
The Inky Way.
The late Frank Norris’s advice to embryo-novelists:
“ Don’t write a Colonial novel. Don’t write a ‘ Prisoner
of Zenda ’ novel. Don’t write a novel. Try to keep your
friends from writing novels.”

Clipping from Southern Cross Times (W.A.): —
“ Mr. Chester’s Goat. —To the inhabitance of Southern
Cross All yea that as got Dogs, Cats, Sheep, Fouls,
goats, Horses, Cows, or any Domestic Animals, My advice
to you is to tie them up, and all your children to, for
there’s a cruel mean writch laying poison about Polaris-st.

Which up to the top it did float.

To poison young Chesters pet goat
