“ Inkfinger ” : The two most astonishing men about a
great daily paper, when they are dabs at their special-
ism, may be the law reporter and the commercial
editor. I knew a law reporter who could not make
a mistake by any chance. .He would turn up fright-
fully late—at the right time. No verbal judgment
ever caught him napping. His fugitive notes were on
odds and ends of paper. Yet the reports came out long,
symmetrical, perfect. The commercial editor, in my eye,
dumbfounded every other journalist in the place. He
was never seen till very late, when an appalling heap of
documents would be accumulated on the table. The
fashion in which he ripped through them was miraculous.
He skewered them with his electric mind, which went
like the flashing knives of a chaff-cutter. But neither
of these men was any good outside his specialty.
• • * »
When Herr Flurscheim spoke in Sydney some years
ago on “ Bimetallism,” late John Farrell was told off by
his editor to write a leader on the subject. “ I knew
nothing of it,” he used to tell afterwards, “ but I wrote
the leader. Nash (the financial editor) told me it was a
new thing and that the people needed to be educated up
to it, so I sat down and gave it slops; said the time wasn’t
ripe for such drastic changes, and that the people needed
to be educated up to it, and, generally speaking, I
demolished it. Next morning I was riding in a tram,
when two men started yarning. Said one : * See here,
have you read this here bimetallism yarn in the
Telegraph ? You can’t beat these papers. I’m blest if I
know how they do it! Here’s a man comes out lecturing
on an entirely new' thing—something none of us ever
heard of before —yet these papers have men ready for him
with their leading articles —men who know all about it.
Read this article, it will tell you what it is. ’ I could have
disabused him, but I didn’t. The time wasn’t ripe."
• • • •
Yet another Melbourne pressman has been obliged to
give up the game through ilbhealth, and try to nurse
himself to life again at a sanatorium. This is Donnelly,
of the Argus, who developed consumption when roughing
it in the service of his paper. Another Argus man, little
Magona, had to leave for similar reasons a few months
ago, and is now working in Westralia in a partially
patched-up state. The real grafting reporter on a
Melbourne daily, who can go on much beyond his
thirtieth year without developing some weak spot or
other, may consider that he had a marvellous consti-
tution to begin with.
* * * *
Victorian political pressmen find it hard to determine
which is to be viewed as the greater affliction—lrvine or
Bent. The cold and venomous Irvine always treated
reporters with a contemptuous reserve which was accen-
tuated directly a little bit of criticism crept into the
papers. The only satisfactory thing about this attitude,
so far as the reporters were concerned, was that they
had less to write. Bent is just the antithesis, and starts
talking discursively whenever he thinks there is a
reporter within earshot. The difficulty before the scribes,
now that he is Premier, is to decide how much of his
daily two or three columns of irresponsible chatter is
worthy of publication.
* » * *
Great changes, all of a sudden, among the Adelaide
weeklies. First Alfred M’Kain, founder and editor of
the Critic, resigned owing to ill-healih, being followed
by C. J. Dennis.. Then Crawford Vaughan resigned
editorship of Quiz, and J. L. Hutchinson (elected M.H.R.)
dropped out of the editoral chair of the Labor Herald.

to raise funds to change the existing Labor weekly into
a daily.

“ B.”: Looking over a pile of “ exchanges ”in the office
recently I amused myself counting up the different cor-
respondence appearing therein, dealing with every topic
under the sun. Result: “ Pro Bono Publico,” 21 letters ;
John Plummer, 20; “ Veritas,” 18 ; George Pile, “ Justi-
tia,” “Fair Play,” and “ Observer,” 17 each.
In the course of a recent lecture over in Westralia
Jimmy Thomson gave some interesting reminiscences of
his 36 years of press life. Starting as an apprentice on
the Australasian, Jimmy set up the copy of Marcus
Olarke, Dr. Jim Maloney, “ Orange Blossom ” Chapman,
Henry Kendall, Dr. Neild, James Smith, Charles Bright,
and many others. His old boss, Hugh George, often put
him on to special work, and one of the softest things of
the month was when the mail steamer arrived at Albany.
In those days there was no direct cable, and the budget of
hews collected at Point de Galle (the old stopping place
for P. and O. steamers) was brought on to Albany and
wired across. The telegraph station was in William-
street, in the premises now occupied by the Harbor
Trust, and the fastest hansom in the place was engaged
by the Argus to bring the messages to the office. It fell
to Jimmy’s lot to bring the first news of the fall of Sedan
and the abdication of Napoleon the Little, and he was
nearly torn to pieces by the vast crowd that completely
blocked Collins-street. Incidentally Jimmy mentioned
that, while in India as Victorian secretary at the Calcutta
International Exhibition, he visited Darjeeling in tjie
Himalayas and put up at the Woodlands Hot’l. On tbe
library table waa a copy of The Bulletin only four
weeks old.
THE TRANSFORMATIONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
It fell upon the Wishing Night,
Beside the Wishing Well ;
A Boy gazed in the water bright,
And spoke the magic spell.
He spoke the spell, and in the well
Cast quickly pebbles three.
*• I wish," he said, and turned his head,
“ An Editor to be.”
At that same hour on that same night
There sat, without a coat —
For it was warm—a weary wight
Who at a table wrote.
His brow was lined, his look was wise,
His head was bald, alas 1
He wore a shade above his eyes
To guard them from the gas.
Upon his face there was the trace
Of struggle and of pain ;
“ I wish," said he, “ that 1 could be
A careless Boy again."
The words he spoke in half a joke ;
But swiftly through the air
He seemed to go, like light, and lo
The Boy was in his Chair.
The Boy was old, with face that told
A tale of living free ;
Bald was his head, his nose was red
As never nose should be.
There is a careless Boy who plays
Beside the Wishing Well;
On moonlit nights and sunny days
You hear his happy yell.
No fun that’s going he will miss,
His days are full of mirth,
He knows the worth of youth ; he is
The wisest Boy on earth.
There is a weary Editor
Whose life is void of joy,
He haunts the country, breathing gore,
To find that careless Boy.
In vain with store of gold galore—
Which Editors amass—
Tiie Boy, to change their lots once more,
He tries to bribe. Alas !
Alas, for Youth that does not know
The glory Time doth mar 1
Alas for what we were, and, oh,
Alas for what we are I
H.s.w.
V.J.D.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Even too much whisky of the very best brand
is an evil, but it. is a harmless business compared with the
overdose of horse-racing from which Sydney suffers.
The programme for the current week ending February
27th is as follows :
Monday.—Rosebery Park races; Moorefield entries;
Canterbury Park settling; Warwick Farm weights;
Newmarket Handicap forfeit.
Tuesday.—New South Wales Trotting Club races.
Wednesday.—Kensington races; Brighton entries;
Newcastle races.
Thursday.—A. J.C. special general meeting ; V.R.O. St.
Leger and Australian Cup forfeits.
Friday.—Brighton races; Kensington and Rosebery
Park entries ; V.R.O. Sires Produce Stakes forfeit.
Saturday.—Warwick Farm races; V.R.O. autumn
meeting begins.
This isn't a vice, it’s a public nuisance ; and the army of
officials, bookmakers, spielers, jockeys, trainers and scum
of the earth who live on it ought to devote at least a few
days in the week to honest graft.
id * * *
It appears to be an understood thing amongst
some sporting folk that there is really nothing wrong in
“taking down” the bookie who joyfully lays “dead
’uns ” all the year round. But Chief Justice Stout (M.L.)
has just pointed out that, even though the penciller is
engaged in the illegal and nefarious practice of laying
tote odds, he can claim the protection of the law if an
attempt is made to swindle him. This was apropos of a
case in which two well-known and highly-respected men
got at Mo for a large amount through the old device of
ante-dating telegrams. They bravely planked their
beans on a moke that had already won on New Year’s
Day. One of the accused was a post and railway station
master, and how his office came to be open for the trans-
mission of telegrams on a general holiday is still a
mystery to the authorities. The receiving offices were
open certain hours, but his was a little side show.
Accused were admitted to probation.
* * * •
The Chinese lottery still manages to work on
smoothly enough in Melbourne. There’s one place in
Li’l’ Bourke-street, round the corner from Swanston-
street, where one may go and “mark.a ticket” any
time. Outside, there' is a shop-sign, something like
“Sun Hi, fancy goods,” such goods only consisting of
writing materials, blank tickets, books of closed banks,
and a chair or two. The yellow man evidently knows
that it pays to defy Vic. gambling laws, in spite of an
occasional set-back.
* * * *
The surprise of Saturday’s racing at Caulfield
was the comparatively long price offered against
Playaway for the Futurity Stakes. On paper _it looked
as though the English-bred daughter of Carbine must
have a great chance at 6.7 in the Futurity if there was
any reason for making her first favorite for the New-
market, where she gets comparatively the worst of the
weights Yet “ books” and punters held Playaway quite
cheaply, merely because the word had gone round that
Emir, the sensational Derby failure, couldn’t get beaten.
The same soft Emir, looking exactly as he looked in
November, was backed down to 5 to 4, whilst Playaway
receded to sto 1. And how did she win 1
• * * •
Playaway’s victory, Saturday, was third win in
succession of Caulfield Futurity Stakes by N.S.W. trained
horses. Sir Foote and Sir Leonard scored previously.
Playaway is a 4yr.-old daughter of Carbine, by the St.
Simon mare Concertina, and unquestionably the fastest
over a short distance Australia’s whilom champion has
sired. Concertina was bred by the Duke of Portland, her
dam, Comic Song, being by Petrarch out of Frivolity, by
Macaroni.
* * * *
Although the time was not fast, it was good
enough considering the heavy rain that had fallen a few
hours previously. Pendant made her best pace from the
jump, with Independence, Mistral and Playaway nearest
in pursuit. Of the others, only Emir ever looked im-
portant. For an instant the Ballarat colt made a rush
towards the leaders, and then weakened, as usual. Ap-
parently Emir is a great natural galloper, lacking the
strength or the inclination to sustain an extra effort.
Anyway, the roar of Ikey Mo proclaimed the favorite
beaten just as Playaway flew past Independence and
Mistral caught Pendant and came away on her own. The
little mare won by, perhaps, five or six lengths, and re-
turned to the birdcage looking every inch a victress,
whereas Emir and some others were not particularly
gay after the race.
b J * * * s>
Opinions are bound to differ intelligently as to
the question whether Playaway is a “ cert” for the New-
market. Nobody can calculate the difference that an
extra 181 b. may make to so very small a mare. Pendant
meets her on 111 b. better terms ; Mistral (a daughter of
Aura) will have an advantage of 141 b. ; Bonnie Chiel will
have an even bigger difference of weight in his favor,
likewise Independence. One of them may be revenged
on Plavaway when they come scampering along the
straight six furlongs at Flemington next Saturday. On the
other hand there is no evidence that the moderate weight
of 7.11 will stop Playaway any more than it has stopped
other flying ponies. The little ’un is a Carbine, and she’s
no longer a chicken in years. Probably the extra 181 b.
on her still light-weighted back, will not go for much
more than the extra 71b. on top-weight Pendant. This
scribe expects Playaway to win the Newmarket Handicap
from Mistral, Katoomba, and Pendant. The market
price is short against the favorite, or against anything
else with a palpable chance. But, then, the probability
of a “turn-up” is hardly worth considering this year.
Better to lick honey off thorns than to drop a sovereign
into the soup.
* * * *
Mo dies hard. After paying over many of the
bets in connection with Marmont’s apparent win of the
Bond Cup, that animal was disqualified and the race
was awarded to Proceedor. Then some of the bedizened
Noses asked those who backed Proceedor with them to
accept their money less 30 per cent., to make up for the
“ books’ ” financial mishap over Marmont. Bond Cup is
an unlucky vessel for Marmont’s owner, . He won same
race last year with Falkirk, only to see him break down
immediately after. Proceedor has been a gold mine to
Mr W. H. Jones since he purchased him for 15 guineas
from Mr. Jas. Wilson, who had broken the nag into
harness and was wasting his great possibilities between
the shafts of a buggy. r
The Australian Cup, on Tuesday next, should
be an interesting race as regards the 3-year-olds. At
time of writing Lord Cardigan (8.8), F.J.A. (8.10) ana
Scottish King (7.12) are likely starters, and the last-
named is favorite at 2to 1, not because his “form is
within ten and twelve pounds of the “form” of the
Melbourne Cup and Derby winners, but because they
are expected to be troubled by a first-class weight
in a 24 miles race, whilst Scottish King will not
feel his lighter burden at all. Leslie Macdonald’s
thundering big colt is now assumed to be actually
better at w.f.a. than several colts—not to mention
the filly Sweet Nell— that were thought to hold
him quite safe four months ago. A lot of people will
want to see Scottish King win a race on Saturday before
thinking him a genuine good thing for the Australian
Cud Thusfar he has made his reputation in slow-run
races over 9 and 11 furlongs. Lord Cardigan, at any rate,
should beat the King next Tuesday. Patronus Elderslie
and Seaport have chances, but if Marmont (6.12) comes to
the post in his best condition, and gets a fair run, the
hurdle-racer will win easily, this scribe predicts.
Present warm favorite, Scottish King, for Aus-
tralian Cup will surely prove better than his supporters
assess him ; he didn’t look much more than half fit for
the Y A.T.C. meet, though great things were done by
him there. Scottish King is a splendid sample of the
massive thoroughbred, standing 16.1 and built in pro-
portion. # . *

How is this for a scoop ? At Alberton and
Yarram (Vic.) race meetings recently, Mr. G. Collis’s
horses started in nine races and won them all; since the
year began same owner’s nags have been first past the
judge in every one of 15 starts.
* * * *
Wren’s tote is now doing business as usual at
the old address—the one from which it ejected Irvine’s
policeman upon his ear, and out of which it threw his
clothes and his gun in derision. While Irvine’s police-
man was in charge of the premises the tote did business
at a new address, which wasn’t much difference. If
Irvine or Bent really wanted to suppress the unlawful
tote a much milder measure than the Strike Suppression
Bill would do it, but presumably nobody is deeply in-
terested.

Final acceptances (Monday) for Newmarket
Handicap left only 17 of the 43 that survived the first
forfeit. This is due to exceptional form shown during
past week or two by several nags engaged—notably Play-
away and Pendant. Only twice in the event’s thirty
years’ history have smaller fields lined up—Maid of
Avenal’s year (1874) saw 9 strip, and two years later, when
Sultan won, there were 15 starters. Largest muster was
37 in 1882. Most notable of Monday’s malcontents was
whilom brilliant Emir, who ruled favorite for some time.
Despite the rush, Saturday last, for United States (in
same stable as Emir), scratching pen was also put through
his name. If an outsider is to get home, it might be
Mairp or Latchkey

A great many trotters run until they attain a
green old age. Ida and Sultan, who ran first and second
in an event in Northern M.L. lately, were 18 and 22 years
old respectively.
* • * *
“0.K.” . There’s no gainsaying the fact that
Warner’s men, who seemed to have shot their bolt after
their lucky win in the second test, have lately played in
a bolder and more masterly style than any visiting Eng-
lish team since the days of Shaw and Shrewsbury. They
are hitting more freely than they did, and they “came at
the right end ” of the return matches with Vic. and
N.S.W.—a quite startling development of.,pluck and
stamina in a modern English team when,pitted against
Australians. To my way of thinking, the match con-
cluded in Sydney on Monday week, where the visitors
scored 651 agrfins't the 373 of N.S.W.; and polished them
off within three days, was equal to the most dashing
performance of any Australian eleven in England, and far
superior, in respect of pluck, to anything shown by the
Stoddart or Maclaren teams in Australia. With 6 wickets
down for 92 in their first innings the visitors were
already playing an uphill game, and when the two
“ ruck ” men, Hirst and Bosanquet, had given the score a
useful lift along towards 190 it was “ up against ” their
bonders to prevent N.S.W. getting a big lead. To win by
278 runs in the face of a first innings of 190 only was a
most un-English proceeding, and to do this without
getting much assistance from their recognised “crack”
bats and bonders was an astonishing feat—for the visitors.
Rhodes took only 3 wickets for 70 ; Arnold was standing
out of it. The first five bats didn’t score a fourth of the
sum total, whereas the three bowlers (Bosanquet, Hirst,
and Rhodes) made 303 runs among them, the latter scor-
ing 51 and carrying his bat each time. Another 127 runs
were credited to Knight, who is usually left out of the
big games. Looking at these figures and noting the
improvement in the bowling averages of Bosanquet and
Hirst since the first test, there is no encouragement to
fancy Australia for the rubber. The writer put his own
£1 on Australia before the series was started, and he
reckons that its present value on paper is not more than
2s. 6d.
* * * *
At Bathurst (N.S.W.) last week colored bowler
Marsh was at last allowed to flesh his leather on a real
live English team. Hirst and Knight w’ere caught off him
for 10 and 19 respectively. He bowled Warner, Fielder, .
Strudwick, and Lilley fqr 5,4, 0, .and 7 respectively.
For the first English innings his record was 96 balls, 55
runs, 1 maiden,“s wickets. Second innings 1 wicket for
36 runs. Altogether the brown man made a real good
showing with the ball, but went ignominio'usly for a
duck with the bat.
* * * *
Diamond, the captain of the Western District
(N.S.W.) team which met the Englishmen at Bathurst,
having won the toss, wisely went to the wicket first. The
innings resulted in 248. The Englishmen only succeeded
in putting together 176. The match being limited to two
days, the local men closed their second innings for 151,
which had cost them but three wickets. Consequently,
when play closed, the Englishmen had lost five wickets
and scored only 115 ; so that, though it ended in a draw,
the result was very much in favor of the local men. C.
Gregory’s 139 in the first innings was a very high-class
performance. English bowling was weakened by the
absence of Rhodes and Hirst, the latter deciding to rest
his arm. . »
* * * * *
Sydney’s first-grade cricket produced nothing
very startling on Saturday last, although, for the
University against Waverley, E. F. Waddy put up
118 before Newell succeeded in bowling him, and
Fisher carried his bat for 112. On the other hand,
the bowling does not seem to have been deadly, and
anyone can make a good score if the bowling is suffi-
ciently feeble and erratic. Even a blind man might do
something if the bowler were dead.
* * * *
Despite Warner’s insistance that Sydney’s fast
bowler Marsh is a real “ shier,” and his expressed belief
tnat Crockett would no-ball him, fact remains that the
aboriginal sent lightning-like ones in from either end at
Bathurst Friday and Saturday, and not a single delivery
was taken exception to. Marsh’s swift balls and those of
the local crack (Kenna) were not at all relished by the
Englishmen. The resemblance to cannon balls was too
strong.
* • * *
Probable that Sydney express bowler Cotter, if
selected, will not be able to play in fourth test, com-
mencing Friday ; his shoulder trouble is worse than at
first thought. On Saturday he couldn’t raise the injured
arm above his head, and how the cure necessary to see
him through such an exacting ordeal as a big match is
going to be effected in the time is a mystery, unless
Dowie fixes him up by faith.

' * * * *
S.A. League of Wheelmen held their carnival at
Adelaide Oval, last Saturday, in excellent weather, but
the crowd was meagre and the racing far from exciting.
Taylorand Ivor Lawson were both absent owing to the little
trouble in Melbourne the previous Wednesday evening;
consequently the show’ was a sort of benefit for Floyd
MacFarland. He mopped up everything he started
for, and he started for everything available. The pie-
eater of romance wasn’t a circumstance beside Mac* ar-
land ; he gathered in the six big events, and never gave
anybody else a show. The pie-eater did occasionally
watch another fellow eat a pie to see what the process
looked like. This success of the Yankee wheelists in Aus-
tralia grows monotonous. Although MacFarland had to
meet men like Scheps and Walker, he won easily. In the
Northcote Stakes he conceded Walker 10 yards and Good-
soil 40. The secret seems to lie in the final sprint, the
culminating jump. Until Australian riders develop more
of the kangaroo characteristic at the final the all-fired
Yankee will go on accumulating Australian gold.
* * * *
London Sporting Life describes the Austral
Wheel Race as “ the most classic of cycle races held
annually in Sydney (Australia), on the grass track belong-
ing to the Melbourne Cricket Ground.”
* * * *
People in a position to see how it happened
declare that Major Taylor’s “ bad fall ” at Melbourne
Exhibition Oval was the result of his riding right into
Lawson wheh the latter had a slight lead and was holding
it. Having come to grief, as the result of accident, or
collision with his opponent, Major Taylor appar-
ently thought himself more hurt than he was. A
cyclist, in racing condition, is not knocked out of time
by a fall which breaks no bones. Neither is he im-
mediately conscious of any bruises or scraping he may
have received. In the excitement of the moment he
naturally picks himself up, but Major Taylor “ lay gasp-
ing on the track.’’ Couldn’t move without assistance.
Tragic spectacle. The Major in his character of damaged
hero is highly impressive.
* *
The Ivor Lawson-Taylor affair is shrouded in
mystery. On the night of Wednesday, the 17th, the pair
met on Melbourne Exhibition track. Lawson made a
tremendous bound and led Taylor by several lengths.
The darkie made a splendid effort to overtake his rival,
but just as he neared Lawson’s wheel went down smash,
and Lawson, continuing, was adjudged the winner.
Then the “no race” signal was hoisted: Taylor had
charged Lawson with interfering with him. The com-
mittee, after investigation, disqualified Lawson for 12
months. It’s a heavy sentence for a man in Lawson’s
position, but if he really did bring Taylor down not one
whit too severe-dri fact, for bringing down a competitor
while travelling at such a speed six months gaol seems
a fair thing. But no one can understand how Lawson
could have done it. The disqualifying Victorian body
is dumb as an oyster, and no one connected with cycling
in Sydney can get one iota of information from it.
Seeing that Lawson was in front, and the darkie was not
interfered with in passing (which the fact that he nearly
overtook Lawson seems to prove) no one seem quite to
know how the man in front knocked the fellow
behind off his equilibrium, unless he threw a brick back
at him—and, if so, where is the brick? MacFarland
narrowly escaped disqualification at the hands of the
same body, and now that Lawson has gone the coming
Sydney Thousand meeting will be shorn of a lot of
interest.
* * * *
The action of the stewards at Adelaide in fining
MacFarland ss. for looking behind him during his heat of
the Northcote Stakes was highly commendable. It’s a
dangerous habit, and has caused more smashes on the
cycling track than anything else. Moreover, it’s a habit
which, once contracted, the ordinary cyclist seems unable
to leave off. Blinking is nothing to it, and it seems to be
more insidious than the blinking habit in women. Major
Taylor never turns his head over his shoulder ; he bends
down and looks under his arm. This isn’t so easy to
notice, but it’s just as dangerous, and a new rule should
be passed by all the cycling leagues that any rider caught
looking back under any circumstances should be com-
pelled thereafter to ride in blinkers.
* * * *
“ Plunge”: Stated in last week’s Australasian
that “ Wickham has introduced a new form of racing. It
consists in running along the bottom of the baths. He
comes up to breathe now and again.” A man ■who can
“ run” along the bottom, or even walk, without a heavy
weight to keep him there, shouldn’t want to breathe at
all, unless as fish do.
• * * *
Cecil Healey’s 25 l-ssec. for the 50 yards swim
at Rushcutter’s Bay baths (Saturday) was 2-ssec. better
than Wickham’s world record. On the other hand, it was
l-ssec. slower than a sprint Wickham put up since, but
which, owing to the absence of A.S.A. timekeepers, was
not officially’ recognised. If Cecil can keep going he’ll
probably earn a place amongst the world’s best.
* * * *
“Nat.”: So called “distance diving,” which is
really swimming under water, though long ago abolished
in N.S.W. and M.L., is still allowed in Victoria. At
Maryborough S. C. gala Cartridge covered 100 ft., and
whiie rising fell forward. Rescuers found him on the
bottom unconscious and face downwards, but, luckily,
not beyond resuscitation. It was such happenings which
led to the abolition of the practice in N.S.W. and
Seddonia.
* * * *
Rowing is reviving on the Clarence River
(N.S.W.), and there are hopes that the district may ere
long produce another champion to win back the laurels
lost on the death of Searle. The first wager-boat race
for years past will be rowed at Maclean on Easter Monday,
for Clarence and Richmond scullers only. On the same
day John Philp and Ernest Fischer row-in working boats
for £5O a-side. The first amateur championship for years
was rowed on January 26, the winner being Jack Mitchell,
of South Grafton, who will probably, in the near future,
abandon the amateur ranks and engage in professional
rowing. He has a younger brother who is also coming
on well. Rowing seems to run in families on the Clar-
ence. Five brothers of the Shannon family rowed in one
race recently. Four M’Namaras were asked to represent
South Grafton in the recent water brigade championship
at Newcastle (won by South Grafton), but only two could
go. The Fischer family also furnishes a quartette of
oarsmen.
♦ * * *
Hunter River sculler, Tresidder, sick of trying
to draw world’s champion Towns out for a match, has
claimed the premiership, and put up £soas solid evidence
that he is ready to argue the point with the first comer
for £5OO a side. If the other party hails from outside
Australia £5O expenses will be allowed. Though Towns
won the championship two years ago he hasn’t since
noticed a challenge. Unfortunately, Tresidder's claim
won’t be recognised ; there are no rules—absolutely
none _limiting the time during which a champion
may remain such and still refuse challenges. Gaudaur
snapped his fingers at all comers, and only rowed when
and where it suited him, and in accordance with con-
ditions laid down by himself. A time may yet come
when a man will win the championship at 20, and then
sit on his laurels for 40 years, and die at 60, still champion.
* * * *
Sydney bowling Pennant tourney is now in
final stage. Close of Saturday’s play saw so far unbeaten
City Club account for Victoria Park by 9 after a close
game. Last-named was in front at the end of two hours’
play, but in next and concluding half-hour.lost 14 points.
Petersham and City meet in the final Saturday week ;
former will have to win twice to succeed, while if beaten
onee it drops out, and City secures supremacy.
* ♦ * *
A Kerang (Vic.) “sportsman,” who was lately
fined £7 for duck-shooting with a punt gun, was using a
piece of artillery weighing 1181 b., measuring 18ft. from
butt to muzzle, and with a 2in. bore ! And he regarded
himself as a sportsman—with his hundredweight of
cannon.
* » » ♦
“Cross”: That referee who declared the
Murphy-Keenan fight at Kalgoorlie a draw because the
men fell out of the ring simply didn’t know his business.
The* fight should have gone on, and the ropes been
replaced while it proceeded. Even if that could not
be done the fight should have gone on just the same.
The thing has happened in Sydney and other places in
Australia several times, but never before did any one
declare the scrap a draw on that account
* » * »
Lightweights Casey and J. Barr fought 17
cheerful rounds at Sydney National Sporting Club last
week. Barr’s seconds, realising their man was utterly
done, reluctantly threw the haberdashery into the ring,
but before Casey realised the fact he had dropped Barr
with a crash on the point. This was the first show Casey
had for about a year and he made the best of it. Why
the gritty little fighter should have been frozen out is a
mystery ; his merit deserves better recognition.
Sydney owns a young pug. from the oppressed
country in the person of Denis Moloney—as tough a piece
of humanity as the game knows. He isn’t clever, but
hitting him seems about as futile as hitting the Tower of
London. Denis never goes out without inches of a bright
green handkerchief hanging from his outside breast
pocket and an expansive necktie of the same hue spread
well across his wide display of shirt-front. He has
already fought 27 battles, and had only three given
against him —all on points.
In a head-line during the last few days
the Daily Telegraph has called Australia and
M.L. “Britain’s Butcher,” and the S.M. Herald
has called them “John Bull’s Butcher.” Won’t
these papers ever let us forget our S’African
crime ?
There is trouble over the Tas. House of
Reps, election—only five contests, and two peti-
tions are notified, and Braddon has vacated a
third seat. Brickhill versus the O’Malley, elect of
Darwin, will be one fight, and t’other will see
Norman Cameron trying to oust Fysh. Fysh and
Cameron were parted by less than a scor.e of votes
at the finish.
A curious announcement in the Sydney
papers of last Tuesday: —
The Railway Commissioners have had under considera-
tion the question of employees being allowed to smoke in
"uniform while proceeding to and from duty. Numerous
complaints have been made to the Commissioners about
the unseemliness of the practice, and it has now been
decided to frame a regulation prohibiting officials from
smoking when in uniform, whether on duty or not.
The Bulletin would like to inspect the heads
of the “numerous ’’ persons who have complained
about the “unseemliness” of a tramway man
smoking when he is off duty but has his official
clothes on. Surely the awful dignity of a twenty-
five shilling blue suit can stand the strain, just
as well as "an ordinary citizen’s £5 brown suit.
If smoking is such a disgrace that it is calculated
to bring the threepenny tramc-ar down with
horror to the grave, then the Commissioners
shouldn’t smoke themselves, and should denounce
smoking at all times—or else they should get a
stronger tramcar that can stand the shock. A tram-
way man has the same right as any other citizen to
smoke in his own time, even if he hasn’t yet got
home and changed those official clothes that
seem to be so easily disgraced—he can’t change
them in front of the car, so he has to go home in
them. Most people will consider that these
41 numerous ” complainants are a lot of meddlesome
old cats, and that if the Commissioners listen to
them they will thereby show themselves three
silly old women.

EARLY MORNING CATARRH.
In an editorial on Catarrh in the last number of
the “ Review of Nose and Throat Diseases” there
is mentioned the tendency of catarrhal diseases to
become specially troublesome in the morning.
This number of the “Review” also contains a
“Supplement” giving the details on a large
number of cases of catarrh caused by a new
method, which has been successful where operative
proceeding and cauterisations have failed. There
is, likewise, a report form, by hieans of which
patients suffering from deafness, or ear, nose, or
throat disease can obtain free advice by post.
The “Review ” is published in London, England,
but a free copy can be obtained by sending name
and address to :—“Review” c/o Box 241 G.P.0.,
Sydney.*
George Swinburne, whom Bent has in-
duced by cable to join him in his Ministry, will
have his hands pretty full if he keeps all the
irons in the fire that he has there at present;
for, besides a private engineering practice, he is a
director of more co.’s than probably any other
man in Australia. It is hardly reckoned the
right thing in Victoria to leave him off the board
of any gas co., and in N.S.W. he is a director of
the Sydney Hydraulic Power Co. on one side,
and of the Broken Hill Water Supply Co. on the
other. And he still finds time to give the Wes-
leyans round Melbourne many a lift. Bent wanted
him for a Railway Commissioner, but, apart from
other things, the pay wasn’t good enough to tempt
him.
Gory war has only lately arrived at its
true commercial purpose. It was designed oy
Providence in order that printing firms might
publish war maps at the ridiculously small price
of one shilling. Sands and M'Dougall, of Mel-
bourne, who busily improved the blazing hour
when the Boer war was about, have now issued a
war map of the Far North. This shows the
routes of railways already opened in the yellow-
brown lands, and gives a table of distances by
rail and sea, from which an enthusiastic student
may calculate the time required for bringing Japs
and Russians into murderous assault and battery
at various points.
THE COLINTON ESTATE, QUEENSLAND.
Matters are beginning to get a bit lively at Oolinton.
People are arriving to inspect from all parts, and in order
to line! accommodation the auctioneers, Messrs. Isles,
Love, and Company, have arranged with a well-known
firm of contractors to erect an enormous pavilion of wood
and iron, measuring 60ft. x 114 ft., which will contain
about 120 bunks, as well as dining and kitchen con-
veniences. The first detachment of the Salvation Army
are leaving Brisbane on Monday next to take charge of
the catering, and the well-known writers, " Koraji ” and
“ Delphia” are at present enjoying a trip over the estate
with the idea of contributing articles concerning same to
the leading Brisbane dailies. The running of the Esk i rain
through to the Cressbrook terminus (which is only six
miles from the Colinton boundary), which commenced
last Monday, will greatly convenience persons going up
to inspect, and as has already betn announced, all
travellers to the estate who bear a written certificate
from the auctioneers, can book from all parts of Queens
land to Cressbrook at excursion fares, provided that the
distance to be travelled is upwards of twelve miles. The
circulation of the illustrated pamphlet, issued in con-
nection with the estate, has now reached the enormous
total of upwards of 170,000. We would strongly advise
persons who contemplate purchasing some of this grand
property to inspect as early as possible as if everybody
comes with a rush, just before the sale, it will overtax the
guides and station horses and cause much inconvenience.*
♦
Sydney Even Nooze, in a notice of the
Dook of Gampridge, says that that warrior com-
manded the first division of the British army
throughout the Crimean campaign, which is not
true. The Dook commanded the brigade of
Guards, a part of the first division, and was
present only at the Battle of Alma. He never
saw either Inkermann, Balaklava or Sebastopol
Sky-blue funk was said to have set in after the
Alma, and the ducal warrior returned home on
“ urgent private affairs.” He left one good story
behind him. At the Alma he is said to have fled
wildly to Lord Raglan and reported in shocked
astonishment, “They’re firing on the Guards.”
The idea of the Russians shooting at that expen-
sively-clothed regiment looked like sacrilege to
Gampridge. Apparently he didn’t expect to be
shot at.

A highly appropriate incident is recorded
from Warsaw. One Count Demensky became
suddenly demented in the course of a patriotic
demonstration, and he relieved his fervor by
blazing away at the crowd with revolvers. Having
killed three and wounded 20 of his fellow patriots,
the Count was arrested in an attempt to shoot
himself. Jingoism is so essentially bloodthirsty
that Demensky’s example may be commended to
Australian emulation. Next time the stay-at-
home citizens of this country have a mind to
maffick for the glory of the Empire, they can
enjoy all the horrors of war by firing at a stock-
brokers’ procession. Every display of patriotic
feeling radiates nowadays from a gathering of
stock-jobbers armed with paper flags and tin
trumpets.
About an old Speck identity :
William Hart, M.L.O. (Tas.), who died last week, and
whose estate will run into six or seven figures, started
life in Launceston 60 years ago as a tinsmith in a small
way. There are a few people in Launceston old enough
to remember Hart, with an apron on, hammering the
bottom on to a tin can or soldering up a hole in a veteran
frying-pan. His father brought him from London when
he was eight years old. Hart, sen., started in the tin-
ware line in the northern town of Tasmania in the early
’3o’s. Father and son parted when the latter came of
age. Hart, jun., never lost his old interest in tin. He
was chairman of the big Mt..Bischoff co. when he died.
He also had a weakness for gold. He was the original
purchaser of the Tasmanian G.M. co. at Beaconsfield, and
was a director of the old co., as well as of the newco. lately
formed. Hart kept his seat in the House of Landlords
till he died. He was absent from the House the whole of
last session, and his seat became forfeited. Then one or
two wire-pullers at Launceston got up a requisition asking
the old man to stand for re-election, though they knew
very well he would never see Hobart again. He was
induced to “ stand,” though he was lying on his death-
bed, and was re-elected without opposition. Hart was a
devoted Methodist, and has left £5OOO to that body. The
old man was 82 when he died, and was one of the half-
dozen men in the Tas. House of Landlords whose united
ages made 500 years.
English papers record the death in London
of Dr. William Peirce, formerly medical superin-
tendent of N.S.W. Coast Hospital and lately Port
health officer at Sydney. The deceased gentle-
man was in private life a student, a collector of
old engravings and antiquities, a numismatist,
and an artist of no mean skill. He came of a
family whose members have been physicians for
over 150 years. After taking his M.D. at Dublin
he made a voyage in a yacht as physician to its
millionaire owner. He afterwards visited the
chief ports of the world, and while on his voyages
cultivated with much success the artistic instinct
that was strong within him. His sketches,
whether in pencil, pen and ink, or oils, were
characterised by great attention to detail and rare
delicacy of touch. He once showed the writer a
color sketch of a veritable tropical paradise marred
by unsightly buildings. On inquiring for the
name of the place he told me it was his idea of
what could be done to beautify the grounds of the
Coast Hospital. As a work of imagination it was
magnificent. For many years past his work was
mainly official, but a paper he wrote on the treat-
ment of pyrexia was widely quoted and reprinted
in America. He was a quaint, kindly old man,
almost a recluse, but he had a Spartan idea of
duty. During the plague scare, and at a time
when he was suffering agonies from a painful com-
plaint, he continually exposed himself in all
weathers in the performance of his duties, and so
undermined his constitution. He left Australia a
few months ago to place himself under a specialist
in London. The deadly English winter was too
much for his enfeebled frame, and he died of
pneumonia on the last day of the year.
“G.H.’’ : There was no “misconception ’
in rny par. anent John Farrell (B. 4/2/’O4).
Farrell certainly was a “worker all the time ’’ ;
but the man who works with a protest-label
attached to him is very uninspiring. I again say
that had he (Farrell) worked like fury, with the
strong man’s delight in making the chips fly, and
the anvil sway and ring, he might have hewn out
a paper of his own that would have towered above
the D.T. and S.M. H. as £5 towers above 3d
“Gentle contempt ” for the bustle and bang and
whirr of the modern world is all mighty fine for
phrasing purposes ; but in actual practice against
the said hurrying world it doesn’t amount to a
banana-husk.

The “ bell-topper,” after having enjoyed
an arbitrary reign for nearly a century, is gradually
disappearing from London streets. Cycling,
golf and motoring are cited as the principal
c luses of the decadence. The old “ chimney-
pbt, ” or “ tile, ” or “ lum “ as it used to be called
in Scotland, or “ nail-keg •’ as the Yankees termed
it, has been called by names of derision for many
years It stood all that; but it can’t stand the
new methods of fast travelling. They make the
lofty abomination blow off.
“Scallywag”: Your sub-leader (4/2/’O4)
referring to mode of Japanese subsistence
set me on to statistics re a man I know
down this way ( iouth Coast of N.S.W.).
He gets 10s. a week as a farm-hand, and has a
wife and eight children. This salary pans out
approximately Is. 6d. per day for the family, or
ijd. daily to feed and clothe each individual. The
wage-earner works every day, Sunday included.
He gets Xmas Day, Good Friday, and one show
day off during each year. And there’s no offal
for the family down this way. His employer is a
large land-owner.
A quaint matrimonial tangle was disclosed
at West Ham (London) police court, t’other day.
Rebecca Redhead, the party in trouble, was
charged with bigamy as regards Henry Foster,
who had been co-respondent when her husband
(Redhead) got a decree nisi against the lady. The
giddy she married her loving co. before the decree
was made absolute, wherein lay the offence. Sub-
sequent complications had nothing to do with the
case, but they were quoted for the edification of
Bench and public. Co-respondent Foster, having
married Mrs. Redhead before she was legally quit
of her one and only husband, went to S’Africa as
a kharkie hero Returning to England, he found
that his unlawful “wife” had perpetrated a
second bigamous marriage with another fellow ;
also the original Redhead had a shock when he
learned that-his wife’s mother, believing him dead,
had married his father. Redhead, thus far, has
not troubled to complete his divorce proceedings,
consequently his father is his father-in-law, his
mother : in-law is his step-mother, and his wife is
his step-sister. The two men who were unlawfully
married to his wife can be classed as family con-
nections all round.

hand, they had the propertied party, who hacl no
community of interest.
The propertied people aren’t even linked together
by a common yearning to look after their
property, it seems.

Business, Bobbery, etc.
C.C.H.’ : The little faith I ever had in the
human race is dead. Had a holiday last Wednesday. Wife
went out shopping, and I looked after the house —or tried
to. Sat down with a book, intending to spend a quiet after-
noon. Heard a knock at the back gate ; young fellow
selling door-mats, or rather door-mat—he only had one
left. Told him the wife had bought one for the front
door the previous week. He said he’d let me have it dirt
aheap, half-a-crown. Remembered then the wife had
paid Bs. 6d. for hers, and it didn’t look a scrap better
than this one ; also remembered that she said she’d get
another for the back door when she had the cash to spare.
Asked the man how he could sell so cheaply. He said he
made them himself, and was content with small profits.
1 bought the mat, put it down at the back door, and stood
off a bit to admire the effect. Then I began to ruminate
on the enormous profits made by furniture warehouse
people. I would compare the two mats, and if I could
find no difference in size or quality I would writs to the
papers showing how the people are robbed. I went to
the front door for the other mat. It wasn’t there. Took
another hurried look at the back-door mat. It was ths
front-door mat.
• s s s
“Eiddal”: Maya kindly Providence protect
the cockle from the malleable iron castings that “ Chas-
selas”(B. 4/2/’O4) dovetails into his creed of cheapness
and utility. You rarely hear of a breakdown in the
Australian-built combined harvesters, ploughs, etc., but—
as with the Yankee watches, lawn-mowers, sausage-meat
machines, bicycles, phonographs, etc., etc. —in agricul-
tural tools built on “ Chass’s ” light and airy principles,
first cost is not the last cost by long chalks. Yankee
goods pass through so many hands that there is little
profit left the retailer. But a kindly manufacturer has his
eye on him—the breakages bring in good profits. Break-
downs must occur where malleable iron is used for the
cog-wheels and parts that bear a strain. The Yankee has
a playful way of making the parts that should be soft
hard, and the hard parts soft! Hence the frequency of
breakages, wideness of bearings, and general and accursed
ramshackleness, of imported machinery, that is run
together without regard to local requirements. It’s
a good job the Australian articles are protected by letters
patent, otherwise the farmer, in his eternal chase for
cheapness, would fall-in worse than ever over the wheat-
bagging, labor-saving machines.

Stawed (Vic.) Winery and Brandy Co.’» pro-
perty, which cost shareholders, mostly German, nearly
£20,000, was recently sold for less than the amount of
subsidy it had received from the Victorian Government —
£2OOO. The then-languishing wine industry was to have
been revived by granting subsidies of £2OOO to co-opera-
tive wineries which purchased 20,000ga1. of wine, Of
grapes representing that quantity, for three successive
years. Four such wineries were established, costing the
country at least £BOOO. Only one showed even a sign of
success.

There is trouble again in the sugar-growing
districts of N.S.W. through a move on the park of the
Colonial Sugar Co. to reduce the price of cane. The
monopoly of the trade enjoyed by this co. gives it a very
strong position ; but its advantage, if pushed too far,
may result in competition.

From World’s Work : —“A professional man,
unaccustomed to the ways of the city, was the other day
in the office of the manager of a leading London bank.
Suddenly the door opened and the head and shoulders of
a customer entered. ‘Fifty?’remarked the customer to
the manager. ‘ Yes,’ was the reply. * Week ? ’ was the next
question. The manager nodded. ‘Quarter?’ continued
the customer. ‘ Yes,’ from the banker. ‘ Right,’ and the
head and shoulders were withdrawn. ‘ Pardon me,’ said
the visitor, ‘ but, might I ask what all that meant?' ‘lt
meant,’ replied the banker, ‘ that I have arranged to lend
him £50,0C0 for a week at 3| per cent.’ This is said to
be a fair illustration of the methods of modern banking
in the city.”
* * * *
“D’Harum”: Recently chatted with an English
sailor out of a berth. “ Yes, I think I’ll get back to
London,” he said. “ I’ll go down to the Bluff (M.L.) and
wait for a boat there.” “ And work your passage, I pre-
sume.” “ Work me passage ? No fear 1 All I have to do
is to wait there for a sailing boat on its way back. When
one comes I give a sailor four or five pounds to desert.
As I m about the onlj' man obtainable in the town, and it
is necessary for the captain to take on another hand, I
get the snap at a wage that I name myself. The sailor
who deserted wjits for the next boat generally, bribes
another sailor to desert, gets his job, and makes a nice
little sum on the run back.”
“C.T.C.’ (Southern Cross, Westralia) writes:
“ A recent Bulletin par in reference to a battery made
of wood brings to mind the wooden crusher at Parker’s
Range, constructed by Jack Douglas about 13 years ago.
It is still standing, solid and impervious to the ravages
of time. The old man chopped and squared the wood-
work from bush timber, and the only iron about it is a
much rust-eaten stamp and die, which were brought by
him from Southern Cross, 33 miles away. The stamper
box is an up-ended log about 3ft. in diameter,
with a large hole scooped in it to hold the die.
In this log an aperture was chiselled out for
feeding purposes. To drive the battery, the ingenious
Douglas manufactured a wind-mill, also out of
bush timber—a large, cumbersome, but withal effective,
arrangement equal in size to an ordinary four-roomed
house. With this eccentric combination he treated
specimen stuff from a small leader on his lease, and made
a very fair pile before he decided to down tools and quit
the district. Everyone going to Parker’s Range pauses to
gaze in wonderment at Douglas’ weird device. The
lease where it stands was, with a number of others, taken
up some months ago by Frank Gardiner, of Perseverance
fame, but was abandoned again after a very lame
prospecting effort.
• * * •
“XXX”: Baldness becomes unpleasant when
a man passes 40. Up to that age it wins him the reputa-
tion of being a brain worker, afterwards of being an
“ ancient.” Now, to ask the hairy one for the best cure
for baldness is to seek an opinion from an inexperienced
quarter ;to ask the hairless is a gratuitous insult. I got
over the difficulty by consulting the press. A “cure *
which claimed to have been used by the crowned
" heads ” of Europe was recommended by an “ Isobel ” in
answer to “ Afflicted ’’ in the ladies’ column :
“ 1 Afflicted ’: Use Madame Lenie’s remedy. It’s the
best for the hair affliction and is used by royalty. I
have tried it, with success.”
I got a bottle, with its French label, from the nearest
drug shop, and passed a wheeze to the counter youth
that I wouldn’t get my hair off if the cure failed. I
experienced a mild sensation of toadyism as I vigorously
rubbed in the same hair cure simultaneously with the
“crowned heads.” I applied the “remedy” till my
remaining hair came away as with a mange. Before the
week was out my head resembled an egg, and I was a
by-word and a reproach to my friends. It was then that
a friendly interpreter came along and gave me a transla-
tion of the French label:—
“Madams Lenie’s Hair Core.—For the afflicted
woman. This cure is used by royalty and the ladies of
the court. It is guaranteed to remove every super-
fluous hair, and one bottle will remove every vestige of
growth.”
“ Madame ” had spoken the truth !

A MOST. DISTINGUISHED AWARD.
It is averred that the mantle of Merit fairly falls
upon those who earn it. The soldier on the battle-
field wins his “ Victoria Cross, the sailor his
“ Recognition of Bravery,” the thinker his ‘"Order
of Merit,” and thescientist his “Degree of Honor,”
significant of the intellectual altitudes to which
he has arisen, and the approbation of an admiring
world. Merit is the true award for arduous labor,
and exhaustive thought, in whatever form it may
be received, exercised for the benefit of an
individual or the general national good.
Australia is a nation, but young in years. Her
death rate in many classes of disease is abnormal.
Typhoid fever rages everywhere, and disease in
general form is publicly manifest. In the country
parts far from medical aid, men die yearly through
lack of medical treatment, and thousands of
premature deaths are recorded which increase
the death rate enormously. A decided improve-
ment has now asserted itself, a long felt want has
been supplied, by the introduction of the Parcel’s
Post system of medical treatment initiated years
ago by the Specialists associated with the medical
staffs of the Freeman and Wallace Medical Instit-
ute, Sydney, and at Bourke and Elizabeth-streets,
opposite General Post Office, Melbourne. It is
now possible for any man, whether he lives in the
farthest part of Cape Torres, or the wilds of
Western Australia, to receive the best advice and
treatment that professional skill can offer him.
This advice is given free, and all letters are replied
to with the promptitude associated at all times
with the great Institute and its medical experts.
Thousands of men are thus treated yearly by the
Specialists, and saved from early death and
premature decay. •
The Institute is unquestionably the medical
feature of Sydney and Melbourne. A quarter of a
century ago it first came before the Australian
public, and, though receiving strong opposition
from the medical profession, on account of its
anti-conservative principles, it passed greatly into
favor through the importance of its parcel post
services, and the great cures effected by its
Specialists. Its literature is in such great demand
that editions comprise millions of volumes of its
medical work, “ Clinical Experiences,” 570 pages,
150 medical plates, which are supplied to its
patients, and the volume of business accruing
from Victoria and the Western States has
necessitated the opening in Melbourne of one: of
the finest medical Institutes in the Southern
world. Some excellent photographs of the interior
and exterior of this health centre may be seen* by
referring to the firm’s advertisement on page 31.
Each Specialist associated with the Medical Staff
is a graduate from famous European and American
Colleges. The Physician-in-chief is-registered by
Australian, English, and American Medical
Boards, and he is a skilled diagnostician and an
author of distinguished degree. The supervision
of the great Melbourne Branch is under his direct
care. At its opening in January last some of
the most distinguished citizens of Melbourne
assembled to do the Specialists honor, and the
most influental newspapers of the city commented
favorably upon it. These great journals com-
prise the Aim us, Age, Herald, Australasian,
Leader, Punch, Truth, Tocsin, and Weekly
Times. Since the opening of this great Branch in
Melbourne, thousands of applications have been
received from suffering men and women from all
parts of Victoria, and the Specialists’services have
been solicited, and an era of usefulness entered
upon which argues well for the Victorian public
and the general benefit of humanity.*
The latest improvements in scientific dentistry
are being introduced to Melbourne by W. and W. E.
Thomas, who have opened a large and elaborately-appointed
establishment in Swanston-street, where aJ. O "* H su .^, n v 0
nublic can have undesirable moiars pulled and highly
desirable ones substituted on the most approved American
principles, and with the most moderate charges.*

Turon River Dredging 00. (N.S.W.) was one of
the most disastrous flotations for the public ever effected
in the State. The promoters took advantage of a boom-
tide, and made fortunes. By skilful Exchange operations
shares (36,000) were run up to over £3, and as the gullible
outsiders bought in so the knowing insiders sold out.
The boom lasted long enough for the insiders to get out,
or, if they didn’t, they are not the “ smart” men they are
credited with being, When the boom burst, shares dropped
to as many shillings as they had been pounds, and for a
long time past have been unsaleable. After working
years with two dredges, the co. declares its first dividend
—6d per share, or £9OO. Records are not available to
show what the total production has been, but as in 1903
the return was between £9OOO and £lO,OOO, it is fair to
assume that the aggregate has been over £35,000 The
£9OO div. is a meagre percentage of profits. Droughts
ajid unsuitable plant are the reasons assigned for the
ghastly disappointment. No dredging machine has been
invented that will pick up the gold from the rough
bfiftom of the Turon. The only appliances to get
irijbo the cracks and crannies are shovels and
scrapers, and these were vigorously used in olden
drought times. Directors state that plant is now arranged
oh an efficient and economical basis, and that steady
profits and di vs. may be confidently looked for; but it will
require something more than directors’ expressions of
confidence to induce the 1 public to nibble at Turons again.
* * • »
Strenuous efforts are being made to start a
boom in Hill End (N.S.W.) shows, and one cannot read
weekly reports from mines which have been recently
re-opened without remarking what arrant fools the origi-
nal owners or co.’s must have been. In every instance all
that is necessary to discover payable scone is to clear
olit the old workings ! The real test of payableness, how-
ever, has yet to come , and, meanwhile, Tub Bulletin’s
advice to people who are being pestered to buy into this
eihd that “ good thing ” is to wait, unless it be that they
dearly understand that they are speculating in purely
prospecting ventures, and that the money which they
may: subscribe will go into something deeper than
breeches pockets. Patriot politicians, whose only object
is “ their country’s good,” may prate about the hidden
treasures of Hill End, and may try to engineer schemes
to raise large sums of money to be spent in search of the
treasures, but all in vain. Investigation by practical men
will clearly demonstrate that no field in N.S.W. has been
more thoroughly prospected, and that for every ounce of
gold won another ounce in value has been spent in the
quest for more. “ ’Awkin’s 'lll ”is no longer a name to
conjure with.
* * * *
Why iron from scrap (£85,707) should be included
in the official returns of N.S.W. mineral production is
incomprehensible.
Wyalong has outlived an early prophecy that it
would be worked out before the railway reached there.
Last year’s yield totalled 19,1240 z., value £72,847, being
slightly less than in 1902. This is a drop from a good first
to a bad second to Cobar. Current year is likely to show
better results. Great things are expected from the
Barrier mine, where stoping is about to be started
from 300 feet on the long, rich shoot of ore
discovered south of the original workings. This
mine was included in a group held under
option for a couple of years by a Hongkong
syndicate, and abandoned by it after £7OOO
had been spent. The Barrier was immediately
taken on tribute by a party of miners, and in a few
months they bought it for a mere song. It is said that
in the syndicate’s time a “ hitch” w*as cut in the casing
of the new formation, and that at another point a round
of holes which were bored but not fired would have re-
vealed it.
* » * *
The sound of the hammer is to be heard again at
the Grassy Gully mine (N.S.W.)—but it will be that of
the auctioneer, not of the miner. This was the mine that
was going to cause gold-top to “ flow a fountain ” at
Cranbrook (now the State Governor’s residence). Divs.
were to be paid as soon as the battery started, and there-
after'were to be fat and frequent; but the non-imagina-
tive machinery soon dispelled all the lovely dreams, and
now sundry Sydney men are thirsting for blood, and
can’t get it.
♦ * # *
Oroya-Brown Hill, Kalgoorlie, is being worked
on the same lines as its neighbor, the Associated
Northern, used to be. Although no stoping is being
done, the bulk of the rich ore is being rooted out. For
instance, at No. 6 level (656 ft.), the first level at which
the rich chute was encountered from Pomeroy shaft,
four crosscuts east have been made into the lode. From
each of these crosscuts incline rises are being put up on
the rich ore, and from each of these, again, branch rises
are being put up. The average ore in these developments
ranges from 3oz. 17dwt. Bgr. to 9oz. 15dwt. 18gr. From
this it can be seen that the rich ore is being closely fol-
lowed and pretty well gutted. The incline winze below
No. 10 level (870 ft.) is down about 250 ft., or about 1000 ft.
vertical. The lode is so flat that 2fc. on the incline is
equivalent to Ift. vertical. The rich chute runs obliquely
through the lode at an angle of about one in four (but
gradually getting steeper) to the south, and for a depth,
from top to bottom, of about 400 ft., so that the winze
from No. 10 level will very soon be “below the values,”
or, as the late Captain Rodda picturesquely described it,
“ the bottom will be out of the mine." That’s where
Economist Curie erred. He inferred that the Oroya
chute would live down, because it carried “ very rich ore
at its deepest point, below 900 ft. vertical.'’ The Brown
Hill got “below the values” at about 400 ft., and the
Associated Northern at about 550 ft., and it is pretty cer-
tain that the Oroya is very near the bottom of the chute
now. It is worth noting that there are no values at No.
7 level, and the country between Nos. 6 and 8 appears
to be pretty nearly barren. The mine has still a big
future before it, but it is as well to keep in mind that
this lode practically carries no values except in the rich
chute, which is quite different from the position of the
other lodes on the belt. A crosscut has been carried
right across the lease at No. 9 level, a distance of 1140 ft.,
but nothing very encouraging was encountered.
* * * *
White Feather Main Reefs, Kanowna (W.A.),
has materially improved its position of late, the January
returns reading 15950 z. from 1740 tons. The month’s
profit was a shade over £3OOO, and, as the co. previously
had in hand the cash for a 6d. div. (£4000), a declaration
cannot be long delayed. The north drive on the east
reef at 700 ft. is in very good values, and is supplying
most of the stone. The chute at this point is dipping
northwards, the same as the original surface chute on
which the mine was floated. In the south end ,of the
property, at 200 ft., fairly promising results are being ob-
tained. The mine, on the whole, is looking well, but not
being in a fashionable district, the scrip is not in demand,
and is quoted at ss. to 6s. only
* « * *
During 1903 no less than 36 of J. Bull’s Westra-
Jian wild cats perished ; and, as some of these, like the
Limerick, date back to the beginning of 1895, they have
had nine years of life, even if they haven’t had nine
Hives. If the gone-bung Australian-owned co.’s were in-
cluded, last year must have seen at least 50 cats wiped
off. The current year should pretty well blot out the
-remainder, as shareholders’ money can’t last for ever.
* * * *
Ida H., Laverton (W.A.), is very poor in the
bottom, and it looks very much as if it would follow the
ilead of all the Laverton shows, such as British Flag,
‘Golden Rhine, Mt Barnicoat, etc., and quietly peter out.
:Shortly after the flotation the £1 shares, with ss. paid,
were quoted at £3 premium ; and, as the W.A. vendors’
-shares were not negotiable, owing to promoter Wadding-
rton’s cuteness, he had the market to himself. The split
shares are now practically unsaleable at par, which shows
what the market thinks of the property.
* * * *
Hainault, Kalgoorlie, was to issue 11,000 new
shares at par, the lists of applications to close on January
11. Great efforts were made to boom the scrip over par
in order to tempt shareholders to subscribe for the new
issue, but the attempt was a failure, and it is hardly
likely that the new shares were placed with cheaper
shares offering in the market. The proceeds of the new
issue were to go to liquidate co.’s debts and make some
’ small additions to the treatment plant. The profits only
average £B5O a month. The crosscut from the 1000 ft.
level of the Kalgurli, to test the Hainault ground at that
depth, has been suspended, and nothing is being done
below 650 ft. Scrip seems dear.
* * » *
Victory United, Cuddingwara (W.A.), con-
tinues to have marvellous luck. As already stated, the
. west crosscut at the 640 ft. level passed through ore at
89ft. from shaft worth Bto lOoz. per ton. In continuing
the crosscut the manager reports that, at 178 ft. from
shaft, a 2ft. reef worth 3oz. 12d\vt. was encountered. The
funny part of the business is that each new discovery has
merely helped to depress the scrip. A call of 3d.
synchronises with the latest rich discovery, which may
account for the milk in the cocoanut. The mill has been
running for some time, and the result of the first
crushing will prove how much confidence can be placed
in the glowing reports.

if he had not had the luck to get charge of a great mine
he might not have become a great manager. Nicholls, of
the Perseverance is to be banqueted next month How

The Barton’s Mine, Nullagine (W.A.), is owned
by four miners, who have erected a 10-head battery out
of the gold won, and are now adding a cyanide plant.
They have crushed 1000 tons for an average of 26dwt.,
and have satisfied themselves that the reef is permanent.
The Barton’s is surrounded by a number of locally-owned
shows, and Government is now erecting a 10-head battery.
Between Marble Bar and Nullagine—at Warrawoona —a
party of prospectors crushed recently 4£ tons for an
average of 970 z. Cost of cartage from the sea coast is,
however, £l7 per ton, and firewood is £3 10s. per cord,
and hard to get at that.
• * * *
With its copper-smelting furnace Mt. Morgan
(Q.)afew days ago commenced a new life. As a gold
mine pure and simple, of course the Queensland marvel
is not done yet; but its auriferous copper deposit and
smelting works will probably be heard most of in the near
future. When the plans already in existence are fully
carried out the monthly output should be 15,000 tons of
about 3 per cent, copper ore, carrying about 6dwt. gold,
worth in all, say, £40,000. The present output averages
95000 z. (it was 95240 z. in January, and an average of
94130 z. in the nine months before that), contained in ore
averaging about Bjdwt. ; and- the new class of stuff ought
to give quite as much profit as this is doing
* * * *
Queensland Mines Minister Browne has decided
that in future his department shall only talk about gold
in fine ounces. This is honest; the other thing—the
thing that has been going on for years—isn’t. A co. or a
paper, barracking for its district, may call something gold
that contains 30 per cent, silver and 10 per cent, copper,
but a State is hardly justified in backing up the deception
in its official publications.
• * * •
“ Leveson ”: Bulletin (24/12/03) drew attention
to the late Queensland Government’s grant of assistance
to prospect for coal (by boring) near Townsville.
The area is situated at Stewart’s Creek, and is held by
people who have flourished during the Philp distribu-
tion, and who have not, as far as I can ascertain, spent a
shilling themselves in testing the mythical coal measures.
I say mythical; and, as I have had experience in coal
mining in Staffordshire and in Newcastle (N.S.W.),
besides opening up the coal measures of Moreton Bay
districts, I ought to have some knowledge of the subject.
Although the delta of the Burdekin River in the neigh-
borhood of Ayr and Brandon (some 35 to 50 miles distant
from Stewart’s Creek) may carry extensive coal measures,
I would stake the heaviest odds against even a 6in. seam
of solid clean coal being discovered within 10 miles of
the area selected for operations at Townsville.

© * » *
Another white labor v. piebald mine labor com-
parison. The consulting engineers whom Chamberlain
called in to assist the S’African Labor Commission re-
ported that the average cost with piebald labor on the
Rand was 31s. 2d. per ton of ore. The New Moon (Eagle-
hawk, Vic.) last half-year crushed and treated 13,615
tons of stone at a complete cost, including depreciation, of
£12,035, or an average of 17s. Sd. per ton. And the aver-
age for the Moon co.’s whole career is even lower than
that. Total gold value, £376,497 ; capital paid up, less
present cash balance, £9OOO ; dividends and machinery,
£205,225; working expenses (including office expenses),
£180,272 ; stone treated, 223,964 tons ; average per ton,
16s. Id. Sixteen shillings is, by the wav, the figure at
which the Bewick, Moreing firm is said to aim in Wes-
tralia, not by reducing wages, but by using the white
man’s brain, which always comes to the aid of the white
man’s brawn.
* * * * . * .
Some sanguine person will presently be hustling
G.-G. Northcote out of Melb. Government House Domain
under a miner’s right. T’other day’ some laborers,
dressing down a piece of land for a reserve near Haw-
thorn bridge, turned up two or three pieces of quartz
showing gold ; whereupon someone rushed off to the
mining registrar and applied for a lease. A decade ago
some enthusiastic prospectors sank a shaft on a small rise
a quarter-mile away, in St. James’ Park, but after a few
weeks’ work concluded that three specks to the load was
hardly good enough. As a matter of fact, all the land
east and N.E. of Melbourne is auriferous, from Dandenong
up to Whittlesea, but, until one gets to the hilly country
20 miles out, it doesn’t crush more than a bushel to the
acre, so to speak.
* * « *
“Wombat”: What has become of the Ringa-
rooma (Alberton, Tas.)? Remarks re Cobar (Bulletin
28/l/’O4) fit the position in connection with the shep-
herding at Alberton (Tas.). For the past two years not
more than four men have been employed at any time on
the leases held, which amount to over 50 acres, without
mentioning about 320 acres of gold-bearing freehold, the
whole property being supposed to be in the hands of
tributors. Rome of the leases lately forfeited for
non-payment of rent had not had a pick in them for
years. Part of the field has long been dummied.
Shareholders and others interested are quietly awaiting
developments, as to which there are curious rumors.
* * * *
Waihi (M.L.) return for 1903 (£649,000) exceeds
that of 1902 by £127,286. The gross output to 30th Dec.
last amounts to £3,152,305, of which shareholders have
had £1,252,896, including £250,000 paid in 1903.
* * * *
The Westralian Government has decided to put
up a smelting furnace—the first experiment of the sort—
at Phillips River. S. Australia tried the experiment at
Port Augusta., and dropped thousands in a month or two.
But Westralia has a better chance of success, having
about £25,000 worth of ore stacked, on which it has
advanced £15,000 ; and it is alleged that in getting out
this ore much more of good quality has been developed.
The idea when ore-buying was started, in lieu of the smelter
that was then asked for, was to see how developments
went; if they were not satisfactory, a steamer would
have been chartered, and the stacks shifted round to
N.S.W. for treatment. It is to be hoped that the Govern-
ment will do better as a smelter than most private co.'s
have done in Australia. They have been pretty bad rob-
bers mostly, and yet they have nearly all come Lo disaster
in the end. Possibly a highly moral smelter might make
a pile.
At Poverty Point.
An Australian boxing kangaroo has been getting his
name up in Paris. Passing through the Society of iine
Arts premises in the Champ de Mars he saw a huge
mirror with a reflected kangaroo in it. He put them up,
and so did the other one, and while the crowd was enjoy-
ing the joke Australia got in the first blow. Damages
4s. 6d.

Armstrong, a daughter of “Jumbo” Armstrong, Crown
Prosecutor and once Acting-D.C. Judge of N.S. Wales.
The lady is a niece of the late Justice Innes, who was a
great patron of the drama.

“ Only Me ” : He par (B. 4/2/’O4) on side-show at Manly,
where a man in a cask used to allow sticks to bethrown at
him at four for 3d. Have seen in Yankeeland,durmg fair
time in various towns, a large canvas screen rigged up,
with a hole in it, through which a nigger sticks his head.
The words, “ White man, can you hit me?” are painted
in large letters on the screen, and heavy leather balls are
let out to the “ white men ”at three for a nickel. This
show always does a splendid trade, as the American
crowd loves to see a nigger damaged.
Mrs. Langtry still hangs on to the notoriety that
came to her 30 years ago when the “Jersey Lily "burst
into bloom. Latest news of the once lovely one credits
her with the intention to write a play. Who will be Mrs.
L.’s ghost in this literary undertaking ?
Fanny Robina, the Little Jack Sheppard of the early
Brough and Boucicault days, is mostly a singer of
sentimental ballads these times. A Dublin paper to
hand last mail speaks of Robina’s personal magnetism,
her tender singing of “ Daddy,” and “ her popular item,
'My Little Babv Boy,’ with‘a sweetly rendered chorus by
her little daughter Florrie.” The critic doesn’t explain
how little daughter Florrie is able to render a chorus
all on her little own. Even if she were a double-voiced
infant she couldn’t manage more than a duet.
That plaster cast in “The Eternal City," at Her
Majesty’s, was a delusion and a snare. It wasn’t even a
product of local industry. And it didn’t represent Julius
Knight at all. It was a copy of the head of the actor who
played the part in London, a job lot having been sent
along for the Australian season.
Wallace Brownlow was going strong in San Francisco
at latest dates. Got a triple encore for his first song at
the Tivoli Opera House, when he appeared as Apollo (with
his lyre) in something vaguely described as “a Xmas
piece.” Subsequently he took the name part in “a real
American opera, trimmed with hoopskirts and tuned with
the songs of the South,” which work of Yankee art is
titled “When T ohnny Comes Marching Home.” Brown-
low plays Job >iy, and the local critic talks thus: —
“ Wallace Brownlow, the new barytone (they spell it with
a y in ’Frisco), has magnificent opportunities which serve
to prove his rare qualifications as a singer and actor.
His song, ‘My Own United States,’ is an exquisite affair.
It is fit to be made a national air,”&c., &c. Strauss’s
opera, “ The Gypsy Baron,” is to be revived, with Brown-
low as the beauteous Baron, the tenor music being
lowered in pitch to suit his voice. And he thinks of
knocking them anon as Henri of Navarre. Talking of a
giddy Henri and “ Ma Mie Rosette,” Nellie Stewart and
George Musgrove passed through San Francisco in Janu-
ary, and testified that old friend Brownlow was “all
right ” in his new part.

IN “AS GOOD AS GOLD” CASES
THE MEANING OF THE TERM—
“ GOLD=FILLED.”
FEW people correctly understand the meaning of the term “ Gold-filled ” as
applied to our watch cases. Some seem to regard “Gold-filled” as a
form of electro-plating—from which it is entirely different. Gold-filled
Watch Cases are intended as a substitute for solid gold, not only in
appearance, but in wear. A Solid Gold Watch Case is valued for its beauty—
for the gold look about it. The beauty and the gold-look are entirely on the
surface. The gold hidden away in the thickness of the case adds nothing
to its beauty, but does add greatly to its cost. To make a gold-filled
watch case an ingot of metal is placed between two ingots of solid 14-carat
gold, and the three are then rolled flat, forming a solid sheet with a thick out-
side of solid gold and a centre of metal. This centre adds strength and lessens
cost, but its presence cannot be detected unless the gold is cut away. It will
be seen that gold-filled has nothing in common with gilding or plating ■ the
former implying a substantial layer of solid gold, the latter meaning gold
deposited on the base metal article after it is made Men who cannot afford to
buy a solid gold watch, and men who can afford to, but do not care to run the
risk of losing such a valuable article, are recommended to consider the advan-
tages attached to buying one of these Gold-filled Watches.
