“ B.” : There is a yacht called the Merrie
England, and it is the floating home of the
Governor of British Papua. Said exalted person
is just now on furlough, or some such animal, and
his place is being filled by the Crown Prosecutor
as acting-Governor, and Solomon in all his pomp
did not bulge out his chest like this same poten-
tate. When the China steamer Taiyuan called
at Thursday Island the Government yacht was in
possession of the wharf. It moved away under
(I suppose) the skipper’s orders to allow the
Taiyuan to come alongside. When the Chow
steamer was nearing the wharf, the mighty acting-
Governor appeared and (I suppose) gave instruc-
tions to put back to the wharf at once, and so the
two boats got beautifully mixed up. The second
mate of the Chow boat telegraphed to the skipper,
and informed him frantically they were getting
into “holts” with a darned old ferry-boat.
There was also a great commotion on the
vice-regal punt; but that was nothing com-
pared to the expression on the vice-regal phiz
when the passengers on the Chow boat urged
his crew to throw the acting-Governor of
British Papua overboard. His Acting-Ex. stood
on deck in evening dress and surveyed the scene
like the Lord surveying a newly-created comet.
Our captain stood on his deck and backed his ship
furiously. Our passengers cursed profusely. A
fireman, with a bit of grimy waste round his neck,
threw a lump of coal at things in general and
missed. And all this fuss arose because a person
ill the only boiled shirt about the island wouldn’t
get his cockleshell out of the way. Then the sun
went down on our wrath, and things somehow set-
tled themselves without bloodshed. But his Acting-
Ex., I believe, has complained of our conduct in
not instantly vanishing off the face of the earth
when we saw the refulgence of his boiled shirt on
deck, and if the thing happens again he will pro-
bably throw his official throne at us.
“ Burglar Bill ” : The Chow loses much
of his stoicism in gaol. One, unable to speak
English, occupied the cell next to me during
“separate treatment,” and he spent the day
drawing rice fields, chop-sticks, and fat ducks on
his slate, which slate he would show to the warder
at inspection. The warder refused to see the
meaning of the drawings, and the Chow would cry
bitterly and answer all inquiries from sympathetic
officials by pointing at the slate. The doctor took
pity on him at last and put him on oatnual, and
he thrived considerably.


* COMMON-SENSE DIET
HEALTH NOTES
Don’t bolt your food. If you haven’t
time to eat your dinner without hurrying
over it, you had better go without it till
you have. Better be hungry than ill.
Don’t over eat. Indeed never take
quite as much as you feel you could. If
you are uncomfortable after meals it is
a sure sign either of excess or digestive
weakness.
Machinery works best when it is
cleansed and re-oiled. It is just the same
with the human machinery, the stomach
requires periodical cleansing and the
gastric juices require stimulation.
The retention of undigested food or
fermented liquids generates poisonous
gases.
Kutnow’s Powder is the only perfect
safeguard against the perils of Dyspepsia,
Indigestion, Liver-trouble and Constipa-
tion.
DR. HUGH LANE, fI.R.C.S.,
L.R.C.P., says :
“The more I try Kutnow’s Powder in my
practice the more I like it.”
It demonstrates the value of flushing, cleansing
and throwing out the impurities from the system,
and concurrently acting as a natural lubricant to
the digestive functions and the whole alimentary
tract.
PROF. LAWSON TAIT, M.D.,
also realised the necessity of cleansing the system
frequently to remove the debris left by undigested
food. He not only prescribed Kutnow’s Powder
in his practice, but used it himself for years.
The “ Daily Express,” London, Dec. 31st, 1903, says:
“If, among the many new resolutions which will be taken in the course of the next
clay or two, the determination to introduce greater common-sense into the ordinary meals
were to tind a place it would be well with the households where it was adhered to. Now it
is winter, and we wear thick clothes and overcoats; in the dog-days we shall put on light
vesture and discard all wraps. But hot or cold, winter or summer, we eat the same food ;
we stoke, our bodies with the same amount of fuel. Then we are surprised by the pains and
miseries of Indigestion. This is absurd. Still more absurd is the fact that in cities, by
men of sedentary occupations, practically the same dietary is followed as was customary
when our forefathers lived in the country and were actively engaged under the open sky.”
Health and happiness are possessions we all desire to obtain ; too often failure arises from
the simple neglect of suitable food and the correction of excesses in diet. Every other
person you meet nowadays seems to suffer from Indigestion in some form or another.
Every man, and every woman, can, however, so regulate the digestive functions as to defy
this modern terror of our civilisation. Every stomach requires cleansing occasionally,
especially after rich living. Every liver requires periodical stimulation, whilst it is obvious
that to flush the bowels must perforce be beneficial. Kutnow’s Powder is the only perfect
exponent of this cleansing treatment.

Indigestion: Dyspepsia.
The torments endured by a person who suffers from Indgestion are, unfortunately, so well known to
m ist of us that it is not necessary that the symptoms of that modern scoui ge should be described here.
Vliat sufferers want to know is how to become Permanently cured, and they can be enlightened
by reading this article.
There are several kinds of indigestion : Gastric Catarrh, in which the food becomes covered
with mucous, and consequently withstands the action of the digestive juices ; Gastric flatulency
the development of wind or gas; Gastric Diarrhoea, caused by the food being hurried through the
digestive organs too fast; and Constipation, which is a very prevalent and obstinate cause, which
must be removed, or digestion cannot be expected to proceed.
Food must be digested and converted into blood. Nature makes this one of her most imperative
laws of life. During the process of digestion food is entirely changed in composition by the action of
the juices of the internal organs through which it passes. In the mouth it is thoroughly mixed with
saliva. I hen it is swallowed, and enters the stomach, where it is acted upon by the gastric juices and
becomes partly liquefied. From the stomach it passes to the smaller intestine—wliich is about 18 feet
in length—and there certain portions of the food are liquefied by the bile and other juices. The food
thus made fluid is in a condition to be absorbed into and become a constituent part of the blood.
The indigestible portion of the food is discharged into the larger intestine, whence it is in turn
expelled from the body together with other refuse matter.
Just as certainly as that it is necessary to life that food must be absorbed, so, likewise it is
essential that the blood must be in a condition to absorb the food. Torpidity of the liver is the chief
cause of nearly every case of indigestion, and when the liver is torpid, the kidneys are always sympa-
thetically affected to a greater or lesser extent. The blood, which should be transformed, cleansed
and Altered by the kidneys and liver, then contains urinary and biliary poisons, and is there-
fore a feeble absorbent of nutriment. This condition of the blood reacts upon the nervous system of
the digestive organs, and prevents the flow, and alters the quality of the digestive juices.
The entire nerve energy of a person suffering from indigestion is weakened, owing to this con-
taminated condition of the blood, and the general feeling of mental and physical depression, which is
experienced during an attack of dyspepsia, is due to this cause.
cannot
The blood must be continuously purified by the action of the liver and kidneys, or good digestion
lot be expected to occur. K ®
Warner’s Safe Cure
cures indigestion and dyspepsia simply because it restores the liver and kidneys to health and activitv
and, as a consequence, the blood becomes pure, it absorbs nutriment freely, and conveys this nutri
ment to the nerves throughout the body Nature is merely aided in her efforts to preserve a balance
in the manifold and complex processes of waste and renewal by which life is maintained.

oug time ago ” —tlie old man paused
And passed his hand across his eyes.
I turned away. I knew what caused
His grief. Said he, “ Oh, dash the flies !
Long time ago, when me an’ Dan
Was young, an’ hadn’t got no sense.
That’s when we courted Mary Ann—
We uster sit on this ’ere fence.
“ An’ Mary’d come as soon’s she 'eard
Us yarnin’ ’ere upon th’ cliff.
But when she’d come, of course no word
Was spoke. She chewed ’er ’ankerchief.
“ An’ after we ’ad watched th’ sun
Sink down inter th’ evenin’ sea,
She’d laugh an’ say, ‘ Now jus’ f’r fun,
You come an’ chop some wood for me ! ’
“ An’ Mary uster lead th’ way,
An’ Mary’s dad, ’e always laughed
An’ waved ’is pipe. They uster say
’E never done no sort o’ graft.
“ An’ until night ’ad come, an’ we
Was deadly tired an’ fit t’ drop,
Th’ two of us—that’s Dan an’ me—
We’d take it, turn about, t’ chop.
“An’ Mary, she ’ud dodge inside,
An’ sing an’ bustle roun’ th’ place,
As gay an’ ’appy as a bride,
Th’ lamplight on ’er laughin’ face.

I’d smoke an’ dream upon th’ stump,

When I was young I was a chump !

VIC.
KODAK.
“A Question To Be Asked.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Two horses drawing a wood-dray across the
common, the ground ringing under their tread,
and the wheels rumbling as though on a sounding-
board. Mackenzie sat on the side-rail, his arms
folded, balancing himself to the jolts and bumps
like a sailor on a ship’s deck.
A week’s work was over. Mackenzie was going
home. His sun-blackened face, half hidden under
his hat, brightened from time to time with a
smile. At these moments he called to the horses
to hurry up. He was thinking, with the placid
content of a lucky newly-married man. For he
was lucky, there was no doubt of that; everyone
said so—the pretty school-mistress might have
had the pick of the district, and she had chosen
him. He smiled again as he thought of it, and
how she would be anxiously waiting for him to
come home.
T‘ ey drew out of the bush into the clear. He
stood up in the dray to get a view of the house.
“Shemight be waiting for me at the sliprails,”
he said to himself. His smile passed. No one
was there, nor, when ha drove up to the house
and under the lean-to, did Carrie come to the
doorway witli a smile and a word of welcome.
The man's face clouded ; he had hurried to tret
home, but being there, he unharnessed and fed
the horses with dilatory slowness, looking from
time to time towards the house. “No doubt
Carrie is busy getting tea,” he repeated to him-
self consolingly. He entered the front room.
Two women were seated there, a near neighboi
and his wife —the latter a dainty sweet-faced
little woman with quite a Christmas Annual
regularity of outline.
“Is that you, Harry ? How late you are 1 ” She
did nofc rise to meet him. “ I wish you’d get some
sticks and light the fire for tea. lam so tired to-
day.” Mackenzie nodded to the neighbor and
without a word went into the back room, where
the sound of crackling wood soon told that he was
busy.
The neighbor looked at Carrie interrogatively.
“You think I ought to have got the tea
ready?” said Carrie, answering the look. “In
the first place I’m tired, and in the next I think it
right a husband should be broken in right from
the commencement. It’s a woman’s own fault, I
always say, if she is trampled on. You can spoil a
husband as you can a child by letting him have
too much his own way. I found that out with the
school, and I know.” She spoke quite resolutely
with her crisp, Sydney accent, and laid down the
law like the queen she looked.
“We shall see, my dear, we shall see. Perhaps
you are right, but my old man would make the
devil of a row if he had to get his own meal, and
me about.”
When tea was over and the neighbor gone,
Carrie, brushing the company smile from her lace,
asked: “Why did you not come home earlier,
Harry. I wanted you to drive me to Terry’s.”
“What, leave my work? You were not)
obliged to go ! ”
“ But surely you don’t expect me to be shut up
in the house all day, and I’m sure you might have
come home for once.”
He did not seek to explain that loss of time was
loss of money. Perhaps he had done so, vainly,
before. He looked doubtingly at her, and then
his face brightened, as, putting his hand to his
pocket, he pulled out three notes and laid them
down triumphantly.
“Is that all ?” she snapped, tossing her pretty
curls. “How am I to pay all the bills with
that? ”
“It’s more than anyone else has made who
draws to the mill,” replied Mackenzie, sobered
now, his smile gone, “and I want eighteenpence
of that for tobacco.”
“What, two plugs a week, and I who have to
pinch and scrape ! Could you not do with one?”
“ Well—say one,” he said after a pause. “ But
where does all the money go ? ”
“How should you understand? There is the
housekeeping, and I must dress ; you would not
like to see me a fright.”
“ I thought you could make your own dresses ?'*
said Mackenzie, hesitating.
“‘What if I can ! We have not come to that
yet, and I don’t mean to.”
* * • *
Weeks passed. Mackenzie showed less and
less eagerness to hurry home with his cheque.
He got to lingering at the Cross-roads, playing
dominoes for drinks, the horses waiting in the
road patiently till, late in the evening, he half-
walked, half-staggered to the dray. One night
his mates had to carry him out and dump him
oyer the tail-board like a heavy sack, and though
his home was five miles off, no one doubted the
horses would take him safely there.
It was the next day that Mrs. Terry said to her
husband. “ That poor Mrs. Mackenzie has had
such a bad fall; she has blackened one of her
eyes against the doorpost.”
“Doorpost be blowed ! Harry’s put his brand
on her, and a good job too. She wanted it.”
Bad times came in the wood-trade, nothing but
economy and the hardest work made it pay.
Mackenzie lost his team, and fell from the dignity
‘ of a drawer to be a eutter, and a large part of his
diminished earnings passed over the bar. There
was now a baby at home, but it was beautifully
dressed, and the home itself a model of thrift and
care. Three days a week Carrie walked six miles
into town to do dressmaking, and often, after her
twelve-mile journey, she would start out again to
fetch Harry home from the public-house bar.
She came one night as usual to the Cross-roads,
Mackenzie’s voice sounding out loud above that
of the other revellers. She came into the crowd
quietly, squeezing to his side, coaxed and wheedled
him out into the road. He, big burly man of six-
feet-six, rolling and shouting ; she trying to lead
him, and when he stopped, after going a few steps,
pulling him slowly along.
“Now, come along, Harry, do, dear. Tea is
waiting for you.”
“ Oh, I’m all right; let a fellow alone ! ‘ For
he’s a jolly good fellow ’”
“Now do come,” and she put up her little
mouth with a caressing voice—“do come ! You
can sing as you go along. I’ve got fish for tea. ’
Half-leading, half-dragging, she got him into
the road, he all the time laughing down at her, as
at a merry child whose wilfulness he humored.
And she looked a child; her dainty smallness
beside his bulk.
Old Terry stood on the hotel verandah watch-
ing them.
“ I expect he’ll hammer her when she get’s him
home.”
“I shouldn’t wonder,” replied the landlord,
philosophically.
“It’s a strange thing,” said the old man as he
went in to finish his beer, “that bad husbands
mostly have good wives. How is it ? ”
“ That’s it! How is it ? ” repeated the publican
as he wiped a glass.
HENRY FLETCHER.
Trooper Ridley’s Zeal.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
PoLicE-Trooper Ridley waa always quoted by his
superiors as a rising and zealous young officer.
His zeal was, indeed, beyond question, and the
Law, as made and established, was to him a thing
to be approached with veneration. There was
but one entity for which he had a more profound
respect than the Law, and that was the personal
well-being of Trooper Ridley.
Outside of official life, Ridley had other pleas-
ing qualities, which made him popular in the
social life of Barwonah. He was a smart, well-
set-up young fellow, and when clothed in the
glory of shining boots, well-fitting breeches, and
jingling accoutrements, was the cause of some
heart-burnings among the feminine population of
the township. Although Ridley endeavored to
distribute his favors impartially (he held that a
man of law should, at times, be strictly impartial),
it was conceded that his attentions to pretty
Kitty Lawson, parlor-maid at the Royal, be-
tokened serious intentions, and Kitty was accord-
ingly the object of a certain amount of envy, and
a corresponding amount of malice among certain
of her fellow-feminines. So Kitty and Ridley
walked out and drove out, and enjoyed their brief
interval of bliss.
* * * *
Trooper Ridley gazed dejectedly at an official-
looking blue document that lay before him.
He thought that Fate had been particularly
hard on him to single him out for this duty. But
then, again, his official character for zeal was at
stake, and he thought of the stripe that was
almost within his grasp. Besides, if he didn’t
carry out the thing someone else would, and his
refusal would be of no use, and would effectually
block his advancement in the force.
He paused irresolutely, then put on his cap,
and walked down to the Royal Hotel.
“Is Catherine Lawson here?” he asked Mrs.
Flynn, the landlady, in his most official voice.
“ Sure she is, poor gurl,” was the reply; “ more
shame to ye, Mr. Ridley, for not cornin’ to see her
before, in her thrubble.”
He waved away the comment, and proceeded
to the room indicated. Knocking at the door, a
voice bade him enter.
The girl rushed to meet him with outstretched
arms and tear-reddened eyes.
“Jim, Jim dear,” she cried, “I knew you’d
come. Oh! Jim, I was mad ! What will they do
to me ? ”
“Is your name Catherine Lawson?” queried
Trooper Ridley, officially.
“ Why, Jim dear, you know it is. What do you
mean?” asked the girl, wonderingly.
Touching her on the shoulder the trooper said,
in his largest business voice, “ Catherine Lawson,
I arrest you on a charge of murdering your male
child on or about the 10th instant. I warn you
that anything you may say will be produced
against you as evidence at your trial. Come ! ”

The Sea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Greeting, O, Ocean wide ! O, vast, green Sea !
A few straight words and true I’d say to thee.
A question or two, also, would I ask,
O thou, who soundest hollow, like a cask,
Or like a soul that mourns indefinitely in a keg,
And prompt and truthful answer I fain of thee
would beg.
Tell me, O grasping Sea, why dost thou hold,
In covetous embrace, the stolen gold
From sunken ships, and galleys and triremes —
Wealth far beyond the wildest of man’s dreams?
Thou buy’st with it no drink at any harbor bar !
No mermaid handetli down to thee from rocky
shelf the bottle of Three-star !
And Sea, I pray thee answer furthermore,
Why dost thou leave thy dead upon the shore,
Their clothes destroyed, their hats all sodden
wrecks,
Unsaleable seaweed wisped round their necks?
The gold thou takest is for ever lost to us ;
The bones we have to bury at the public cost,
which makes us cuss.
These sad remains had money when they died,
And watches, trinkets, other things beside.
Thy long green hands their pockets did explore
Ere leaving them, insolvent, on the shore,
With hairy prawns amid their trailing garments
hid,
And in their hair the remnants of some dead,
dashed, damp, unprofitable squid.
Thy caves are full of safes and lined with gems,
And eels run races through lost diadems ;
And o’er them thou-dost gibber shapelessly,
Or gnash thy rocky teeth, or howl in glee,
Or rush around and scream insanely in thy ancient
lair
With planks and masts and sodden useless fire-
wood in thy hair.
There are rich liquors in thy great salt halls,
And drowned art treasures tumble round thy
walls;
Yet when man builds a city by thy side—-
A marble city with white towers of pride—
No golden tribute comes in sign of friendship
from thy boundless store,
But silently by night dost thou leave some dead
wet animal beside his door.
N.S.W.
EDGAR H. HARRIS.
Bridget Gwendoline.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“ Kim’ere ! Way-a ! Wo-back!” shouted Jim
Whelan to his team of miserable bullocks, hitting
the near-leader across the face and rubbing it in
with imprecations on the brute’s eyes.
The long drought had opened a market for any
sort of fodder, and Whelan was on his way to
Bunya with a big load of pumpkins when he met
the boyish rector of Bunya breathing his horse in
a long, deep sand-drift.
The young parson had but recently arrived from
England, and had been tactless enough to freely
express his contempt of bush manners and people.
The bushmen promptly retaliated by an exag-
gerated display of roughness and blasphemy when
the parson was about.
“Goo’ day !” said Whelan, uncouthly, giving
his head a side jerk. “Pum’kinsis riz, I hear.
How ’re they sellin’ up your way ?”
“Really, I am not interested in the pumpkin
market; but could you not drive your oxeii with-
out swearing at them ? ”
“I dunno,” replied Whelan with a grin.
“Sure they wouldn't bulge an inch else. Talk
about me swearin’! Y’ ought jus’ t’ hear Jack
Bowlin’ perform when ’e s stuck in th’ Glue Pot
back there be the Fourteen Mile ! Now that’s
swearin’ if v’ like ! Why, be jakes, he d lif tli at
off y’r head.”
•• i tear the life you bush people live is very de-
grading,” began the parson, but Whelan broke in
conclusively :
“ The bush’s right ’nough f’r them what s use
t’ it, an’un’erstan’s it, an’ makes the best of it. I
reckon we’s as good as them what’s alw’ys throwin’-
off at us.”
He turned away from the man who did not
understand him to the cittle which did. I hen he
half turned round again to the parson and said :
“There’s a joey t’ be branded up at my place.”
“ I beg your pai.'on ; I don’t understand you,
I’m afraid.”
“ Why, a joey—a kid—to be branded. '
As the parson showed plainly he did not yet
understand, in spite of the lucid explanation,
Whelan proceeded to make his meaning clearer
by signs, as if he were knocking it into a black-
fellow. He laid his whip across his left arm, and
made signs over it with his right hand.
“ Christenin’; you know ! ”
It dawned on the parson that Whelan wished
to have his child baptised. He was about to
lecture the heathen when the mail-coach came
into view before a cloud of dust, and ploughed
through the deep sand.
“Now then, Whelan, shif’ y’r sanguinary cows !
What the hell d’ yo’ mean blockin’ up the road
for, where nobody kin pass ? ” , .
The coach pulled-up while Whelan shifted his
cows, and the parson began to reprove the coach-
driver for his language. The coach was behind
time, the horses were so.poor that Rory had been
belting them along the last few miles, and he was
in no good temper.
“He understand,” he said, sulkily, “an it was
intended on’v f’r him. Them it wasn’t meant f’r
needn’t listen. If yo’ alw’ys cock y’r ears y’ll
hear lots y’oughtn’t to. Git-up!”
The parson drove on in great indignation.
“These dreadful people,” he said to himself,
“appear to have no respect for anyone or any-
-1,1 Whelan and Rory on the far end of the sand-
drift were exchanging opinions as they filled their
pipes. His Majesty’s Mail said :
“ Why, blow it! a bloomin’ bishop wouldn t
make as much dust. I ’ad a bishop an’ a arsh-
dicken or somethin’ on the box th’ other day, an
blest if I could make out their lingo, Lat’n ’r
Greek ’r somethin’, givin’ it t’ each other like me
an’ you might; but I didn’t inte fere. Who s that
cove, anyhow, teachin’ us to talk? Why, we
talked ’fore he was born. He’s balmy, I reckon.”
‘‘Dashed if ’e noo what brandin’ a joey was,”
said Whelan.
Before the parson returned from the far end of
his parish he sent a note to Whelan, fixing a time
for the baptism. Whelan’s house, built of adzed
slabs and roofed with bark kept in place with
greenhide and logs, stood on the river bank about
a mile from the main road. Though Whelan had
been on the land several years he had not bothered
to clear a track to the house. Like a true bushman
he never moved timber he could screw his dray
round or bump over. The parson led his horse
through the timber, jolting cushion and luggage
from his sulky, and reached the house in no very
amiable mood. Here he had to defend himself
with his whip from sundry mongrels. Every-
thing in this awful bush conspired to deepen his
impressions of its utter barbarism. Mrs. Whelan
was in the barn chopping up pumpkins when the
barking of the dogs brought her out. She stood
outside the barn door with an axe in her right
hand and a pumpkin in her left. Her rolled-up
sleeves exposed her sun-tanned arms ; her skirt,
gathered up from each side and fastened behind,
showed, beneath a short, faded, red flannel petti-
coat, that her stockingless extremities were thrust
into a pair of her husband’s boots; on her head was
a torn and greasy felt hat. She shook the axe at
the parson in welcome aiid called off the dogs.
“All right, Mister ! I’ll be there’n a minit,”
was her greeting.
Then she sent forth a voice of piercing shrillness
into the quiet bush.
“Car-ra-li-na ! Coo-00-e ! Car-ra-li-na ! ”
The echo came back across the river, and a flock
of white cockatoos rose up noisily from the culti-
vation paddock. From the bush, a short distance
off, came an irritable and, to the parson, unex-
pected response.
“O, shutup ! I ain’t deaf ! Whatchawant ? ”
“My word ! I’ll warm y’r hide f’r you, me
lady. Fetch along that there kid ’ere at wonst!”
“Naw, I won’t! Kim ’n’ fetch ’er yisself ! ”
Mrs. Whelan responded with alacrity. She
dropped axe and pumpkin and swooped into the
bush. Her exact location was presently indicated
by screams of rage and pain mixed with maledic-
tions from the undutiful Caroline. The mother
returned with the baby under her left arm, and
leading the squealing elder offspring by the ear
with her right hand. At the door of the barn
Caroline’s face was again slapped hard, and she was
cast inside to keep the pumpkins company, and
the door was shut and fastened on her.
“Walk inside, mister, an’ take a seat,” said
Mrs. Whelan, preceding the parson to the house.
“ That there young minx won’t do nothin’ I tell
’er ; but wait ’ll y’r gone, an’ by gosh ! I’ll skin ’er
alive —see if I don’t.”
The parson took a seat on an empty gin-case.
There was not a chair in the house, and hardly
any furniture; a rough, home-made bush table
stood in the middle of the uneven earthen floor;
tlie wall paper of illustrated weeklies was torn to
ribbons, partly by the “kids,” and partly by
Whelan when he wanted pipe lights ; a silent,
dirty-faced clock, with the minute-hand gone,
stood on the mantel-piece. The whole place
bespoke neglect and decay. “God help the
children reared in such a den ! ” thought the
parson, taking in all the wretched details. He
was startled by Mrs. Whelan’s first question.
“Y’r a noo chum, aintcha?”
“ I have not been long out here, if that is what
‘ new chum ’ means,’ he replied, stiffly.
Her troubles about his stiffness. She proceeded
easily.
“I thought’s much fr’m y’r looks. Yo’ kin
alw’ys tell a noo chum be lookin’ at him. But
never min’, mister, y’ll soon git broke-in. My
word ! the bush’s the place f’r knockin’ the stuffin’
out o’ noo chums. ”
She smiled down on him in motherly pity. The
smile was not returned, but he flushed and
frowned.
“I called to baptise your child. Is your
husband about? He should be present.”
“The boss "s gone t’ Bunya with another load.
’E’s sorry ’e couldn’t wait for it, but ’e don’ want
t’ miss a ctianst o’ maltin’ a bit.”
“ He had no right to go when he knew I was
coming. Where are the sponsors ? ”
“ What’s them ?” she asked, bewildered.
“Dear me ! ” muttered the parson at her depth
of ignorance. Then, aloud, “We shall require a
little water, please.”
She brought in a large tin dish, used for tne
household washing-up, and set it on the table.
“ Have you such a thing as a prayer-book? I
dropped mine out of the sulky.”
She paused a moment to think before she
answered : “ Blest if I know where ’tis ; b’lieve
it’s in the box. Jus’ ’ol’ the kid, mister, while I
look ! ”
Before the parson could decline, as he meant
emphatically to do, for it was an exceedingly
dirty “ kid,” he found the child across his knees.
He looked at the grubby thing with such an
expression of disgust and repulsion that she broke
into howls. A consoling voice came from the
bedroom.
“ All right, ducky ; mum’ll be there’n a minit.
Don’ let ’er fall, mister.”
“Do please cotne and take her,” the parson
pleaded, in mortal fear of consequences. “Never
mind the book ! Gome quickly ! I shall let her
fall! ”
Mrs. Whelan returned with two dilapidated
books, which she laid before the parson, and took
the child from him, remarking with a laugh :
“ Y’ ain’t used t’ kids, I kin see. ”
“God forbid !” he exclaimed thoughtlessly but
fervently.
He opened each of the books delicately with the
end of a finger, shut it, and looked up at the
woman now suckling her child without needless
modesty.
“ Ain’t naythur o’ those them ? ” she asked in a
disappointed tone.
“No !” he answered with a sneer. “One is a
cookery book, the other a dictionary. Never
mind, I’ll manage without a book.”
He uttered a few prayers from memory and put
the prescribed formula to her :
“Name this child!” without taking the baby
in his arms. Her answer staggered him.
“Blest if I ain’t clean forgot about the name.
She’s registered Bridget, but me an’ the boss
thinks as Bridget’s too common, an’ we wants it
changed. We was so busy loadin’ the dray, an’
the boss went off in sich a tear, we clean forgot.
P’r’aps you kin think o’ one that’ll suit ’er —a nice
one.”
“Gwendoline!” suggested the parson, ironi-
cally.
“My word ! ” She assented at once, delighted.
“ That’ll do fine ; fus’-rate.”
So the awful brand was put on Whelan’s joey:
“Bridget Gwendoline !”
When the boss reached home and was told the
new name, he exclaimed : “ Crikey ! ”
Then his heart opened.
“Blow me! missis, if I don’ leave’im a bag o
pum’kins.”

“Hamlet the Mummer.” r ‘4ough most people are

Before they retired, however, these jolly boys had
banded themselves into the Baliymaniacs. Their consti-
tution, by-laws, and everything were this :—When one
Ballymaniac saw another, no matter where he happened
to be, he was to give the Ballymaniac whistle (one
specially composed b.v a Ballymaniac, who was first violin
i*ta uiehire orchestra), and unless the other Ballymaniac
whistled in answer to the call, put his hand above his
head and did three or four steps of the Highland fling, he
had to pay Is. as a Ballymaniac contribution to the
Distressed Actors’ Fund. Sometimes this whistle would
be heard at very inconvenient times. Perhaps a fellow
would be taking tea at a 6 o'clock crowded restaurant
with a lady, and would suddenly hear the weird call.
Aba rule he would sooner dub up Is. than make a fool of
himself. I remember walking along the Melbourne
Block, one afternoon, and seeing a Ballymaniac. I
whistled. He looked round with a tired sort of look, and
said, “I’ve had enough of this d d Ballymaniac busi-
ness ! You’re the third one to-day who has taken me in.
The three of you hav<; whistled when you knew I couldn’t
do the blanky dance. Thfee hob wasted ! I’ll either
have to chuck this confounded business or keep out of
the main streets. Look ! come and have a drink, and say
nothing about me not doing the jig.” I was satisfied, so
was he—the drinks ouiy cost him 3d apiece. Another
evening I was with a Ballymaniac at one of Rickards’
shows. Another of the cult, who was also a comedian by
profession, was doing a turn that Right. In the middle
of his performance! my friend in the box gave the war-cry,
and I thought the comedian was going to break up. He
didn't. He stopped in his patter, and, taking out his
watch, remarked, ‘‘ I see it is time for me to take a little
exercise my doctor has ordered,” and with that he did
the jig. The Ballymaniac Society is now on its last legs.
Beryl Faber and beauteous Mrs. Maesmore Morris made
a v ery unwise venture in theatrical management last
month. They took the Avenue Theatre, London, for the
production of “ All Fletcher’s Fault,” and the piece ran a
fortnight, which is to say that it didn’t draw the public
at all. The hero of this ill-fated fiction is a tired young
oarl, who gets turned to deeds of human kindness by a
doctor’s prescription, as Horace Parker had been turned
m “ Message from Mars,” where the conversion from lazy
selfishness to furious philanthropy was only the end of
the story and not the whole of it, as in this case. The
author having seized a stale idea from one play, proceeds
to introduce it in imitation of a merely casual scene in
( The Great Millionaire ” melodrama. The tired hero of
. AH Fletcher’s Fault ” starts his new life by order-
ln£ his Servants to invite a number of poor
wayfarers into his ancestral mansion. He gets a
motley gathering of guests—elaborated counter-
parts of the seedy strangers who came and “scoffed”
Albert Norman’s choice refreshments in “The Great
Millionaire ’’—and one of them is a flower-girl whose
lame sister he afterwards rescues from a burning house,
like Parker in “ Message from Mars.” By this heroic
conduct he gets the adoration of a high-born female,
corresponding to Minnie in “ The Message.” She had
despised him until he took to rescuing helpless children
from the flames but now she consents to marry him
without pausing to think what a troublesome philanthro-
pist he will be. Five years ago “ All Fletcher’s Fault ”
would have scored a success on account of the same
scenes and sentiments that doomed it to failure last
“ L.W.” : There seems to be at lot of variety talent going
to waste in this country for sheer lack of opportunity.
At a church shivoo in Drummoyne, lately, a girl oame out
and sang a comic song with a kookaburra on her shoulder-
The song was encored, and in response the girl gave a
recitation of some humor. The house shrieked, but the
bird sat still—the picture of gravity. The lady touched
him on the end of the bill. “That was a joke, dear!”
she said. The bird threw up his head and went off into
a wild fit of laughter. Every time he realised it was a
joke he rumbled away into hilarity. As her supply of
jokes ran out the bird's oacchinations began to. slacken off
till he was once more the monument of grave sobriety,
’heedless of the girl’s pleading that “ that was a joke,
dear.” He only shook his bill by way of saying that he
didn’t see it. So she ignored him and confined her atten-
tion to the audience, and made some more than usually
humorous remarks. The bird exploded, he toppled over,
hung by one claw, and then flopped helplessly, still
laughing, to the floor, and, as the girl bore him away, hjs
appreciation of her last item could be heard growing faint
in the distance.
J. F. Hogan, after ransacking the British Museum,
writes out to the Melbourne Advocate a really tangible
biography of Lola Montes, and does justice to her fine
qualities. She was born in Limerick, of an Irish father
and Spanish mother, and died in New York, aged 43,
after some years of faithful missionary work in connec-
tion with the Magdalen Asylum. In young womanhood,
she lived with her mother at Bath. At the age of 17 she
eloped with and married Captain James because her
’mother wanted her to wed a mouldy Baronet of 50. The
marriage was unhappy. She became a dancer, bewitch-
ing London and the Continent. Her rule of Bavaria,
which lasted just one year, w r as statesmanlike and bene-
ficial in every way. Her intellectual powers were evi-
dently superior to those of a Bernhardt or a Duse, but
she had no histrionic ability. Hogan gives extracts from
her admirable lectures, especially one upon the “ Women
of History.”
A negro, or coon-colored giantess, well-known in
America, has been booked for an Australian tour. She
stands 7ft. 4in., and is probably the tallest on the show-
market just at present, if not the tallest in the world.
One hears very little about giants of late. The Fat
Sisters from Gippsland (Vic.) have visited England and
spread the fame of Australia as a meat-producing
country, but a real Commonwealth Giant, cJr Giantess,
whose length will lick creation is a novelty yet to be dis-
covered. What’s the height of Australia’s tallest son,
and where does he hang out, anyhow? This journal
knows of a speculator who would like to open negotia-
tions with an Bft. native.
“Hamlet”: Manager Pollock has got a couple of
shows for Adelaide at last —Tom Perman’s Panto., and
possibly wanderer George Darrell. The local Theatre
Royal is at times a real white elephant—particularly at this
season. Only a few second and third-rate companies are
obtainable so soon after Christmas, and usually they are
so poor that a lessee is obliged to pay their travelling ex-
penses to get them. A lessee of a theatre like the Ade-
laide Royal, besides being a business man, should be an
actor, so that'when there is a dearth of touring com-
panies he could scratch together a company, and play
“East Lynne” while the theatrical drought lasted.
Charley Holloway is the kind of man I mean.
Barnum and Bailey’s old circus business is now owned
by a joint-stock co., mostly English; besides the
“Greatest Show on Earth,” it runs the Buffalo Bill
crowd. Last year it paid a 10 per cent, div., and set
aside £35,000 against expenses in preparation for next
season.
“ J.A.C." : “ Chili ” Gonzales, recently deceased, a one-
time well-known circus performer, and father of the clever
Gonzales Sisters, used to relate that once while he was
with a circus in Brazil two performers from the United
States joined the show. Their act, known as “La
Perche,” consisted in one of them balancing a long pole,
while the <other climbed up it and went through certain
tricks. The “bearer” introduced his partner to the
audience in what he doubtless supposed to be Portuguese.
“Lo hombre arrivato qui,” he said, “clamfceroa rotondo,
rivoluto alia serpente, er—,” and here his vocabulary
failed him, “er—and then slides down again like a Gor-
dam lizard 1 ”
